The Worldly Benefit of Creed
Benefit of creed for worldly actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creed is necessary for the correctness and success of all actions, both religious and worldly.

Core Idea
Creed is the foundation of all successful action.

This lesson explores the first part of Shaykh al-Hashimi's answer to a question about the value of studying creed. He explains that creed is not only a religious requirement but a practical necessity for any meaningful endeavor, providing the motivation and unity of purpose needed for success.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and initial answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the answer begins by stating the benefit in three dimensions: legally, customarily, and rationally.
2
Distinguish creed from tawhid
The answer distinguishes between creed (a condition for starting an action) and tawhid (a condition for its success). Understand this key distinction.
Tawhid here means unity of purpose and leadership, not just theological monotheism.
3
Reflect on the examples
The author gives concrete examples: ships, cars, airplanes, etc. Consider why unity of goal and leadership is essential for any collective endeavor.
These examples show the universal applicability of the principle.
4
Connect to your own experience
Think of a personal or observed situation where lack of unity led to failure. How does this align with the author's claim?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بين يدي الكتاب
بين يدي الكتاب
الحمد لله واهب المنن ،والعطيات والصلوات الطيبات المباركات على سيدنا ومولانا محمد سيد الكائنات، وعلى آله أولو المجد والمكرمات، وسلم تسليماً كثيراً إلى يوم الدين، آمين.
أما بعد:
سئل سيدي العلامة العارف بالله محمد الهاشمي رحمه الله ورضي عنه عن الثمرة التي يجنيها قارئ علم العقيدة الإسلامية وما هو الوبال الذي يلحقه من عدم قراءته؟
فأجاب رضي الله عنه : قال العلماء بل وجميع العقلاء : إن فائدته في الدنيا صحة الأعمال ونجاحها؛ شرعاً، وعادة، وعقلاً، فالعقيدة سواء كانت صحيحة أو باطلة هي شرط في ابتداء العمل، والتوحيد شرط في نجاحه، فالعقيدة باعث قوي على العمل خيراً كان أو شراً، والتوحيد شرط في نجاح ذلك العمل، إذ لا يمكن الابتداء في عمل بهمة وقوة وجد إلا بعقيدة وتوحيد، فلا يمكن سلامة باخرة أو سيارة أو طائرة أو مملكة أو مدرسة أو معمل أو بلدة أو بيت بلا توحيد الهدف والقيادة، ولا يمكن نجاح عمل تعددت رؤساؤه وأهدافهم.
وأما الوبال الذي يلحق عديم العقيدة في شيء يجلب به منفعة أو يدفع به مضرة؛ فهو دوام الحيرة والحسرة والخيبة والتردد والوساوس، ولا يقدر أن يشرع في عمل بهمة وعزيمة وإن شرع لا يقدر أن يداوم عليه.
Between the hands of the book
Between the hands of the book
Praise be to Allah, the Bestower of favors and gifts, and the pure blessed prayers be upon our master and lord Muhammad, the master of all creatures, and upon his family, possessors of glory and honor, and abundant peace until the Day of Judgment, Amen.
To proceed:
My master, the scholar, the knower of Allah, Muhammad al-Hashimi (may Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him) was asked about the fruit that the reader of the science of Islamic creed reaps and what is the calamity that befalls him from not reading it?
He answered, may Allah be pleased with him: The scholars, indeed all rational people, said: Its benefit in this world is the correctness of actions and their success; legally, customarily, and rationally. For creed, whether correct or false, is a condition for the initiation of action, and monotheism is a condition for its success. Creed is a strong motivator for action, whether good or evil, and monotheism is a condition for the success of that action, since it is impossible to begin an action with determination, strength, and earnestness except with creed and monotheism. Thus, the safety of a ship, car, airplane, kingdom, school, factory, town, or house is not possible without unity of goal and leadership, nor can an action succeed whose leaders and goals are multiple.
As for the calamity that befalls the one lacking creed in something by which he seeks to obtain benefit or repel harm: it is perpetual confusion, regret, disappointment, hesitation, and whispers; he is unable to commence an action with determination and resolve, and if he commences, he is unable to persevere in it.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer exchange. The questioner asks Shaykh Muhammad al-Hashimi about the benefit (thamara) of studying Islamic creed and the harm (wabāl) of neglecting it. The answer proceeds in two parts.

First, the benefit: The shaykh states that according to scholars and all rational people, the worldly benefit of creed is the correctness (ṣiḥḥa) and success (najāḥ) of actions—legally (sharʿan), customarily (ʿādatan), and rationally (ʿaqlan). He then explains that creed, whether true or false, is a condition (sharṭ) for beginning any action. Monotheism (tawḥīd) is a condition for the success of that action. Creed is a strong motivator, but monotheism ensures unified direction. He illustrates this with concrete examples: ships, cars, airplanes, kingdoms, schools, factories, towns, and houses all require unity of goal and leadership to function properly. Multiplicity of leaders and goals leads to failure.

Second, the harm: The one lacking creed suffers perpetual confusion (ḥayra), regret (ḥasra), disappointment (khaība), hesitation (taraddud), and whispers (wasāwis). Such a person cannot begin any action with determination and resolve, and if he does begin, he cannot persevere. Thus, creed is presented as essential not only for religious acts but for all meaningful human endeavor.

Key Terms
عقيدة
توحيد
عمل
ثمرة
شرط
شرعاً
عادةً
عقلاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creed only matters for religious worship and has no impact on worldly activities.
Correction: The passage shows that creed is necessary for the correctness and success of all actions, whether religious or worldly.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the condition for the success (najāḥ) of an action?
A) Having a correct creed only
B) Monotheism (tawḥīd) and unity of goal
C) Strong motivation regardless of creed
D) Legal validity alone

Key Takeaway
Creed initiates action, but tawhid (unity of purpose) ensures its success; both are indispensable for any meaningful endeavor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you been trying to succeed without unifying your goals under the tawḥīd of Allah? What specific step will you take this week to bring that area under His single leadership?
?
The passage says creed is a condition to start any action. Is there a good deed you have been putting off because your creed is weak or your tawḥīd is not firmly applied? What belief do you need to strengthen to begin immediately?
?
Think of a collective endeavor you are part of (family, work, community). Where have you contributed to divided goals or multiple leaders? How will you repent and act today to promote unity of purpose according to this lesson?

The Harm of Neglecting Creed
Harm of lacking creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the psychological and practical consequences of lacking a unifying creed in one's life.

Core Idea
Without creed, life is perpetual confusion and failure.

This lesson addresses the second part of Shaykh al-Hashimi's answer, which describes the calamity of being without a correct creed. He lists the psychological harms that prevent a person from starting or persisting in any action with determination.

Guided Path
1
Read the harm section carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note that it begins with 'As for the calamity...' and describes both internal states and practical inability.
2
Identify the specific harms
The passage lists five harms: perpetual confusion, regret, disappointment, hesitation, and whispers. Understand each term.
These are psychological and spiritual ailments.
3
Understand the practical consequence
The harm is not just internal; it prevents a person from beginning actions with determination and, if started, from persevering.
This connects to the earlier point about creed being a motivator.
4
Contrast with the benefit
Compare this description to the benefit described in the previous lesson. How does lacking creed lead to the opposite of correctness and success?

Source Text

الحمد لله واهب المنن ،والعطيات والصلوات الطيبات المباركات على سيدنا ومولانا محمد سيد الكائنات، وعلى آله أولو المجد والمكرمات، وسلم تسليماً كثيراً إلى يوم الدين، آمين.

وأما الوبال الذي يلحق عديم العقيدة في شيء يجلب به منفعة أو يدفع به مضرة؛ فهو دوام الحيرة والحسرة والخيبة والتردد والوساوس، ولا يقدر أن يشرع في عمل بهمة وعزيمة وإن شرع لا يقدر أن يداوم عليه.

To proceed:
My master, the scholar, the knower of Allah, Muhammad al-Hashimi (may Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him) was asked about the fruit that the reader of the science of Islamic creed reaps and what is the calamity that befalls him from not reading it?
He answered, may Allah be pleased with him: The scholars, indeed all rational people, said: Its benefit in this world is the correctness of actions and their success; legally, customarily, and rationally. For creed, whether correct or false, is a condition for the initiation of action, and monotheism is a condition for its success. Creed is a strong motivator for action, whether good or evil, and monotheism is a condition for the success of that action, since it is impossible to begin an action with determination, strength, and earnestness except with creed and monotheism. Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer exchange. The questioner asks Shaykh Muhammad al-Hashimi about the benefit (thamara) of studying Islamic creed and the harm (wabāl) of neglecting it. The answer proceeds in two parts.

First, the benefit: The shaykh states that according to scholars and all rational people, the worldly benefit of creed is the correctness (ṣiḥḥa) and success (najāḥ) of actions—legally (sharʿan), customarily (ʿādatan), and rationally (ʿaqlan). He then explains that creed, whether true or false, is a condition (sharṭ) for beginning any action. Monotheism (tawḥīd) is a condition for the success of that action. Creed is a strong motivator, but monotheism ensures unified direction. He illustrates this with concrete examples: ships, cars, airplanes, kingdoms, schools, factories, towns, and houses all require unity of goal and leadership to function properly. Multiplicity of leaders and goals leads to failure.

Second, the harm: The one lacking creed suffers perpetual confusion (ḥayra), regret (ḥasra), disappointment (khaība), hesitation (taraddud), and whispers (wasāwis). Such a person cannot begin any action with determination and resolve, and if he does begin, he cannot persevere. Thus, creed is presented as essential not only for religious acts but for all meaningful human endeavor.

?

The Relative Pronoun in Praise: Grammar and Theology
Al-Sawi explains the opening phrase, analyzes 'al-ladhi', and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and theological justification for using the relative pronoun 'al-ladhi' to describe Allah in opening supplications.

Core Idea
The relative pronoun in praise is valid when interpreted as derived.

Al-Sawi begins by deferring the discussion of basmala and hamdala to later, then praises the opening phrase 'al-ladhi nawwara...' as a clever beginning. He then delves into the grammatical nature of 'al-ladhi', revealing a subtle debate among scholars. This lesson unpacks that debate and shows how a grammatical objection is resolved through interpretation (ta'wil).

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the opening phrase
Notice that al-Sawi first sets aside the explanation of basmala and hamdala for later, before focusing on the relative pronoun phrase. This shows his systematic approach.
2
Analyze the definition of barā‘at maṭla‘
Al-Sawi praises the phrase as a good opening. Understand that barā‘at maṭla‘ means the author hints at his main objective from the very beginning.
This is a rhetorical device, not purely grammatical.
3
Examine the grammatical nature of 'al-ladhi'
Al-Sawi presents the classification of the relative pronoun as partitive (juz’ī) or universal (kullī) according to different scholars. Compare the views of al-‘Aḍud and al-Sayyid with that of al-Sa‘d.
This is a technical grammatical debate.
4
Resolve the objection regarding derived vs. non-derived terms
The objection claims that since 'al-ladhi' is non-derived (jāmid), it cannot be a descriptive term (na‘t). The answer is that it is interpreted (mu’awwal) as a derived term, making it valid.
This shows how grammatical rules can be applied flexibly in theological contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بسم الله الرّحمن الرّحيم الحمد الله): سيأتي الكلام على البسملة والحمدلة موضحاً في كلام الشارح عند ذكر المتن لهما . قوله : (الذي نور قلوبنا ... إلخ) : فيه حُسْن افتتاح وبراعة مطلع، وهي أن يأتي المؤلّف أو الخطيب مثلاً في مبدأ كلامه بما يشعر بمقصوده، و(الَّذي): اسم موصول جزئي وضعاً واستعمالاً، كما قاله العضد والسَّيد، خلافاً لقول السَّعد : كلي وضعاً جزئي استعمالاً، يذكر ليتوصل به إلى وصف المعارف بالجمل، وحقُ الجملة الموصول بها (١) أن تكون معلومةً الانتساب عند المخاطب، وهو هنا صفة الله تعالى باعتبار صلته ؛ لوروده في القرآن (۲) ، كذلك جيء به للمدح مع زيادة إفادة الغرض المسوق إليه الكلام من استحقاقه تعالى الحمد وانفراده به وبيان نعمه الموجبة لحمده. لا يقال : النَّعت مشتق، والموصول جامد، فلا يصح النَّعت به؛ لأنَّا نقول : هو مؤوّل بالمشتقّ؛ أي : الحمد الله الموصوف بكونه نوراً إلخ . (۱) أي: الواقعة صلة. (۲) وذلك مثل قوله تعالى : الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ الَّذِي أَنزَلَ عَلَى عَبْدِهِ الْكِتَبَ ﴾ [الكهف: ١].
His statement: 'In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, praise be to God': The discussion on the basmala and hamdala will come clarified in the words of the commentator when mentioning the text for them. His statement: 'the One who illuminated our hearts... etc.': In it is a good opening and an excellent start (barā‘at maṭla‘), which is that the author or orator, for example, begins his speech with something that indicates his objective. And 'al-ladhi' is a relative pronoun that is partitive (juz’ī) in form (waḍ‘an) and usage (isti‘mālan), as stated by al-‘Aḍud and al-Sayyid, contrary to the statement of al-Sa‘d: universal (kullī) in form, partitive in usage. It is mentioned to enable describing known entities (ma‘ārif) by clauses. The proper thing concerning the clause connected by the relative pronoun (1) is that it be known in its attribution to the addressee. And here it is an attribute of God Almighty with respect to its connection, because it occurs in the Quran (2). Likewise, it is brought for praise with the increased conveyance of the purpose for which the speech is directed: His deserving praise, His uniqueness in it, and the mention of His favors that necessitate His praise. It should not be objected: 'The descriptive term (na‘t) is derived (mushtaqq), while the relative pronoun is non-derived (jāmid), so it is invalid to use it as a descriptive term.' Because we say: it is interpreted (mu’awwal) as a derived term, i.e., 'Praise be to God, the One described as being light... etc.' (1) Meaning: the clause is the connection (ṣila). (2) That is like His statement: 'Praise be to God who sent down to His servant the Book' (Quran 18:1).
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Sawi on the opening words of al-Dardir's Sharh al-Kharida. First, al-Sawi notes that the explanation of the basmala and hamdala will be provided later when the commentator addresses them. Then he focuses on the phrase 'al-ladhi nawwara qulūbanā...' (the One who illuminated our hearts). He praises it as a good opening (ḥusn iftitāḥ) and an excellent beginning (barā‘at maṭla‘), meaning the author hints at his purpose from the start. Next, he analyzes the relative pronoun 'al-ladhi': grammatically, it is a relative noun (ism mawṣūl). According to al-‘Aḍud and al-Sayyid al-Sharīf, it is partitive (juz’ī) in both its form (waḍ‘an) and usage (isti‘mālan). However, al-Sa‘d al-Taftāzānī holds that it is universal (kullī) in form but partitive in usage. The relative pronoun is used to enable the speaker to describe known objects (ma‘ārif) by clauses. Al-Sawi then states the rule that the clausal part (the relative clause) should be known in its relation to the addressee. Here, 'al-ladhi' is an attribute of Allah due to its connection (as in the Quranic occurrence). It is used for praise and to convey the purpose: that Allah alone deserves praise and His favors necessitate praise. An objection arises: a descriptive term (na‘t) must be derived (mushtaqq), but 'al-ladhi' is non-derived (jāmid), so it cannot be used as a descriptive term. The answer is that it is interpreted (mu’awwal) as a derived term, meaning 'the One described as being light...' etc. The footnotes clarify: (1) The 'clause connected' means the clause is the connection (ṣila). (2) A Quranic example: 'Praise be to God who sent down to His servant the Book' (18:1).

Key Terms
اسم موصول
جزئي
كلي
نعت
مشتق
جامد
تأويل
براعة مطلع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is invalid to use a relative pronoun like 'al-ladhi' as a descriptive term (na‘t) for Allah because relative pronouns are non-derived (jāmid) while na‘t must be derived (mushtaqq).
Correction: Al-Sawi explains that the relative pronoun is interpreted (mu’awwal) as a derived term, meaning it is understood as 'the One described as...' thereby satisfying the requirement of derivation.

Checkpoint
According to al-‘Aḍud and al-Sayyid, how is the relative pronoun 'al-ladhi' categorized?
A) Universal (kullī) in form and usage
B) Partitive (juz’ī) in form and universal in usage
C) Partitive (juz’ī) in both form and usage
D) Universal in form and partitive in usage

Key Takeaway
The relative pronoun 'al-ladhi' can function as a descriptive term (na‘t) for Allah when interpreted as derived, resolving the grammatical objection and enabling its use in praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah, do you ever pause to consider whether your words truly reflect His majesty and the precise meanings they carry? How might this lesson change the way you recite or write praise?
?
The objection that 'al-ladhi' cannot be a descriptive term because it is non-derived was resolved by interpreting it as derived. Are there any aspects of your worship that you have dismissed or found difficult because you didn't understand them deeply? Will you now seek to resolve such doubts?
?
This passage shows that even grammatical precision matters in praising Allah. Examine your own heart: do you approach His attributes with the same careful reverence, or have you become careless in your understanding? What one step can you take today to deepen your reverence?

Derived Terms Indicate Causation
Commentary on derived term and objection-answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the use of a derived term (al-mustaq) in a theological statement indicates causation, and how the objection that this restricts praise is answered.

Core Idea
Derived terms show causation, not limitation.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a subtle grammatical point about the phrase 'al-ḥamdu lillāh.' He explains that the use of a derived term indicates that the attribute from which it is derived is the cause of the judgment. This triggers an objection—doesn't this restrict praise to that attribute? The answer preserves the full scope of praise while explaining the role of the specific attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage begins mid-sentence, continuing from a previous discussion.
The word 'الشيخ' refers to the author of the original text, al-Kharida.
2
Identify the grammatical claim
The commentator states that attaching a judgment to a derived term indicates causation (ʿilliyya). Understand this grammatical principle.
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues that using a derived term would restrict praise to that specific attribute, whereas Allah deserves praise for His essence and all attributes.
4
See the answer
The commentator clarifies that the attribute 'enlightening' is not the cause of deserving praise, but the cause of the Shaykh's informing us that Allah deserves all praise.
This preserves the universal praise of Allah.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how grammatical analysis helps clarify theological statements. The derived term points to a reason for the statement, not a restriction of the attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتعليق الحكم بالمشتق يدلُّ على عليَّة ما منه الاشتقاق، فكأنه قال: الحمد لله لتنويره، فهو حمد في مقابلة نعمة فيثاب عليه ثواب الواجب الزائد على النفل بسبعين درجة .
فإن قيل : تعليق الحكم بمشتقّ يفيد قصر الحمد على خصوص ذلك المشتقّ، مع أنَّه يستحقُ الحمد لذاته وصفاته.
أجيب : بأنَّ التَّنوير ليس علَّةً لاستحقاقه المحامد، بل علَّةً لإخبار الشيخ بثبوت استحقاقه تعالى لجميع المحامد.
And the attachment of the judgment to the derived term indicates causation from which the derivation is taken, so it is as if He said: Praise be to Allah for His enlightening, so it is praise in return for a favor, for which He will be rewarded with the reward of the obligatory, which is seventy degrees above the supererogatory. If it is said: The attachment of the judgment to a derived term implies the restriction of praise to that particular derivative, whereas He deserves praise for His essence and attributes. The answer is: That enlightening is not a cause for deserving praises, but rather a cause for informing [by] the Shaykh of the affirmation of His, exalted, deserving of all praises.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological explanation of the phrase 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' found in the original text. The commentator begins by noting that the use of a derived term (al-mushtaq) – in this case, a term derived from 'enlightenment' – indicates that the attribute from which it is derived is the cause of the judgment. Thus, the phrase 'Praise be to Allah for His enlightening' is understood as praise for a specific favor, and such praise entails a higher reward. A potential objection is then raised: using a derived term restricts praise to that particular attribute, but Allah deserves praise for His essence and all attributes. The commentator answers that the attribute of enlightening is not the cause of Allah's deserving praise; rather, it is the cause of the Shaykh (the author of al-Kharida) informing us that Allah deserves all praise. This preserves the understanding that Allah is praised for His essence and attributes without limitation.

Key Terms
المشتق
العلية
قصر الحمد
التنوير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attribute 'enlightening' is the cause of deserving praise.
Correction: The commentator clarifies it is only the cause of informing about that deserving, not the cause of the deserving itself.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the derived term used in 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'?
A) To restrict praise to the attribute of enlightening
B) To indicate that enlightening is the cause of the praise
C) To show that praise is for the essence alone
D) To deny that Allah deserves praise for His attributes

Key Takeaway
A derived term in a judgment indicates the cause of the judgment, not a restriction of praise to that attribute alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever inadvertently limited your praise of Allah to a single attribute or favor, as if He only deserves thanks for that one thing? What would it take for you to cultivate a constant state of praise that acknowledges His infinite perfection, even in trials?
?
The lesson shows that a derived term can indicate a cause for praising, but not a limit on praise. Is your understanding of Allah's worthiness of praise limited to what you have experienced, or does it extend to His essence? What step can you take today to expand your praise beyond your immediate blessings?

Lexical Commentary on Nur, Qulub, Ma'rifa, and Harrara
Lexical and metaphorical analysis of opening terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical and metaphorical meanings of the key terms 'nur', 'qulub', 'ma'rifa', and 'harrara' as used in the opening line of the theological text.

Core Idea
Spiritual light illuminates the intellect, freeing it from ignorance and imitation.

Before diving into the doctrinal arguments, the commentator pauses to clarify the precise meanings of the words used in the opening invocation. Words like 'light', 'hearts', 'knowledge', and 'freed' carry rich metaphorical layers that shape the theological message. This lesson unpacks those layers so that later discussions rest on a clear lexical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the full Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the terms being explained.
The passage is dense, so do not worry about understanding every detail yet.
2
Understand 'nur' as spiritual light
Notice that 'nur' is defined as spiritual light, derived from 'tanwir', and is a praise of Allah's attribute of action.
The metaphor connects to the Quranic 'light verse'.
3
Interpret 'qulub' as intellects
The commentator explicitly states that 'hearts' here means 'intellects', because spiritual light resides in the intellect, and the heart is its locus.
This prevents a literal reading of the physical heart.
4
Grasp the metaphor in 'tahrir'
'Tahrir' (freeing) is a metaphor for the intellect being liberated from ignorance and blind imitation through knowledge.
The cause-effect relationship between enlightenment and freedom is highlighted.
5
Appreciate stylistic variation
The author used 'hearts' first and 'intellects' second for stylistic variety, not to indicate different concepts.
This shows attention to eloquent expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و(نور): مشتق من التَّنوير ؛ وهو إيجاد النُّور الحسيّ أو المعنوي، والمراد هنا : المعنوي، الذي ضرب الله تعالى مَثَله بقوله جلَّ مِن قائل: مَثَلُ نُورِهِ ... الآية ، فهو حمدٌ على صفة الفعل بعد إسناده للذات العليَّة ؛ إشارة لكونه تعالى محموداً لذاته ولصفاته .
وقوله : (قلوبنا :أي عقولنا ؛ لأنَّ النُّور المعنوي ينسب للعقول، وسمِّيت العقول قلوباً ؛ لحلولها بها .
قوله : (بمعرفة) : متعلق بـ(نور)، والباء سببية، وسيأتي معنى المعرفة، والعقائد، والتّوحيد.
قوله : ( وحرَّر) : معطوف على (نور) عطف سبب على مسبب؛ فهو من جملة صلة الموصول.
والتَّحرير: إخراج الرقبة من الرّقّ، فقد شبه العقول التي نارت بالمعارف وخرجت من الجهل والتقليد برقاب كانت في أسر الرّقّ، فأعتقها سيدها على سبيل الاستعارة بالكناية، والتحرير تخييل .
عبّر أوَّلاً بالقلوب، وثانياً : بالعقول ; تفتُناً
And 'Nur' is derived from 'tanwir', which is the creation of sensory or spiritual light, and what is meant here is the spiritual light, which Allah the Exalted gave as a parable in His saying, Mighty is He Who speaks: 'The parable of His light...' the verse. So it is praise for an attribute of action after attributing it to the High Essence, indicating that He, exalted, is praised for His essence and His attributes. And His saying: 'Our hearts' means our intellects, because spiritual light is attributed to intellects, and intellects are called hearts because they reside in them. His saying: 'with knowledge' is attached to 'Nur', and the 'ba' is causal, and the meaning of knowledge, beliefs, and tawhid will come later. His saying: 'and freed' is coordinated with 'Nur' as a coordination of cause to effect, so it is part of the relative clause. And 'tahrir' is the removal of the neck from slavery. He likened the intellects that were illuminated with knowledge and emerged from ignorance and imitation to necks that were in the bondage of slavery, so their master freed them by way of metaphor via katamera, and 'tahrir' is imaginary. He expressed first with 'hearts' and secondly with 'intellects' for stylistic variety.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage provides lexical and metaphorical commentary on several words from the opening line. The commentator first explains that 'nur' (light) is derived from 'tanwir', which can be sensory or spiritual; here it is spiritual, alluding to the Qur'anic verse about Allah's light. This praise is for an attribute of action, indicating that Allah is praised for both His essence and His attributes. Then 'qulubina' (our hearts) is interpreted as 'our intellects' because spiritual light is associated with intellects, and the term 'qalb' (heart) is used for intellect due to its residence in the heart. The phrase 'bi-ma'rifa' (with knowledge) is attached to 'nur' with a causal 'ba'. The word 'harrara' (freed) is coordinated with 'nur' as a cause to effect, meaning that enlightening causes freeing. The commentator then explains that 'tahrir' (freeing) is a metaphor: intellects that became illuminated with knowledge and left ignorance and imitation are like slaves being freed. Finally, the commentator notes that the author used 'hearts' first and 'intellects' second for stylistic variation.

قلوب
عقول
تحرير
تقليد
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'qulub' (hearts) in this context refers to the physical heart or emotional center.
Correction: The commentator clearly states that 'qulub' here means 'intellects' ('uqul'), as spiritual light is associated with the intellect, not the physical heart.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'qulubina' (our hearts) mean in this context?
A) The physical hearts
B) The intellects
C) The souls
D) The bodies

Key Takeaway
In this passage, 'nur' is spiritual light, 'qulub' means intellects, and 'tahrir' metaphorically describes the liberation from ignorance through knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage describes the intellect freed from ignorance as a slave emancipated from bondage. Do you feel spiritually free in your understanding of Allah and His attributes, or are you still held captive by doubts, cultural assumptions, or superficial learning? What chains need to be broken?
?
Spiritual light is attributed to the intellect. Is your intellect genuinely illuminated by the Qur'an and prophetic guidance, or are you content with a dim glow from secondary sources? What specific step can you take today to seek that direct light?

Freed from the Rope of Imitation
Explanation of the rope metaphor for imitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator uses the metaphor of the rope (ribqa) to describe the shackling effect of imitation (taqlid) on the intellect.

Core Idea
Imitation shackles the intellect like a rope around a calf's neck.

The commentator unpacks the phrase 'min ribqa' from the opening supplication. By tracing the original meaning of 'ribqa' as a literal rope used to lead a calf, he builds a vivid metaphor: the imitator is bound by blind following, just as the calf is bound. This lesson explores that metaphor and its implications for seeking truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the translation. Notice the etymology of 'ribqa' and how the commentator uses it.
2
Understand the metaphor
The commentator compares imitation to a rope. Identify the two things being compared: imitation (the thing likened) and the rope (the thing it is likened to).
The grammatical construction is 'idafah tashbihiyyah' — a metaphorical genitive construction.
3
Reflect on the commentator's exhortation
The passage ends with 'fata'ammal' (so reflect). Consider why the commentator urges reflection here. What is the danger of imitation?
Imitation prevents independent reasoning and can lead to blind following.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( من ربقة ) جارّ ومجرور متعلق بـ (حرر والربقة في الأصل: الحبل الذي يوضع في عنق العجل عند جلب أمه . و(الشوائب) : جمع شائبة بمعنى : الأخلاط، وإضافة (ربقة) لما بعده من إضافة المشبه به للمشبَّه ، وإضافة (شوائب) لما بعده بيانية والمعنى: وخلّص عقولنا من (التَّقليد) الشَّبيه بالرّبقة ؛ لأنَّ المقلّد مكبل بتقليده ؛ كتكبيل العجل بالحبل الَّذي في عنقه، فتدبَّر .
قوله : (على سيّدنا ) أي : أشرف بني آدم فهو سيد غيرهم بالأولى، والإضافة فيه لتعريف العهد الخارجي؛ أي: السَّيد المعيَّن المعلوم، وقدَّمه على (محمد) مع أنَّه صفةٌ له، والأصل تأخير الصفة عن الموصوف؛ إشارةً إلى استقلالها بنفسها حتى صارت كالعَلَم .
والسَّيّد لغة: من فاق غيره كرماً وحلماً ، قال الشاعر : [من الطويل]
ببذل وحلم ساد في قومه الفتى
. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
His saying: 'from a rope' is a preposition and noun governed by 'freed', and the 'ribqa' originally is the rope that is placed around the neck of a calf when its mother is brought. And 'ash-shawa'ib' is the plural of 'sha'iba' meaning mixtures. The addition of 'ribqa' to what follows is an addition of the likened to the likeness, and the addition of 'shawa'ib' to what follows is explicative. The meaning: And free our intellects from imitation, which is like a rope, because the imitator is shackled by his imitation, like the shackling of the calf by the rope on its neck, so reflect. His saying: 'upon our master' meaning the noblest of the children of Adam, so he is the master of others a fortiori. The addition here is for defining the external covenant, meaning the specific known master. He placed it before 'Muhammad' even though it is an attribute of him, and the norm is to delay the attribute after the described, indicating its independence to the point that it became like a proper noun. And 'al-sayyid' linguistically is one who surpasses others in generosity and forbearance. The poet said: By generosity and forbearance the youth became master among his people ...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage explains two more phrases from the opening. First, 'min ribqa' (from a rope) is attached to 'harrara' (freed). The commentator gives the original meaning of 'ribqa' as the rope placed around a calf's neck to lead its mother. The word 'shawā'ib' (impurities) is defined as mixtures. The grammatical construction involves a metaphor: imitation is likened to a rope that binds, just as a calf is tied. The commentator urges reflection. Second, 'alā sayyidinā' (upon our master) is explained: the phrase refers to the noblest human, the Prophet Muhammad, who is master of others a fortiori. The addition of 'sayyidina' before 'Muhammad' is a stylistic device indicating that the attribute has become like a proper noun. The linguistic definition of 'sayyid' is given, and a poetic line is cited (incomplete) to illustrate the meaning of mastery through generosity and forbearance.

شوائب
تقليد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'ribqa' refers to a physical rope used in the text literally.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that 'ribqa' is used figuratively to describe imitation; it is a metaphor, not a literal rope.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator compare imitation to in this passage?
A) A calf
B) A rope around a calf's neck
C) A mother's rope
D) A mixture

Key Takeaway
Imitation binds and restricts the intellect, just as a rope binds a calf; true seeker must reflect and break free.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What are some beliefs or practices in your life that you have accepted without examination, simply because you inherited them? In what ways are they like the rope around the calf's neck?
?

?

The Master of Humanity: Understanding 'Sayyidina'
Explanation of the honorific title 'sayyidina' before the name Muhammad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and theological significance of the title 'sayyid' when applied to the Prophet Muhammad, as explained by the commentator.

Core Idea
The attribute 'sayyid' is so distinctive that it functions like a proper name for the Prophet.

The commentator explains why the phrase 'alā sayyidinā' (upon our master) places the word 'sayyid' before the name 'Muhammad', contrary to usual Arabic grammar. This lesson unpacks the reason: the attribute has become so uniquely identifying that it stands alone, and it highlights the Prophet's supreme rank among all beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the key point: 'sayyidina' is placed before 'Muhammad' even though it is an attribute.
2
Understand the grammatical explanation
The commentator says that the attribute has become like a proper noun because of its independence. Consider what this implies about the uniqueness of the Prophet's status.
In Arabic, attributes normally follow the noun; the inversion here is deliberate.
3
Consider the linguistic definition of 'sayyid'
The commentator gives the linguistic meaning: one who surpasses in generosity and forbearance. The poetic line illustrates this. Reflect on how these qualities apply to the Prophet.
The poem is incomplete but the first hemistich is provided.

Freeing the Intellect from the Bondage of Taqlid
Al-Sawi lexically and rhetorically explains the metaphors in the author's opening prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi uses the metaphors of slavery and fetters to illustrate the necessity of liberating the intellect from blind imitation.

Core Idea
Taqlid is a rope that binds the intellect; tahrir is the act of freeing it.

In this opening supplication of the Khariqa, the author prays for liberation from the fetters of blind imitation. Al-Sawi's commentary unpacks the linguistic and rhetorical devices that make this prayer vivid and deeply instructive. This lesson explores how words like tahrir, riqba, and shawā'ib combine to form a powerful call to intellectual freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overall sense of the commentary.
Notice the density of rhetorical terms.
2
Understand the key metaphor
Al-Sawi explains tahrir as 'freeing a neck from slavery'. The intellect is likened to a freed slave.
This metaphor establishes the gravity of taqlid.
3
Examine the term 'riqba'
Riqba originally means a rope around a calf's neck. It is used to depict taqlid as a literal fetter.
The physical image reinforces the spiritual bondage.
4
Grasp the contrast with 'shawā'ib'
Shawā'ib (impurities) are what we seek to remove; taqlid is one of them.
Taqlid is not just a rope but also an impurity to be purified.
5
Reflect on the doctrinal point
The supplication asks to be freed from taqlid. Al-Sawi invites us to reflect on this as a call to seek true knowledge.
Consider why the author begins with this request.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتَّحرير: إخراج الرقبة من الرّقّ، فقد شبه العقول التي نارت بالمعارف وخرجت من الجهل والتقليد برقاب كانت في أسر الرّقّ، فأعتقها سيدها على سبيل الاستعارة بالكناية، والتحرير تخييل . عبّر أوَّلاً بالقلوب، وثانياً : بالعقول ؛ تفتُناً قوله : ( من ربقة ) جارّ ومجرور متعلق بـ (حرر) والربقة في الأصل: الحبل الذي يوضع في عنق العجل عند جلب أمه . و(الشوائب) : جمع شائبة بمعنى : الأخلاط، وإضافة (ربقة) لما بعده من إضافة المشبه به للمشبَّه ، وإضافة (شوائب) لما بعده بيانية والمعنى: وخلّص عقولنا من (التَّقليد) الشَّبيه بالرّبقة ؛ لأنَّ المقلّد مكبل بتقليده ؛ كتكبيل العجل بالحبل الَّذي في عنقه، فتدبَّر .
And 'at-tahrīr': extraction of the neck from slavery; for he compared the intellects that were illumined by knowledge and emerged from ignorance and imitation to necks that were in captivity of slavery, so their master freed them by way of metaphorical indication, and 'at-tahrīr' is an imaginative figure. He first expressed with 'hearts' and second with 'intellects'; as a variation. His saying: (from the collar) is a prepositional phrase attached to (he freed). And 'ar-riqba' originally: the rope that is placed around the neck of a calf when bringing its mother. And 'ash-shawā'ib': plural of 'shā'iba' meaning impurities. The addition of 'riqba' to what follows is an addition of the likened to the thing likened, and the addition of 'shawā'ib' to what follows is an appositional genitive. The meaning: and purify our intellects from (imitation) similar to the collar; because the imitator is fettered by his imitation, like the fettering of the calf by the rope on its neck, so reflect.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This is a lexical and rhetorical commentary on the opening supplication of the Khariqa. The commentator first explains the word 'tahrir' (تحرير) as 'extraction from slavery', noting that the author compared enlightened intellects to slaves that were freed by their master, using a metaphorical figure (takhayyil). He notes the variation in wording: 'hearts' first, then 'intellects'. Then he comments on the phrase 'min riqba' (من ربقة), explaining that 'riqba' is a rope placed around a calf's neck, and 'shawā'ib' (شوائب) means impurities. The grammatical constructions are analyzed: the addition of 'riqba' to what follows is a simile (tashbīh), while the addition of 'shawā'ib' is for specification (bayāniyya). The overall meaning is a prayer to purify the intellect from blind imitation (taqlīd), which is likened to a fetter that binds a calf. The passage concludes with 'reflect' (fatadabbar).

Key Terms
تحرير
ربقة
شائبة
تقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid (blind imitation) is an acceptable and safe way to practice religion.
Correction: The supplication depicts taqlid as a form of bondage (a rope around the neck) and an impurity from which we must be freed, showing it is a serious impediment to true knowledge.

Checkpoint

C) A type of impurity

Key Takeaway
Blind imitation (taqlid) is like a rope that binds the intellect; tahrir is the act of freeing the mind through knowledge.

The author enumerates nine linguistic investigations of 'Amma ba'd'.

This passage appears in many classical texts as a preface to detailed discussions. Here, the author methodically lists nine separate topics that scholars explore concerning the transition phrase 'Amma ba'd'. Definition of 'Amma' and Its Position in Discourse
The author defines 'amma' and explains its position with three types of transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of 'amma' as a discourse transition particle and the three types of transitions it introduces based on the degree of connection between topics.

Core Idea
'Amma' is a discourse tool for topic shifts, classified into three types.

This lesson focuses on the discourse particle 'amma'. The author defines it as a particle of emphasis that serves to transition from one topic to another. He then distinguishes three types of transitions based on the degree of connection between the topics: smooth transition, pure abrupt shift, and a mixture. Understanding this classification is essential for proper use of 'amma' in Arabic composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation to get a sense of the definition and classification. Notice how the author defines 'amma' and then explains its position.
The passage uses technical terms like 'takhallus' and 'iqtidab'.
2
Identify the three types of transition
Look for the three scenarios described based on correspondence: complete correspondence (takhallus), no correspondence (pure iqtidab), and partial correspondence (mixed iqtidab).
3
Examine the example of pure iqtidab
The poem provides an example of pure iqtidab. Notice how the topics of grey hair and praising Abu Sa'id are unrelated.
This illustrates an abrupt shift.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why the author provides this classification. It helps in analyzing Arabic discourse structure and the rhetorical effect of 'amma'.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأمَّا أَمَّا: فهي لمجرَّد التَّأكيد نائبة عن مهما ويكن، وأما موضعها: فيؤخذ من قولهم: هي كلمة يُؤتى بها للانتقال من أسلوب إلى آخر؛ أي: من غرض إلى آخر، فلا تقع بين كلامين متحدين، ولا أوَّل الكلام ولا آخره؛ فإن وقعت بين كلامين متغايرين بينهما مناسبة كليَّة سمِّي تخلصاً، وإن كان بينهما عدم مناسبة أصلاً؛ سمِّي اقتضاباً محضاً، وإن كان بينهما نوع مناسبة كما هنا؛ سمِّي اقتضاباً مشوباً بتخلص؛ فمثال الاقتضاب قول الشاعر: [من الخفيف] لو رأى الله أنَّ في الشَّيبِ خيراً جاورته الأبرار في الخلد شيبا كل يوم تُبدي صروف الليالي خلقاً من أبي سعيد غريبا
As for 'amma': it is for mere emphasis, standing in for 'mahma yakun'. And as for its position: it is taken from their saying: it is a word that is brought for transition from one style to another; i.e., from one topic to another, so it does not occur between two similar statements, nor at the beginning of speech nor at its end. For if it occurs between two differing statements between which there is a complete correspondence, it is called 'takhallus'; and if between them there is no correspondence at all, it is called pure 'iqtidab'; and if between them there is some correspondence, as here, it is called 'iqtidab' mixed with 'takhallus'. An example of 'iqtidab' is the poet's saying: [in the meter khafif] 'If God saw that in grey hair there is good, the righteous would reside with it in paradise gray-haired. Every day the vicissitudes of nights display a character of Abu Sa'id, strange.'
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage defines 'amma' and explains its discourse position. The author states that 'amma' is a particle of pure emphasis, substituting for the conditional phrase 'mahma yakun' (whatever may be). Then he discusses its position: it is used to transition from one style or topic to another. Therefore, it never appears between two identical statements, nor at the very beginning or end of speech. He then classifies the transition into three types: (1) 'takhallus' (smooth transition) when there is a complete correspondence between the two topics; (2) 'iqtidab mahd' (pure abrupt shift) when there is no correspondence at all; (3) 'iqtidab mashub bi takhallus' (mixture) when there is partial correspondence, which he notes is the case here. He provides two lines of poetry as an example of pure 'iqtidab', showing an abrupt shift from talking about grey hair to praising Abu Sa'id. The explanation follows the order: first define the particle, then its position with three scenarios.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'أما بعد' only has one usage.
Correction: The author shows it can be classified into three distinct types of transition based on the connection between topics: complete correspondence, no correspondence, and partial correspondence.

Checkpoint

C) اقتضاب مشوب بتخلص

The Meaning and Position of 'Amma'
Al-Sawi defines 'أما' and explains its positional rules with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the emphatic and conditional nature of 'أما' and how its position in speech determines whether the transition is graceful (takhallus) or abrupt (iqtidab).

Core Idea
'أما' is an emphatic conditional substitute that signals a transition between topics.

The phrase 'أما بعد' is a staple of Islamic scholarly writing. Before we dive into its grammar, Al-Sawi first clarifies what 'أما' itself means and where it should appear in speech. He distinguishes between graceful transitions and abrupt shifts, using poetry to illustrate the difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on 'أما' and its position
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the definition of 'أما' and the conditions for its placement. Then read the translation.
Notice that 'أما' substitutes for the conditional phrase 'مهما يكن'.
2
Identify the three types of transition
Al-Sawi describes three possibilities when 'أما' occurs between two different statements: complete suitability (takhallus), no suitability (iqtidab mahd), and partial suitability (iqtidab mashub bitakhallus). Find each in the text.
He gives an example for pure iqtidab and one for takhallus.
3
Analyze the poetic examples
Read the two poems and see how they illustrate abrupt vs. graceful transition. Ask yourself why the second poem is considered a takhallus.
The first poem shifts topics abruptly; the second moves from a natural image to generosity.
4
Reflect on the importance of this classification
Understanding these categories helps appreciate the rhetorical skill of classical authors in using 'أما بعد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[مطلب في أما بعد]
قوله : (أما بعد) : يتعلق بها تسعة مباحث :
الأوّل: في أمَّا الثَّاني: في موضعها، الثالث: في معناها، الرابع: في إعرابها، الخامس : في العامل فيها السادس : في أصلها، السَّابع في حكم الإتيان بها، الثَّامن في أوّل من تكلم بها، التّاسع: في الفاء بعدها .
فأمَّا أَمَّا : فهي لمجرَّد التَّأكيد نائبة عن مهما ويكن، وأما موضعها: فيؤخذ من قولهم : هي كلمة يُؤتى بها للانتقال من أسلوب إلى آخر؛ أي: من غرض إلى آخر، فلا تقع بين كلامين متحدين، ولا أوَّل الكلام ولا آخره؛ فإن وقعت بين كلامين متغايرين بينهما مناسبة كليَّة سمّي تخلصاً، وإن كان بينهما عدم مناسبة أصلاً ؛ سمّي اقتضاباً محضاً، وإن كان بينهما نوع مناسبة كما هنا ؛ سمّي اقتضاباً مشوباً بتخلص؛ فمثال الاقتضاب قول الشاعر :
[ من الخفيف]
لو رأى الله أنَّ في الشَّيبِ خيراً جاورته الأبرار في الخلد شيبا كل يوم تُبدي صروف الليالي خلقاً من أبي سعيد غريبا
ومثال التَّخلُّص قول الشاعر أيضاً :
[ من البسيط]
أمطلعَ الشَّمس تبغي أن تؤم بنا فقلت كلا ولكن مطلع الجودِ
وأمَّا معناها : فهو نقيض (قبل)، وتكون ظرف زمان كثيراً ومكان قليلاً ، وهي هنا للزَّمان لا غير . وقولهم : إنَّها للمكان باعتبار الرّقم، بعيد كما حققه الشارح .
وأما إعرابها : فلها أربعة أحوال : تعرب في ثلاثة، وتبنى في حالة كما هو مشهور .
وأمَّا العامل فيها : فهو (أمَّا ) على أنَّها من متعلقات الشَّرط ، أو الجزاء على أنَّها من متعلقاته ؛ فالتقدير على الأول : مهما يكن من شيء بعد ما تقدم، وعلى الثَّاني : مهما يكن من شيء فأقول بعد ما تقدم، وجعلها من متعلقات الجزاء أولى؛ لأنَّه يكون وجود المؤلّف معلقاً على وجود شيء مطلقاً .
وأما أصلها : فهو مهما يكن من شيء، كما تقدَّم.
وأما حكم الإتيان بها : فالاستحباب ؛ اقتداءً بالنبي ؛ لأنه كان يأتي بها في خطبه ومكاتباته .
وأما أوَّل من تكلَّم بها : فقد نظم الخلاف فيه بعضهم بقوله (۲) :
[من الطويل]
[Topic on 'Ama Ba'd]
His saying: ('Ama Ba'd): Nine sub-topics are attached to it:
First: On 'Amma; second: on its position; third: on its meaning; fourth: on its grammatical analysis; fifth: on the factor governing it; sixth: on its origin; seventh: on the ruling of using it; eighth: on the first one who spoke with it; ninth: on the Fa after it.
As for 'Amma: it is for mere emphasis, acting as a substitute for 'mahma yakun'. As for its position: it is taken from their saying: it is a word used to transition from one style to another, i.e., from one purpose to another, so it does not occur between two identical statements, nor at the beginning or end of speech. If it occurs between two different statements with total suitability, it is called takhallus (graceful transition). If there is no suitability at all, it is called pure iqtidab (abrupt transition). If there is a partial suitability, as here, it is called iqtidab mixed with takhallus. An example of iqtidab is the poet's saying (from al-khafif): 'If God saw that in whiteness there is good, the righteous would have neighbored Him in Paradise white. Every day the vicissitudes of nights display a character from Abu Sa'id strange.' And an example of takhallus is the poet's saying also (from al-basit): 'O rising sun, do you intend to lead us? I said: No, but the rising of generosity.' As for its meaning: it is the opposite of 'before', and it is often a time adverb and rarely a place adverb, and here it is for time only. Their saying that it is for place considering the digit is far-fetched, as the commentator has verified. As for its grammatical analysis: it has four states; it declines in three and is indeclinable in one, as is well-known. As for the factor governing it: it is 'Amma, on the basis that it is part of the protasis, or the apodosis on the basis that it is part of it. The estimation on the first is: 'whatever thing exists after what has preceded', and on the second: 'whatever thing exists, so I say after what has preceded'. Making it part of the apodosis is preferable because the author's existence is then dependent on the existence of something absolutely. As for its origin: it is 'mahma yakun min shay', as previously. As for the ruling of using it: it is recommended, following the Prophet, because he used it in his sermons and epistles. As for the first one who spoke with it: some have versified the difference of opinion about it in his saying (2): [from al-tawil]
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a detailed methodological explanation of the phrase 'أما بعد' (Amma Ba'd), which is commonly used by Muslim scholars after the basmala and hamdalah to signal the start of the actual subject matter. The author, al-Sawi, enumerates nine sub-topics (mabaahith) that pertain to this phrase and proceeds to explain each in turn.

The nine sub-topics are:
1. The word 'أما' (Amma) itself.
2. Its position in speech.
3. Its meaning.
4. Its grammatical analysis (i'rab).
5. The factor ('amil) that governs it.
6. Its original form.
7. The ruling on using it.
8. The first person to use it.
9. The letter 'Fa' that follows it (in the phrase 'أما بعد').

Al-Sawi begins with the first sub-topic: 'أما' is used for emphasis and substitutes for the conditional phrase 'مهما يكن' (mahma yakun, 'whatever may be'). This shows that 'أما' has a conditional function in origin.

For the second sub-topic, he explains that 'أما' is used to transition from one style or purpose to another. It should not occur between two identical statements, nor at the very beginning or end of speech. If it appears between two different statements that are entirely suitable for each other, the transition is called 'تخلص' (takhallus, graceful shift). If there is no suitability at all, it is called 'اقتضاب محض' (pure iqtidab, abrupt shift). If there is partial suitability, as in this text, it is called 'اقتضاب مشوب بتخلص' (iqtidab mixed with takhallus). He then provides two poetic examples: one from al-Khafif meter illustrating iqtidab, and one from al-Basit meter illustrating takhallus.

Third, the meaning of 'أما' in this context is the opposite of 'قبل' (before), and it functions mostly as a time adverb, rarely a place adverb. Here it is only for time. The claim that it could be for place considering 'الرقم' (the digit) is dismissed as far-fetched.

Fourth, its grammatical analysis: 'أما' has four states, being declinable in three and indeclinable in one, which is well-known.

Fifth, the factor governing it: it can be part of the protasis (شرط) or the apodosis (جزاء). If it is considered from the protasis, the implied meaning is 'مهما يكن من شيء بعد ما تقدم' (whatever thing exists after what has preceded). If from the apodosis, it is 'مهما يكن من شيء فأقول بعد ما تقدم' (whatever thing exists, so I say after what has preceded). Al-Sawi prefers the latter because it makes the existence of the author's statement dependent on something absolute.

Sixth, its origin: it is 'مهما يكن من شيء' (whatever thing exists), as previously mentioned.

Seventh, the ruling on using it: it is recommended (استحباب), following the practice of the Prophet who used it in his sermons and letters.

Eighth, the first person to use it: it is said that the disagreement among scholars about this has been versified by someone, and a couplet was mentioned, though the actual verse is not fully provided in the OCR.

The ninth sub-topic (الفاء بعدها) is not elaborated on in this window; the enumeration is given but the explanation is cut off.

Key Terms
أما
مهما يكن
تخلص
اقتضاب محض
اقتضاب مشوب بتخلص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'أما' is a simple interjection without grammatical significance.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows it functions as a conditional substitute and has specific rules for placement and rhetorical effect.

Checkpoint
What is the term for a transition where there is no suitability at all between the two statements?
A) تخلص
B) اقتضاب محض
C) اقتضاب مشوب بتخلص
D) اقتضاب مطلق

Key Takeaway
'أما' is an emphatic conditional particle used to mark transitions, and its positional appropriateness defines the rhetorical style of the shift.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a recent conversation where you abruptly changed the topic. How did that affect the listener's understanding? Based on this lesson, what could you do differently to make your transitions more graceful?
?
Reflect on your own speech or writing: do you tend to use 'transition words' effectively? When you move from one idea to another, are you conscious of whether the shift is suitable or jarring? Commit to improving one transition in your next conversation or piece of writing.
?

What would it look like to make a 'graceful' transition when changing the subject in a difficult conversation?

The Meaning and Grammar of 'Amma Ba'd'
Al-Sawi explains the meaning and grammatical analysis of 'أما بعد' and discusses the factor governing it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'أما' as a time adverb, its grammatical states, and how the factor governing it can be understood either from the protasis or apodosis.

Core Idea
'أما بعد' means 'after what has preceded', and its grammar involves both declinable and indeclinable states.

After establishing the transitional function of 'أما', Al-Sawi moves to its specific meaning and grammatical analysis. He clarifies that in 'أما بعد', the word 'بعد' is primarily temporal here. He then outlines its four grammatical states and discusses the factor (عامل) that governs the phrase, offering two interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the section on meaning and grammar
Read the Arabic passage from 'وأمَّا معناها' through to 'على وجود شيء مطلقاً'. Notice the emphasis on 'بعد' as a time adverb.
Al-Sawi dismisses the view that it could be a place adverb here.
2
Understand the meaning of 'بعد'
It means the opposite of 'قبل' (before) and is mostly used for time. Here it is exclusively temporal.
The reference to 'الرقم' (the digit) is obscure, but Al-Sawi rejects it.
3
Learn about the grammatical states
'أما' has four states; it declines in three and is indeclinable in one. This is a standard rule.
The details of these states are not elaborated here; it is assumed known.
4
Analyze the factor governing 'أما بعد'
Al-Sawi presents two possibilities: the factor is from the conditional protasis or from the apodosis. He prefers the latter because it ties the author's statement to an absolute condition.
The translation of the implied meanings helps clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[مطلب في أما بعد]
قوله : (أما بعد) : يتعلق بها تسعة مباحث :
الأوّل: في أمَّا الثَّاني: في موضعها، الثالث: في معناها، الرابع: في إعرابها، الخامس : في العامل فيها السادس : في أصلها، السَّابع في حكم الإتيان بها، الثَّامن في أوّل من تكلم بها، التّاسع: في الفاء بعدها .
فأمَّا أَمَّا : فهي لمجرَّد التَّأكيد نائبة عن مهما ويكن، وأما موضعها: فيؤخذ من قولهم : هي كلمة يُؤتى بها للانتقال من أسلوب إلى آخر؛ أي: من غرض إلى آخر، فلا تقع بين كلامين متحدين، ولا أوَّل الكلام ولا آخره؛ فإن وقعت بين كلامين متغايرين بينهما مناسبة كليَّة سمّي تخلصاً، وإن كان بينهما عدم مناسبة أصلاً ؛ سمّي اقتضاباً محضاً، وإن كان بينهما نوع مناسبة كما هنا ؛ سمّي اقتضاباً مشوباً بتخلص؛ فمثال الاقتضاب قول الشاعر :
[ من الخفيف]
لو رأى الله أنَّ في الشَّيبِ خيراً جاورته الأبرار في الخلد شيبا كل يوم تُبدي صروف الليالي خلقاً من أبي سعيد غريبا
ومثال التَّخلُّص قول الشاعر أيضاً :
[ من البسيط]
أمطلعَ الشَّمس تبغي أن تؤم بنا فقلت كلا ولكن مطلع الجودِ
وأمَّا معناها : فهو نقيض (قبل)، وتكون ظرف زمان كثيراً ومكان قليلاً ، وهي هنا للزَّمان لا غير . وقولهم : إنَّها للمكان باعتبار الرّقم، بعيد كما حققه الشارح .
وأما إعرابها : فلها أربعة أحوال : تعرب في ثلاثة، وتبنى في حالة كما هو مشهور .
وأمَّا العامل فيها : فهو (أمَّا ) على أنَّها من متعلقات الشَّرط ، أو الجزاء على أنَّها من متعلقاته ؛ فالتقدير على الأول : مهما يكن من شيء بعد ما تقدم، وعلى الثَّاني : مهما يكن من شيء فأقول بعد ما تقدم، وجعلها من متعلقات الجزاء أولى؛ لأنَّه يكون وجود المؤلّف معلقاً على وجود شيء مطلقاً .
وأما أصلها : فهو مهما يكن من شيء، كما تقدَّم.
وأما حكم الإتيان بها : فالاستحباب ؛ اقتداءً بالنبي ؛ لأنه كان يأتي بها في خطبه ومكاتباته .
وأما أوَّل من تكلَّم بها : فقد نظم الخلاف فيه بعضهم بقوله (۲) :
[من الطويل]
[Topic on 'Ama Ba'd]
His saying: ('Ama Ba'd): Nine sub-topics are attached to it:
First: On 'Amma; second: on its position; third: on its meaning; fourth: on its grammatical analysis; fifth: on the factor governing it; sixth: on its origin; seventh: on the ruling of using it; eighth: on the first one who spoke with it; ninth: on the Fa after it.
As for 'Amma: it is for mere emphasis, acting as a substitute for 'mahma yakun'. As for its position: it is taken from their saying: it is a word used to transition from one style to another, i.e., from one purpose to another, so it does not occur between two identical statements, nor at the beginning or end of speech. If it occurs between two different statements with total suitability, it is called takhallus (graceful transition). If there is no suitability at all, it is called pure iqtidab (abrupt transition). If there is a partial suitability, as here, it is called iqtidab mixed with takhallus. An example of iqtidab is the poet's saying (from al-khafif): 'If God saw that in whiteness there is good, the righteous would have neighbored Him in Paradise white. Every day the vicissitudes of nights display a character from Abu Sa'id strange.' And an example of takhallus is the poet's saying also (from al-basit): 'O rising sun, do you intend to lead us? I said: No, but the rising of generosity.' As for its meaning: it is the opposite of 'before', and it is often a time adverb and rarely a place adverb, and here it is for time only. Their saying that it is for place considering the digit is far-fetched, as the commentator has verified. As for its grammatical analysis: it has four states; it declines in three and is indeclinable in one, as is well-known. As for the factor governing it: it is 'Amma, on the basis that it is part of the protasis, or the apodosis on the basis that it is part of it. The estimation on the first is: 'whatever thing exists after what has preceded', and on the second: 'whatever thing exists, so I say after what has preceded'. Making it part of the apodosis is preferable because the author's existence is then dependent on the existence of something absolutely. As for its origin: it is 'mahma yakun min shay', as previously. As for the ruling of using it: it is recommended, following the Prophet, because he used it in his sermons and epistles. As for the first one who spoke with it: some have versified the difference of opinion about it in his saying (2): [from al-tawil]
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a detailed methodological explanation of the phrase 'أما بعد' (Amma Ba'd), which is commonly used by Muslim scholars after the basmala and hamdalah to signal the start of the actual subject matter. The author, al-Sawi, enumerates nine sub-topics (mabaahith) that pertain to this phrase and proceeds to explain each in turn.

The nine sub-topics are:
1. The word 'أما' (Amma) itself.
2. Its position in speech.
3. Its meaning.
4. Its grammatical analysis (i'rab).
5. The factor ('amil) that governs it.
6. Its original form.
7. The ruling on using it.
8. The first person to use it.
9. The letter 'Fa' that follows it (in the phrase 'أما بعد').

Al-Sawi begins with the first sub-topic: 'أما' is used for emphasis and substitutes for the conditional phrase 'مهما يكن' (mahma yakun, 'whatever may be'). This shows that 'أما' has a conditional function in origin.

For the second sub-topic, he explains that 'أما' is used to transition from one style or purpose to another. It should not occur between two identical statements, nor at the very beginning or end of speech. If it appears between two different statements that are entirely suitable for each other, the transition is called 'تخلص' (takhallus, graceful shift). If there is no suitability at all, it is called 'اقتضاب محض' (pure iqtidab, abrupt shift). If there is partial suitability, as in this text, it is called 'اقتضاب مشوب بتخلص' (iqtidab mixed with takhallus). He then provides two poetic examples: one from al-Khafif meter illustrating iqtidab, and one from al-Basit meter illustrating takhallus.

Third, the meaning of 'أما' in this context is the opposite of 'قبل' (before), and it functions mostly as a time adverb, rarely a place adverb. Here it is only for time. The claim that it could be for place considering 'الرقم' (the digit) is dismissed as far-fetched.

Fourth, its grammatical analysis: 'أما' has four states, being declinable in three and indeclinable in one, which is well-known.

Fifth, the factor governing it: it can be part of the protasis (شرط) or the apodosis (جزاء). If it is considered from the protasis, the implied meaning is 'مهما يكن من شيء بعد ما تقدم' (whatever thing exists after what has preceded). If from the apodosis, it is 'مهما يكن من شيء فأقول بعد ما تقدم' (whatever thing exists, so I say after what has preceded). Al-Sawi prefers the latter because it makes the existence of the author's statement dependent on something absolute.

Sixth, its origin: it is 'مهما يكن من شيء' (whatever thing exists), as previously mentioned.

Seventh, the ruling on using it: it is recommended (استحباب), following the practice of the Prophet who used it in his sermons and letters.

Eighth, the first person to use it: it is said that the disagreement among scholars about this has been versified by someone, and a couplet was mentioned, though the actual verse is not fully provided in the OCR.

The ninth sub-topic (الفاء بعدها) is not elaborated on in this window; the enumeration is given but the explanation is cut off.

ظرف مكان
إعراب
عامل
شرط
جزاء

According to Al-Sawi, which interpretation of the factor governing 'أما بعد' is preferable?
A) It is from the protasis
B) It is from the apodosis
C) Both are equally valid
D) Neither is valid

Key Takeaway
'أما بعد' primarily denotes time ('after'), and its grammatical analysis reveals a conditional origin best interpreted as part of the apodosis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The phrase 'أما بعد' marks a transition to the main content after the opening praise. Reflect on your own habits: Do you rush into important discussions without appropriate preparation? How does the deliberate pause of 'أما بعد' challenge your approach to speaking with purpose? Commit today to think twice before speaking.
?
Al-Sawi argues that attaching 'أما بعد' to the apodosis makes the author's existence dependent on something absolutely. In your life, what are you making your existence dependent on? Are you aware of the subtle linguistic structures that reveal your deepest assumptions? Let this grammatical insight expose any conditional thinking that undermines your reliance on Allah.

Origin, Ruling, and First User of 'Amma Ba'd'
Al-Sawi covers the origin, ruling, and first user of 'أما بعد', with a poetic citation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the origin of 'أما بعد' is the conditional phrase 'مهما يكن من شيء', that using it is recommended (mustahabb) following the Prophet's practice, and that scholars differ on who first used it.

Core Idea
'أما بعد' originates from a conditional, is recommended by the Prophet's example, and its first user is disputed.

Having covered the meaning and grammar, Al-Sawi now addresses three remaining sub-topics: the original form of 'أما', the legal ruling on its use, and the identity of the first person to employ it. He briefly mentions the ninth sub-topic (the letter Fa after 'أما بعد'), but does not elaborate here.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on origin, ruling, and first user
Read from 'وأما أصلها' to the end of the provided text. Note the succinct statements about each sub-topic.
The passage ends with a poetic line that versifies the scholarly dispute about the first user.
2
Understand the origin of 'أما'
Al-Sawi repeats that its origin is 'مهما يكن من شيء' (whatever thing exists), reinforcing the conditional nature.
This was already alluded to in the first sub-topic.
3
Learn the ruling on using it
It is mustahabb (recommended), not obligatory, as a form of following the Prophet who used it in sermons and letters.
This is a legal (fiqhi) dimension.
4
Discover the first user dispute
Al-Sawi cites a poetic verse that summarizes the different opinions about the first person to say 'أما بعد'.
The actual verse is not fully given in the OCR; only a reference.

Meaning and Grammatical Cases of 'ammā'
Al-Sawi defines 'ammā' and explains its grammatical cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'ammā' as the opposite of 'before', its usage as a time (and rarely place) adverb, and its four grammatical states: three inflected and one built.

Core Idea
'ammā' has distinct inflection patterns and primarily denotes time, rarely place.

In this passage, al-Sawi provides a precise definition of 'ammā' and outlines its grammatical behavior. He explains its primary temporal function, dismisses a far-fetched spatial interpretation, and categorizes its four i'rāb states. This lesson will help you grasp both the meaning and the inflectional rules of this common Arabic word.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice how al-Sawi structures the explanation: first meaning, then grammatical analysis.
2
Understand the meaning definition
Focus on the first part: 'ammā' is the opposite of 'before', mostly a time adverb, rarely a place adverb. Al-Sawi clarifies that here it is temporal only.
He also rejects a metaphorical spatial interpretation as far-fetched.
3
Examine the grammatical states
The second part states that 'ammā' has four states: three where it is fully inflected (mu'rab) and one where it is built (mabnī). These are standard grammatical categories.
This is a well-known rule among grammarians.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you remember the number of inflectional cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما معناها: فهو نقيض (قبل)، وتكون ظرف زمان كثيراً ومكان قليلاً، وهي هنا للزَّمان لا غير. وقولهم: إنَّها للمكان باعتبار الرّقم، بعيد كما حققه الشارح رضي الله عنه. وأما إعرابها: فلها أربعة أحوال: تعرب في ثلاثة، وتبنى في حالة كما هو مشهور.
As for its meaning: it is the opposite of 'before', and it is often a time adverb and rarely a place adverb, and here it is for time only. And their saying: it is for place in consideration of rank, is far-fetched, as the commentator (al-Shāriḥ) verified, may Allah be pleased with him. And as for its grammatical analysis: it has four states: it is inflected in three, and built in one, as is well-known.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'ammā' and explains its grammatical behavior. Al-Sawi first states its meaning: it is the opposite of 'qabl' (before), and functions mostly as a time adverb, rarely as a place adverb. In this context, it is solely temporal. He dismisses the opinion that it can be for place metaphorically (by rank) as far-fetched. Then he discusses its i'rāb: it has four possible states—three where it is fully inflected (mu'rab) and one where it is built (mabnī), which is the common knowledge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'ammā' is only a time adverb and never a place adverb.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that 'ammā' can rarely be a place adverb, though in this context it is temporal. He also dismisses the metaphorical spatial interpretation as far-fetched.

'ammā' has four grammatical states: three mu'rab and one mabnī, and its primary meaning is the opposite of 'before', mostly temporal, rarely spatial.

The Factor, Origin, and Ruling of 'Ammā'
Al-Sawi enumerates three aspects of 'ammā': its operator, origin, and ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key aspects of the Arabic particle 'ammā': its grammatical operator, its origin, and the juristic ruling for its use.

Core Idea
'Ammā' carries its own operator, originates from an absolute condition, and its use is recommended.

In this passage, al-Sawi completes his analysis of 'ammā' by examining three dimensions: the linguistic mechanism that governs it, its underlying origin, and the prophetic precedent for its usage. Understanding these points provides a full picture of how this important particle functions in Arabic discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage aloud to get a sense of its rhythmic enumeration. Notice the repetition of 'وأما' which introduces each new point.
2
Identify the three topics
The passage clearly marks three topics: the operator (العامل), the origin (الأصل), and the ruling (الحكم). Underline each term.
3
Analyze the operator discussion
Focus on the two possible interpretations for the operator of 'ammā'. Understand why al-Sawi prefers the apodosis interpretation.
This involves a subtle grammatical point.
4
Consider the origin and ruling
The origin is a fixed phrase 'whatever may be of something'. The ruling is istihbāb, based on Prophetic practice. Reflect on how these two points integrate with the operator discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العامل فيها: فهو (أمَّا) على أنَّها من متعلقات الشَّرط، أو الجزاء على أنَّها من متعلقاته؛ فالتقدير على الأول: مهما يكن من شيء بعد ما تقدم، وعلى الثَّاني: مهما يكن من شيء فأقول بعد ما تقدم، وجعلها من متعلقات الجزاء أولى؛ لأنَّه يكون وجود المؤلّف معلقاً على وجود شيء مطلقاً. وأما أصلها: فهو مهما يكن من شيء، كما تقدَّم. وأما حكم الإتيان بها: فالاستحباب؛ اقتداءً بالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم؛ لأنه كان يأتي بها في خطبه ومكاتباته.
And as for the grammatical operator (al-'āmil) acting upon it: it is (ammā) according to it being among the appurtenances of the condition (sharṭ), or the apodosis (jazā') according to it being among its appurtenances. So the implied meaning on the first is: 'whatever thing may be after what has preceded', and on the second: 'whatever thing may be, I say after what has preceded'. And making it among the appurtenances of the apodosis is preferable because the existence of the author becomes dependent on the existence of something absolutely. And as for its origin: it is 'mahmā yakun min shay'' (whatever may be of something), as previously stated. And as for the ruling of employing it: it is recommended (istihbāb), following the Prophet (peace be upon him) because he used it in his sermons and letters.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage continues the analysis of 'ammā' by focusing on three further aspects: its grammatical operator (al-'āmil), its origin, and its ruling. First, al-Sawi explains that the operator acting upon 'ammā' is itself, based on whether it is considered related to the condition (sharṭ) or to the apodosis (jazā'). The implied meaning changes accordingly. He prefers treating it as part of the apodosis because it makes the author's existence dependent on an absolute condition. Then he mentions that its origin is the phrase 'mahmā yakun min shay'' (whatever may be of something). Finally, he states that the ruling for using 'ammā' is istihbāb (recommendation), following the Prophet's practice in his sermons and letters.

Correction: Al-Sawi shows that the operator is 'ammā' itself, and it can be related to either the condition or the apodosis, with a preferred interpretation.

Checkpoint

D) Because the majority of scholars say so

Key Takeaway
The particle 'ammā' has a self-referential operator, originates from an absolute conditional phrase, and its use is a recommended sunnah.

The First User of 'ammā' and the Role of Its Fā'
Al-Sawi relates the dispute on the first user of 'ammā' and explains the role of the fā' that follows it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical dispute over the first user of 'ammā' and the grammatical role of the fā' that follows it.

Core Idea
The first use of 'ammā' is disputed; its fā' always connects the answer.

Al-Sawi concludes his discussion of 'ammā' with two final points: who first used it and the function of the accompanying fā'. These details, though seemingly minor, reflect the precision with which classical scholars treated the Arabic language and its rhetorical conventions. Understanding them helps you appreciate the depth behind a common construction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the gist.
2
Examine the verse of opinions
Look at the poetic verse listing the five opinions. Notice the names mentioned: Dāwūd, Saḥbān, Kaʿb, Yaʿrub.
The verse is a mnemonic for the dispute.
3
Understand the fā' rule
Focus on the last sentence: the fā' after 'ammā' is a connector (rābiṭa) for the answer (jawāb). This is its established grammatical function.
This rule applies whenever 'ammā' is used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أول من تكلَّم بها: فقد نظم الخلاف فيه بعضهم بقوله:
[من الطويل]
جرى الخلف أما بعد من كان بادئاً بها خمسُ أقوال وداود أقربُ وكانت له فصل الخطاب وبعده فَقِسْ فَسَحبانُ فكعب فيعربُ
وأما الفاء بعدها: فهي رابطة للجواب.
And as for the first person who spoke using it: someone has versified the disagreement about it in his poem: 'The dispute occurred about 'ammā' – who was the first to use it. There are five opinions, and Dāwūd is the closest. He had decisive speech, and after him, compare – Saḥbān, then Ka'b, then Ya'rub.' And as for the fā' after it: it is a connector of the answer.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage addresses two final points about 'ammā'. First, the identity of the first person to use it in speech. Al-Sawi reports that this is a matter of dispute, and someone composed a poem listing the opinions: there are five different views, with Dāwūd (al-Asbahānī?) being the most likely. The poem mentions Dāwūd, then others after him: Saḥbān, Ka'b, and Ya'rub. Second, he explains the function of the fā' that often follows 'ammā': it serves as a connector (rābiṭa) for the answer (jawāb). This ties back to the earlier discussion of 'ammā' being related to the apodosis.
