Analyzing the Word 'Sharḥ' in the Matn
Al-Sawi analyzes the word 'sharḥ' with three interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible interpretations of the word 'sharḥ' in the matn and why its attribution to the book is figurative.

Core Idea
Al-Sawi unpacks the meaning of 'sharḥ' to reveal it is figuratively attributed to the book.

In this passage, al-Sawi examines a single word from the matn: 'sharḥ'. Although it seems straightforward, he shows that the word can be understood in three distinct ways, each requiring a different grammatical or rhetorical analysis. This careful attention to language is characteristic of the scholarly tradition, where every term is scrutinized for precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence once, noting its compactness. Al-Sawi uses a compressed style typical of a commentary.
The passage is only one sentence but contains multiple layers.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Al-Sawi gives three possibilities: (1) 'sharḥ' as active participle, (2) ellipsis of an annexed noun, (3) verbal noun used metaphorically. Identify each in the text.
Look for the key indicators: 'إما بمعنى شارح', 'على حذف مضاف', 'أطلق عليه المعنى المصدري'.
3
Understand the figurative attribution
Al-Sawi concludes that in all three interpretations, the attribution of 'sharḥ' to the book is figurative. Ask: why can't the book itself be literally an explanation?
The true explainer is the person (the author), not the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (شرح): إما بمعنى شارح أو الكلام على حذف مضاف؛ أي: ذو شرح، أو أطلق عليه المعنى المصدري؛ مبالغة كما في: زيد عدل، وعلى كل فالإسناد له مجاز، وإلا فالموضّح والمبيِّن إنَّما هو الشَّخص.
His saying: (Sharḥ): either in the sense of 'explainer' (shāriḥ), or the speech is by ellipsis of an annexed noun: i.e. 'possessor of an explanation' (dhū sharḥ), or the verbal noun meaning is applied to it by way of exaggeration, as in 'Zayd is justice'. In any case, the attribution to it (the book) is figurative, otherwise the explainer and clarifier is only the person.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the word 'sharḥ' as it appears in the matn. Al-Sawi gives three possible interpretations: (1) it means 'shāriḥ' (explainer), i.e., an active participle; (2) it involves ellipsis of an annexed noun, so the full phrase would be 'dhū sharḥ' (possessor of an explanation); (3) the verbal noun (masdar) meaning is applied to the book metaphorically, as in the phrase 'Zayd is justice' meaning 'a just man' (exaggeration). In all interpretations, the attribution of 'sharḥ' to the book is figurative (majāz), because the true explainer is the person (the author), not the book itself.

Key Terms

حذف مضاف
المعنى المصدري
مجاز
مبالغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the book itself is literally an explanation, meaning it can explain itself without an author.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that the attribution is figurative; the true explainer is the person (the author), not the book.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi's analysis of the word 'sharḥ' shows that careful attention to language reveals multiple possible meanings, all of which indicate that the book is only figuratively called an explanation.

The Meaning of 'Laṭīf' in the Matn: Literal and Figurative
Al-Sawi explains the literal and figurative meanings of 'laṭīf' as used in the matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the literal meanings of 'laṭīf' and how al-Sawi uses metonymy and metaphor to derive its intended theological sense.

Core Idea
The word 'laṭīf' is not about physical size but about easiness of understanding.

Al-Sawi unpacks the term 'laṭīf' as it appears in the matn. He first lists its literal dictionary meanings, then explains that the intended meaning here is a necessary consequence of those literals. This involves a careful analysis of figurative language. Pay close attention to how he distinguishes metonymy from metaphor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English to get a sense of al-Sawi's analysis. Notice the terms he uses.
2
Identify the three literal meanings
List the three physical descriptions of 'laṭīf': delicate-bodied, transparent, and small-sized.
3
Understand the primary figurative interpretation
Note that al-Sawi says the intended meaning is the necessary consequence (lāzim), making it a metonymy (majāz mursal).
Metonymy here uses the literal meaning to imply its logical effect.
4
Consider the alternative metaphorical interpretation
See how he also suggests it could be a metaphor (isti'āra) by comparing ease of acquisition to physical delicacy.
5
Reflect on the derivative derivation
Understand that 'laṭīf' meaning 'easy to grasp' is derived in a derivative way (tābi‘iyya) from the metaphorical usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (لطيف): هو في الأصل يطلق على رقيق القوام، وعلى الشَّفَّاف الذي لا يحجب ما وراءه، وعلى صغير الحجم، والمراد هنا: لازمه، فهو مجاز مرسل من إطلاق الملزوم وإرادة اللازم. ويحتمل أنه مجاز استعارة؛ بأن شبه سهولة الأخذ برقّة القوام، أو الشَّفَّاف، أو صغير الحجم، واستعير اسم المشبه به للمشبَّه، واشتقُ لطيف؛ بمعنى: سهل المأخذ على طريق التَّبعيَّة.
His saying: (Laṭīf): it is originally used for delicate-bodied, for transparent that does not hide what is behind it, and for small-sized. And what is intended here is its necessary consequence, so it is a metonymy (majāz mursal) by using the original meaning to imply the necessary effect. And it is possible that it is a metaphorical figure (majāz isti‘āra), by comparing ease of acquisition to delicacy of body, or transparency, or smallness of size, and the name of the compared-to is used for the compared, and 'laṭīf' is derived meaning 'easy to grasp' in a derivative way.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage explains the word 'laṭīf' (meaning 'subtle', 'delicate', 'latent') as it appears in the matn. Al-Sawi notes three original literal meanings: (1) having a delicate body, (2) transparent (shaffāf) such that it does not hide what is behind, (3) small in size. The intended meaning here, however, is not these literal senses but their necessary consequence (lāzim), which is 'easy to grasp' or 'easy to understand'. Thus it is a metonymy (majāz mursal) where the original meaning (malzūm) is used to imply the necessary effect (lāzim). He also proposes another possibility: it could be a metaphorical figure (isti‘āra) where the ease of acquisition is likened to physical delicacy, transparency, or small size, and the term for the physical quality is borrowed. On this second reading, 'laṭīf' is derived to mean 'easy to take' in a derivative (tābi‘iyya) manner.

Key Terms

رقيق القوام
شفاف
مجاز مرسل
مجاز استعارة
الملزوم
اللازم
التَّبعيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'laṭīf' here means small in size, as one of its literal meanings.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that the intended meaning is not the literal sense but its necessary consequence, 'easy to understand', via metonymy.

Checkpoint
What type of figurative language does al-Sawi consider primary for 'laṭīf' in this context?
A) Metonymy (majāz mursal)
B) Simile (tashbīh)
C) Metaphor (isti‘āra)
D) Hyperbole (mubālagha)

Key Takeaway
The term 'laṭīf' in the matn is interpreted through rhetorical devices to mean 'easy to understand', not a physical attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Derivative Metaphor in 'alā Muqaddimatī'
Al-Sawi explains the derivative metaphor in the phrase 'alā muqaddimatī'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the preposition 'alā' in the phrase 'alā muqaddimatī' is used as a derivative metaphor (isti‘āra tābi‘iyya) and why this matters for interpreting the text.

Core Idea
The preposition 'alā' in 'alā muqaddimatī' is a metaphorical borrowing for connection, not physical location.

In this brief but dense passage, al-Sawi unpacks a single phrase from the matn: 'alā muqaddimatī'. He reveals that the preposition 'alā' is not used literally but metaphorically, likening the commentary's dependence on the introduction to something resting upon something else. This rhetorical insight is essential for understanding the relationship between the sharh and the matn.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the author's own words introduce the concept of a derivative metaphor.
The translation is provided to help you follow the reasoning.
2
Identify the metaphor mechanism
Notice that al-Sawi identifies a derivative metaphor (isti‘āra tābi‘iyya) and explains the likening: the commentary's connection to the introduction is compared to something superior over something inferior.
The comparison then extends from generalities to particulars.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this metaphor is important: it clarifies that the preposition 'alā' here signifies affiliation and dependency, not physical superiority. This affects how we understand the relationship between the commentary and its introduction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (على مقدمتي): في الكلام استعارة تبعيَّة؛ حيث شبه ارتباط الشَّرح بالمقدمة بارتباط مستعل بمُستَعلى عليه، فسرى التشبيه من الكليات إلى الجزئيات، فاستعيرت على الموضوعة للاستعلاء الخاص لمعنى اللَّام على طريق الاستعارة التَّبعيَّة.
His saying: (alā muqaddimatī): in the speech there is a derivative metaphor (isti‘āra tābi‘iyya). For he likened the connection of the commentary with the introduction to the connection of something superior with something over which it is superior. Then the comparison extended from generalities to particulars, and the preposition 'alā' which is placed for specific superiority was borrowed for the meaning of the lām (of belonging) by way of derivative metaphor.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage explains the metaphorical usage of the preposition 'alā' (on/upon) in the phrase 'alā muqaddimatī' (upon my introduction) from the matn. Al-Sawi identifies it as a derivative metaphor (isti‘āra tābi‘iyya). The commentator compares the relationship between the commentary (sharḥ) and the introduction (muqaddima) to the relationship of something superior over something inferior (isti‘lā'). The comparison starts from generalities and applies to particulars. As a result, the preposition 'alā', which is originally used for physical superiority, is borrowed to convey the meaning of affiliation or belonging (as if saying 'the commentary belongs to the introduction' or 'based on it'). This is a derivative metaphor because the borrowing is in the preposition, which is a derivative (not a primary noun).

Key Terms
على مقدمتي
استعارة تبعية
الاستعلاء
اللام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'alā' in 'alā muqaddimatī' indicates physical location or literal superiority.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that it is a derivative metaphor where 'alā' is borrowed to mean connection and affiliation, not physical placement.

Checkpoint
What type of metaphor does al-Sawi identify in the phrase 'على مقدمتي'?
A) Explicit metaphor (isti‘āra makniyya)
B) Derivative metaphor (isti‘āra tābi‘iyya)
C) Metonymy (majāz mursal)
D) Personification

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'alā' in 'alā muqaddimatī' is a derivative metaphor conveying connection, not physical superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever read a verse of the Qur'an or a hadith and assumed you understood a small word like 'alā' without considering its deeper rhetorical layers? How does this lesson expose a potential carelessness in your approach to sacred texts? Commit to checking the meaning of prepositions in key texts.

This lesson shows that even a single preposition can carry a borrowed meaning that completely changes the sense of a phrase. Understanding the Term 'شرح' in Al-Sawi's Commentary
Al-Sawi explains the opening line and the term 'شرح'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'شرح' (commentary) is used metaphorically in Al-Sawi's opening line.

Core Idea
The word 'commentary' in kalam is a metaphor—the true explainer is the person, not the book.

Al-Sawi begins his commentary with a poetic line and then immediately clarifies a crucial linguistic point. Before diving into theological arguments, he explains that the term 'شرح' attributed to his book is not literal. This lesson unpacks his analysis of the word's possible meanings and why the attribution is metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening verse and the fa' explanation
First, read the Arabic passage that includes the poetic line and Al-Sawi's note about the letter 'fa' as a connector. Notice how he sets the context.
This poetic line is a reference to differing opinions among scholars.
2
Analyze the three interpretations of 'Sharh'
Al-Sawi gives three possible meanings for 'شرح' when applied to the book: 'explainer', 'possessing explanation', or a verbal noun used as hyperbole. Identify each one in the text.
These three are given as possibilities (إما... أو... أو).
3
Understand the metaphorical attribution
Al-Sawi concludes that all three interpretations involve metaphorical attribution (isnad). The real explainer is the person. Reflect on why this matters for interpreting texts.
This guards against ascribing agency to the book itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا شرح لطيف على مقدمتي المسماة بـ«الخريدة البهية» جرى الخلف أما بعد من كان بادئاً بها خمسُ أقوال وداود أقربُ وكانت له فصل الخطاب وبعده فَقِسْ فَسَحبانُ فكعب فيعربُ وأما الفاء بعدها : فهي رابطة للجواب. قوله : (شرح) : إما بمعنى شارح أو الكلام على حذف مضاف؛ أي : ذو شرح، أو أطلق عليه المعنى المصدري؛ مبالغة كما في : زيد عدل، وعلى كل فالإسناد له مجاز، وإلا فالموضّح والمبيِّن إنَّما هو الشَّخص.
So this is a subtle commentary on my introduction named 'al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya'. The debate has occurred: 'Amma ba'du, whoever begins with it has five sayings, Dawud is nearest, and he has decisive speech, then measure: Sahban and Ka'b are eloquent.' As for the fa' after it, it is a connector for the answer. His saying: (Sharh): either in the sense of 'explainer' or the speech involves an omitted construct, i.e., 'possessing explanation', or the verbal noun meaning is applied to it as hyperbole, as in 'Zayd is justice'. In every case, the attribution to it is metaphorical, otherwise the clarifying and explaining entity is only the person.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the opening line of the commentary. The author first quotes the opening line which includes a poetic verse about differing opinions. Then he explains the word 'شرح' (commentary). He states that 'شرح' can be interpreted in three ways: as an active participle meaning 'explainer', as a phrase with an omitted noun (i.e., 'possessing explanation'), or as a verbal noun used for hyperbole (like calling someone 'justice' itself). In all cases, the attribution of 'شرح' to the book is metaphorical (majaz), because the true explainer is the person, not the book.

Key Terms
شرح
مجاز
إسناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'شرح' here simply means the act of explaining, and the book itself is literally an explainer.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that the attribution is metaphorical; the real explainer is the person (author), not the book.

Checkpoint

A) The attribution of 'شرح' to the commentary is literal.
B) The attribution of 'شرح' to the commentary is metaphorical.
C) 'شرح' means 'explainer' only.
D) The poetic line defines 'شرح'.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi clarifies that the term 'شرح' when applied to a book is metaphorical; the true explainer is the human author.

The Metaphorical Use of 'Latif'
Al-Sawi defines 'latif' and explains its metaphorical use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'latif' is used metaphorically in theological discourse, specifically through metonymy and metaphor.

Core Idea
The word 'latif' takes a metaphorical path to convey divine subtlety.

Al-Sawi's analysis of the word 'latif' reveals how classical scholars unpacked technical terms. Before we can fully grasp the theological nuance, we must see the rhetorical mechanism. This passage shows that 'latif' is not used literally when describing Allah, but through figurative language—either metonymy or metaphor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the three literal meanings
The passage states that 'latif' originally means delicate build, transparent, and small size. Note these before moving to the metaphorical use.
3
Understand the first rhetorical device
The author proposes that it is a metonymy (majaz mursal): the literal qualities imply the effect (ease of understanding). Grasp the cause-effect relation.
4
Understand the second rhetorical device
Alternatively, it is a metaphor (majaz isti'ara): ease of approach is likened to the literal qualities, and 'latif' is derived meaning 'easy of approach'.
5
Summarize the two possibilities
Both interpretations are given as valid. The passage does not privilege one over the other.
This shows the richness of classical rhetorical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لطيف): هو في الأصل يطلق على رقيق القوام، وعلى الشَّفَّاف الذي لا يحجب ما وراءه، وعلى صغير الحجم، والمراد هنا : لازمه، فهو مجاز مرسل من إطلاق الملزوم وإرادة اللازم. ويحتمل أنه مجاز استعارة؛ بأن شبه سهولة الأخذ برقّة القوام، أو الشَّفَّاف، أو صغير الحجم، واستعير اسم المشبه به للمشبَّه، واشتقُ لطيف ؛ بمعنى : سهل المأخذ على طريق التَّبعيَّة .
His saying: (Latif): originally it is applied to delicate build, and to transparent that does not obscure what is behind it, and to small size. What is intended here is its necessary implication, so it is a metonymy (majaz mursal) of applying the cause and intending the effect. And it is possible that it is a metaphorical metaphor (majaz isti'ara), likening ease of acquisition to delicate build, or transparent, or small size, and the name of the likened-to is borrowed for the likener, and derived as 'latif' meaning 'easy of approach' by way of secondary derivational metaphor.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'لطيف' (subtle/delicate). The author lists three literal meanings: delicate build, transparent, and small size. In context, it is used metaphorically. First possibility: it is a metonymy (majaz mursal) where the cause (the literal qualities) is used to imply the effect (ease of understanding). Second possibility: it is a metaphorical metaphor (majaz isti'ara) where the ease of understanding is likened to the literal qualities, and the term 'latif' is derived to mean 'easy of approach' via secondary metaphor.

Key Terms
لطيف
مجاز مرسل
استعارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might take 'لطيف' in its literal sense when applied to Allah.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies it is metaphorical, as theological context requires a figurative interpretation, specifically metonymy or metaphor.

Checkpoint
What type of figurative language does Al-Sawi primarily suggest for the word 'لطيف' in this context?
A) Majaz mursal (metonymy)
B) Majaz isti'ara (metaphor)
C) Both are possible
D) Literal usage

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi explains that 'latif' is used metaphorically in two possible ways: as metonymy or as metaphor, both implying ease of understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the name 'Al-Latif' in your prayers, do you consciously think of its metaphorical meaning—that Allah makes understanding easy for you? Or have you been content with a vague, comfortable feeling? Starting today, how will you intentionally reflect on this meaning every time you hear or say this name?
?
This lesson shows that a single divine attribute (Latif) carries depths of meaning that require careful study. Write down one attribute you will study next.

Understanding 'Ala Muqaddimati' and the Derivation of 'Muqaddima'
Al-Sawi analyzes the metaphor and etymology of two key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the metaphorical use of 'على' in the phrase 'على مقدمتي' and the two possible derivations of the term 'المقدمة' with their implications.

Core Idea
The term 'المقدمة' carries a military metaphor and two valid grammatical derivations.

In this passage, Al-Sawi unpacks the very first words of the text: 'على مقدمتي' (on my introduction). He reveals that the preposition 'على' is used metaphorically, and the word 'مقدمة' itself has a rich history and two possible linguistic origins. This lesson will help you see the depth behind a seemingly simple phrase.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the full Arabic text once without pausing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Analyze the metaphor in 'على'
Identify why Al-Sawi calls this a secondary metaphor (استعارة تبعيَّة). Notice how the preposition 'على' is borrowed to connect the commentary and introduction.
The metaphor likens the relationship to something being above something else.
3
Examine the derivation of 'المقدمة'
The passage gives two possible verbs: the intransitive 'قدم' (to precede) and the transitive 'قدّم' (to present). Notice how each affects the meaning.
The footnote prefers the kasra reading to emphasize merit, not mere placement.
4
Reflect on the preference of kasra
Understand why the author favors the kasra pronunciation: it indicates that the introduction deserves to be advanced because of its qualities.
This connects to the purpose of the book in guiding students.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (على مقدمتي) : في الكلام استعارة تبعيَّة؛ حيث شبه ارتباط الشَّرح بالمقدمة بارتباط مستعل بمُستَعلى عليه، فسرى التشبيه من الكليات إلى الجزئيات، فاستعيرت على الموضوعة للاستعلاء الخاص لمعنى اللَّام على طريق الاستعارة التَّبعيَّة . مقدمة المحشي التي نظمتها في العقائد التوحيدية يوضّح معانيها ويشيد مبانيها، والمقدمة في الأصل : اسم لمقدِّمة الجيش أطلقت على تلك الرسالة ؛ لأنَّ بها يُتَوصَّل إلى معضل كتب التّوحيد، وهي مأخوذة؛ إما من قَدِم اللازم بمعنى : تقدَّم لتقدُّمها على غيرها بسبب سهولتها وجمعها واختصارها، أو من من قدَّم المتعدِّي ؛ لتقديمها الطَّالب الرَّاغب لمعضل الكتب إذا فهمها، وهذا على كسر الدَّال وأمَّا على فتحها ؛ فهي من قدَّم المتعدّي لا غيره، ومعناه : أنَّ الطالب يقدّمها ؛ لما فيها من المزايا . (۱) الكسر أولى من فتحها ؛ لأن الفتح يوهم أن تقديمها بالجعل لا بالاستحقاق، وهو خلاف المقصود هكذا قيل وفيه أن المقدم لها لا يكون إلا من أرباب الاعتبار، فلا يقدم إلا ما يستحق التقديم. «تقريرات بصيلة».
His saying: ('ala muqaddimati): in the speech there is a secondary metaphor, likening the connection of the commentary to the introduction to the connection of something high to something it is upon. So the simile spreads from universals to particulars, and the preposition 'ala, which is placed for particular highness, is borrowed for the meaning of the lam (of purpose) by way of secondary metaphor. The introduction of the commentator, which I composed in the doctrines of tawhid, clarifies its meanings and builds its structures. 'Al-muqaddima' originally is a name for the front of the army; it was applied to that treatise because through it one reaches difficult books of tawhid. It is derived either from the intransitive 'qadima' meaning 'to precede', due to its precedence over others because of its ease, comprehensiveness, and brevity; or from the transitive 'qaddama', due to its presenting the eager student to difficult books if he understands it. This is with kasra (i) of the dal, while with fatha (a) it is from transitive 'qaddama' only, and its meaning is that the student presents it because of the merits it contains. (1) The kasra is preferable to the fatha, because the fatha suggests that its presentation is by placement not by merit, which is contrary to the intent. So it is said. And in it is that the one who presents it is only among those of consideration, so he only presents what deserves presentation. 'Taqrirat Basila'.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'على مقدمتي' (on my introduction) and defines the term 'المقدمة' (the introduction). First, the preposition 'على' is analyzed as a secondary metaphor (isti'ara taba'iyya), likening the relation of commentary to introduction to that of something high over something low. Then, the author defines 'المقدمة'. Originally meaning the front part of an army, it is applied to this treatise because it leads to understanding difficult works on tawhid. Two derivations are given: from the intransitive verb 'قدم' (to precede) because it precedes other works in ease and comprehensiveness, or from the transitive 'قدّم' (to present) because it presents the student to difficult books. The footnote clarifies that the kasra (i) pronunciation is preferred because the fatha (a) would imply mere positioning rather than deserved precedence.

Key Terms
على
مقدمة
استعارة تبعية
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, why is the kasra (i) pronunciation of the dal in 'المقدمة' preferred over the fatha (a)?
A) Kasra indicates the transitive derivation, which is more correct.
B) Fatha suggests that the introduction is presented by placement rather than by merit.
C) Kasra is easier to pronounce.
D) Fatha is used for the front of an army, not for books.

Key Takeaway
The term 'المقدمة' is derived from military terminology and can be understood through two derivations, each revealed by the vowel on the letter dal, with the kasra preferred to emphasize merit over mere positioning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pick up a book on 'aqeedah, do you see it as the vanguard of a battle against ignorance, or just another text to passively read? How does this military metaphor demand active engagement from you?
?
The author insists that an introduction is presented because it deserves merit, not because of arbitrary position. In your own study of Islam, do you approach each book with the expectation that you must earn its benefit through effort, or do you assume it will benefit you simply because you read it?
?
The preference for the kasra derivation over fatha hinges on merit versus placement. Are there areas of your life where you rely on position or convenience rather than earning the right to learn or lead? What would change if you adopted the kasra mindset?

The Definition of Nazm (Composition)
Al-Sawi defines the term 'nazm' as used in the introduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of al-nazm and its specific application to the rajaz meter.

Core Idea
Nazm is intentionally crafted poetic composition in a specific meter.

This lesson examines the first phrase describing the introduction: 'allati nazamtuha' (which I composed). Al-Sawi provides both the lexical origin and the technical meaning of al-nazm, highlighting that it specifically refers to poetic composition in rajaz meter. Understanding this foundation is essential for appreciating the precision of the author's craft.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of nazm
Read both the linguistic and technical definitions in Arabic and translation.
Notice that the linguistic meaning is about threading pearls, implying an orderly arrangement.
2
Note the meter specification
The author specifies the meter as rajaz and gives the foot pattern. Notice the technical detail.
The foot 'mustaf'ilun' repeated six times forms a line of rajaz.
3
Reflect on intentionality
Why does the author emphasize intentionality and meter? This sets apart poetry from prose and shows the care in composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الَّتي نظمتها): النَّظم لغة: إدخال اللآلئ في السلك، واصطلاحاً : هو الكلام المقفّى الموزون قصداً، وهي من (بحر الرجز) وأجزاؤه (مستفعلن) ستَ مرَّاتٍ . قوله : (يوضح معانيها من الإيضاح وهو الكشف والإظهار والمعاني): جمع معنى، وهو ما يعنى ويقصد من اللفظ . قوله : ( ويشيد) عطف على (يوضح) من التّشييد، وهو في الأصل رفع البناء الحسي. و(المباني): جمع مبنى، وهو الألفاظ سمّيت مباني؛ لابتناء المعاني عليها ومن هنا قولهم : الألفاظ قوالب للمعاني.
His saying: (allati nazamtuha): al-nazm linguistically is the insertion of pearls into the string; technically it is intentionally rhymed and metered speech. It is from the meter of rajaz, and its parts are 'mustaf'ilun' six times. His saying: (yuwadihu ma'aniha) from al-idah, which is uncovering and revealing, and al-ma'ani (meanings) is the plural of ma'na, which is what is intended and meant from the utterance. His saying: (wa yushidu) is coordinate with (yuwadihu) from al-tashid, which originally is the raising of physical building. And (al-mabani) is the plural of mabna, which are the utterances, called mabani because meanings are built upon them. Hence their saying: utterances are molds for meanings.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage explains three phrases from the description of the introduction. First, 'التي نظمتها' (which I composed): the author defines 'النظم' (composition) first linguistically as threading pearls, then technically as intentionally rhymed and metered speech, specifying that it is in the meter of rajaz with six 'mustaf'ilun' feet. Second, 'يوضح معانيها' (clarifies its meanings): 'الإيضاح' is defined as uncovering and revealing, and 'المعاني' as what is intended from words. Third, 'يشيد مبانيها' (builds its structures): 'التشييد' originally means raising a physical building, and 'المباني' are the utterances called structures because meanings are built upon them. The author concludes with the classic statement that words are molds for meanings.

Key Terms
نظم
رجز
مستفعلن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'al-nazm' simply means 'arrangement' in a general sense.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that technically it means specifically rhymed and metered speech, not just any ordered text.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, what is the technical definition of 'al-nazm'?
A) The insertion of pearls into a string.
B) Intentionally rhymed and metered speech.
C) Any speech that is well-arranged.
D) Speech that is easy to memorize.

Key Takeaway
Al-nazm, in its technical sense, is intentional poetic composition in a specific meter, not mere arrangement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author carefully defines 'nazm' as intentional composition. Are your own words deliberately crafted, or do you speak without thought, like scattered pearls? Will you commit today to pausing before speaking, ensuring each word is both true and necessary?
?
This passage distinguishes technical from linguistic meaning. In your faith, do you know the precise definitions of key terms like faith (iman), worship (ibada), or tawheed? Or do you use them loosely? Will you take one term this week and learn its exact meaning?
?
The author insists on a specific meter and rhyme. Do you pay attention to the structure of your speech, even in casual conversation? Will you strive to make your words both beautiful and intentional, as an act of worship?

The Building of Structures: 'Yushidu Mabaniha'
Al-Sawi defines the terms 'tashid' and 'mabani' as applied to the introduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the metaphor of building as applied to speech, where utterances are structures supporting meanings.

Core Idea
Words are the scaffolding on which meanings are built.

The final phrase is 'yushidu mabaniha' (builds its structures). Al-Sawi explains 'tashid' as raising a physical building, and 'mabani' as utterances that form the foundation for meanings. This lesson unpacks the architectural metaphor and the famous saying that words are molds for meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of tashid and mabani
Note the shift from physical building to linguistic structure.
2
Understand the relationship
Meanings are built upon utterances, so utterances are called mabani (structures).
3
Reflect on the concluding aphorism
‘Al-alfaz qawalib li al-ma’ani’ (utterances are molds for meanings). How does this reinforce the building metaphor?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الَّتي نظمتها): النَّظم لغة: إدخال اللآلئ في السلك، واصطلاحاً : هو الكلام المقفّى الموزون قصداً، وهي من (بحر الرجز) وأجزاؤه (مستفعلن) ستَ مرَّاتٍ . قوله : (يوضح معانيها من الإيضاح وهو الكشف والإظهار والمعاني): جمع معنى، وهو ما يعنى ويقصد من اللفظ . قوله : ( ويشيد) عطف على (يوضح) من التّشييد، وهو في الأصل رفع البناء الحسي. و(المباني): جمع مبنى، وهو الألفاظ سمّيت مباني؛ لابتناء المعاني عليها ومن هنا قولهم : الألفاظ قوالب للمعاني.
His saying: (allati nazamtuha): al-nazm linguistically is the insertion of pearls into the string; technically it is intentionally rhymed and metered speech. It is from the meter of rajaz, and its parts are 'mustaf'ilun' six times. His saying: (yuwadihu ma'aniha) from al-idah, which is uncovering and revealing, and al-ma'ani (meanings) is the plural of ma'na, which is what is intended and meant from the utterance. His saying: (wa yushidu) is coordinate with (yuwadihu) from al-tashid, which originally is the raising of physical building. And (al-mabani) is the plural of mabna, which are the utterances, called mabani because meanings are built upon them. Hence their saying: utterances are molds for meanings.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage explains three phrases from the description of the introduction. First, 'التي نظمتها' (which I composed): the author defines 'النظم' (composition) first linguistically as threading pearls, then technically as intentionally rhymed and metered speech, specifying that it is in the meter of rajaz with six 'mustaf'ilun' feet. Second, 'يوضح معانيها' (clarifies its meanings): 'الإيضاح' is defined as uncovering and revealing, and 'المعاني' as what is intended from words. Third, 'يشيد مبانيها' (builds its structures): 'التشييد' originally means raising a physical building, and 'المباني' are the utterances called structures because meanings are built upon them. The author concludes with the classic statement that words are molds for meanings.

Key Terms

مباني
مبنى
قوالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that words and meanings are independent and that 'building' is just a metaphor with no practical implication.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that meanings rely on words for their expression, and words are designed to carry specific meanings, hence the architectural and mold metaphors.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are long and complex.
B) Because meanings are built upon them.

Key Takeaway
Utterances are the structures upon which meanings are built, and they serve as molds for those meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Al-Sawi's Definitions of Key Terms
Al-Sawi defines muqaddima and nazm, and explains the opening line of his poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key terms muqaddima, nazm, iydah, tashyid, and mabani as al-Sawi defines them, and grasp why the pronunciation of muqaddima matters.

Core Idea
A muqaddima is not just a preface; it's the key that unlocks difficult books of tawhid.

In this lesson, al-Sawi begins his commentary by defining the very words he uses in his opening line. He shows that even a seemingly simple term like 'muqaddima' carries deep meaning, drawing from military strategy and linguistic derivation. Understanding these definitions is essential for following the rest of the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation to get the gist.
Notice the footnotes in brackets; they are part of the text.
2
Analyze the definition of muqaddima
Identify the two linguistic derivations and why the author prefers kasr over fatha. Ask yourself: how is this treatise like a vanguard?
The derivation affects the meaning.
3
Understand the technical terms
Go through each term: nazm, iydah, tashyid, mabani. Note how each is defined linguistically and technically.
These terms are foundational for the commentary.
4
Reflect on the footnote
Consider the argument about kasr vs fatha. What does the author mean by 'advancement by merit'?
This shows the precision of kalam scholars.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المحشي التي نظمتها في العقائد التوحيدية يوضّح معانيها ويشيد مبانيها، والمقدمة في الأصل : اسم لمقدِّمة الجيش أطلقت على تلك الرسالة ؛ لأنَّ بها يُتَوصَّل إلى معضل كتب التّوحيد، وهي مأخوذة؛ إما من قَدِم اللازم بمعنى : تقدَّم لتقدُّمها على غيرها بسبب سهولتها وجمعها واختصارها، أو من قدَّم المتعدِّي ؛ لتقديمها الطَّالب الرَّاغب لمعضل الكتب إذا فهمها، وهذا على كسر الدَّال وأمَّا على فتحها ؛ فهي من قدَّم المتعدّي لا غيره، ومعناه : أنَّ الطالب يقدّمها ؛ لما فيها من المزايا . قوله : (الَّتي نظمتها): النَّظم لغة: إدخال اللآلئ في السلك، واصطلاحاً : هو الكلام المقفّى الموزون قصداً، وهي من (بحر الرجز) وأجزاؤه (مستفعلن) ستَ مرَّاتٍ . قوله : (يوضح معانيها من الإيضاح وهو الكشف والإظهار والمعاني): جمع معنى، وهو ما يعنى ويقصد من اللفظ . قوله : ( ويشيد) عطف على (يوضح) من التّشييد، وهو في الأصل رفع البناء الحسي. و(المباني): جمع مبنى، وهو الألفاظ سمّيت مباني؛ لابتناء المعاني عليها ومن هنا قولهم : الألفاظ قوالب للمعاني . (۱) الكسر أولى من فتحها ؛ لأن الفتح يوهم أن تقديمها بالجعل لا بالاستحقاق، وهو خلاف المقصود هكذا قيل وفيه أن المقدم لها لا يكون إلا من أرباب الاعتبار، فلا يقدم إلا ما يستحق التقديم. «تقريرات بصيلة».
The introduction of the commentator which I composed on the doctrines of tawhid clarifies its meanings and raises its structures. Al-muqaddima originally is a name for the vanguard of an army, applied to this treatise because by it one reaches difficult books of tawhid. It is derived either from qadima al-lazim meaning 'to precede' because of its precedence over others due to its ease, comprehensiveness, and brevity, or from qaddama al-muta'addi because it advances the eager student to difficult books when he understands it. This is with kasr of the dal; as for with fatha, it is from qaddama al-muta'addi only, meaning that the student advances it because of the merits it contains. His saying: (allati nazamtuha): al-nazm linguistically is inserting pearls into a string, and terminologically is speech that is rhymed and metered intentionally, and it is from the rajaz meter, its parts being 'mustaf'ilun' six times. His saying: (yuwaddihu ma'aniha) from al-iydah which is unveiling and manifestation; al-ma'ani is plural of ma'na, which is what is intended and meant by the utterance. His saying: (wa yushayyidu) is conjoined to (yuwaddihu) from al-tashyid, which originally is the raising of a physical building. (al-mabani) is plural of mabna, which are utterances; they are called mabani because meanings are built upon them, hence their saying: utterances are molds for meanings. (1) The kasr is preferable to the fatha, because the fatha suggests that its advancement is by appointment not by merit, which is contrary to the intended meaning; it is said thus, and therein that the one who advances it is only among people of consideration, so he only advances what deserves advancement. 'Taqrirat Basila'.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of key terms in the title of al-Sawi's commentary. The author begins by identifying the work as 'the introduction of the commentator' which he composed on the doctrines of tawhid, and states that this poem (nazm) clarifies the meanings and raises the structures. He then defines al-muqaddima: originally meaning the vanguard of an army, it is applied to this treatise because, like a vanguard, it enables access to difficult books of tawhid. He gives two possible derivations: from the intransitive qadima meaning 'to precede' (with kasr of the dal), because of its ease and comprehensiveness, or from the transitive qaddama meaning 'to advance' (with fatha), because it advances the eager student. He notes that with fatha it is only from the transitive verb, implying the student advances it for its merits. He then explains 'nazamtu': al-nazm linguistically is stringing pearls, and as a term is deliberately rhymed and metered speech; he specifies the meter as rajaz with six 'mustaf'ilun' feet. Next he explains 'yuwaddih' from al-iydah (unveiling), and 'ma'aniha' as plural of ma'na (intended meaning). For 'yushayyid', he notes it is conjoined to 'yuwaddih' and comes from al-tashyid, originally 'raising a physical building'. He defines 'mabani' as utterances, called such because meanings are built upon them, citing the maxim 'utterances are molds for meanings'. Finally, a footnote argues that the kasr pronunciation is preferable because the fatha would wrongly suggest that the muqaddima is advanced by appointment rather than by merit. The note also references a source 'Taqrirat Basila'.

مقدمة (muqaddima)
نظم (nazm)
إيضاح (iydah)
تشييد (tashyid)
مباني (mabani)
معنى (ma'na)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'muqaddima' just means 'preface'.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows it is a key to unlock difficult books, derived from the military vanguard concept.

A) Because it is the first thing one writes
B) Because it enables access to difficult books of tawhid
C) Because it is short and simple
D) Because it is composed in verse

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi's careful definitions show that every term in the opening line is chosen deliberately to orient the student toward the purpose of the commentary.

Al-Sawi's Method: Avoiding Defective Brevity and Tedious Length
Al-Sawi explains his balanced approach between conciseness and elaboration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Sawi's balanced approach to brevity and length in his commentary and how he resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Al-Sawi's commentary is concise where needed, elaborate where needed — never defective, never tedious.

Al-Sawi explains his methodology for writing his gloss. He avoids two extremes: brevity that omits essential meaning, and length that wearies the reader. But this raises a puzzle: how can a book be both concise and lengthy? In this passage, al-Sawi clarifies his principle and resolves the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the terms ikhtiṣār and taṭwīl.
2
Identify the two extremes
Find where al-Sawi states what he avoided: ikhtiṣār mukhilla and taṭwīl mumill.
3
Find the apparent contradiction
The passage implies the book is both concise and lengthy, which seem opposites. Locate this statement.
This is the crux of the lesson.
4
Understand the resolution
Read al-Sawi's answer: brevity and length are used in different places according to context.
This shows a balanced methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية الصاوي على شرح الخريدة البهية
اجتنبت فيه الاختصار المُخلَّ ، وأعرضت فيه عن التطويل المُمِل، والمراد بـ (التشييد) هنا : تصحيح الألفاظ وتحسينها، بتنزيلها على القواعد العربية، فشبهت الألفاظ المخصوصة من حيث افتقارها لمن ينزلها على القواعد العربية ببيت محتاج للرفع وسد الخلل، وطوى ذكر المشبه به، ورمز له بشيء من لوازمه، وهو التشييد على طريق الاستعارة بالكناية. والتشييد تخييل، وإسناد التوضيح والتشييد للشَّرح مجاز عقلي، وحقه أن يسند للمؤلّف .
قوله : (اجتنبت) : أي : تباعدت.
قوله : (الاختصار) : هو في الأصل تقليل اللفظ، كثر المعنى أم لا قوله : (المخل) : أي : المضيّع للمعنى، فالاجتناب منصب على القيد، وإِلَّا فأصل الاختصار حاصل .
قوله : (وأعرضت) معطوف على (اجتنبت)، وهو بمعنى : الاجتناب وغاير؛ تفتناً.
و(التَّطويل): ضدُّ الاختصار.
وقوله : (الممل) : أي : الموقع في الملل ؛ وهو السَّآمة، فالإعراض منصب على القيد، ومقتضى هذه العبارة أنَّ كتابه هذا مختصر غير مخل، ومطوّل غير ممل، وهما ضدان لا يجتمعان.
والجواب : أنَّ الاختصار في مواضع والتطويل في مواضع على حسب ما يقتضيه المقام في كلِّ
Al-Sawi's gloss on the commentary of the Radiant Pearl: I avoided in it brevity that is defective, and I turned away from tedious length. And what is meant by (al-tashyid) here is correction and improvement of words, by applying them to Arabic rules. So the specific words, due to their need for someone to apply them to Arabic rules, were likened to a house needing raising and filling of gaps. He omitted the mention of the likened object and alluded to it with one of its necessities, which is al-tashyid, in the way of isti'āra bil-kināya. Al-tashyid is takhyīl, and ascription of al-tawḍīḥ and al-tashyid to the commentary is a rational metaphor; its proper object is the author. His saying: (ijtanabtu) i.e., I kept away. His saying: (al-ikhtiṣār) originally is reduction of wording, whether meaning is plentiful or not. His saying: (al-mukhilla) i.e., that which wastes the meaning; so avoidance is directed at the qualification, otherwise the basic brevity is achieved. His saying: (wa aʿraḍtu) is conjoined to (ijtanabtu) and means the same as avoidance, but they differ for stylistic variety. (al-taṭwīl) is the opposite of brevity. His saying: (al-mumill) i.e., that which causes boredom; it is weariness. So turning away is directed at the qualification. The implication of this statement is that this book is concise but not defective, and lengthy but not tedious, and these are two opposites that do not come together. The answer: that brevity is in some places and length is in some places, according to what the context requires in each.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of al-Sawi's methodological statement. It begins with a parenthetical remark clarifying the meaning of 'al-tashyid' (raising/improving) in this context: it means correcting and improving words by applying them to Arabic grammatical rules. Al-Sawi uses an analogy: specific words, needing someone to apply them to rules, are like a house needing repair; he employs isti'āra bil-kināya (allusive metaphor) by mentioning the effect (tashyid) instead of the cause. He notes that ascribing clarification and raising to the commentary itself is a rational metaphor (majāz ʿaqlī); properly it should be ascribed to the author. Then he proceeds to explain each word of the poem line: 'ijtanabtu' means 'I kept away'; 'al-ikhtiṣār' originally means reduction of wording regardless of meaning; 'al-mukhilla' means 'that which wastes the meaning' — so the avoidance is only of defective brevity, not brevity per se. Similarly, 'aʿraḍtu' (I turned away) is a synonym for 'ijtanabtu' used for stylistic variety. 'Al-taṭwīl' is the opposite of brevity; 'al-mumill' means 'that which causes boredom' — so the turning away is from tedious length. This implies the book is concise but not defective, and lengthy but not tedious — two opposites that seem contradictory. Al-Sawi resolves the apparent contradiction by stating that brevity is used in some places and length in others, each according to the need of the context.

اختصار (ikhtiṣār)
تطويل (taṭwīl)
مخل (mukhilla)
ممل (mumill)
اجتناب (ijtināb)
إعراض (iʿrāḍ)
تشييد (tashyīd)
استعارة بالكناية (istiʿāra bil-kināya)
مجاز عقلي (majāz ʿaqlī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Sawi contradicts himself by claiming his book is both concise and lengthy.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that brevity and length are applied in different places according to what the context requires, so there is no contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does al-Sawi resolve the apparent contradiction of having both brevity and length in his book?
A) He says it is impossible and admits fault
B) He says brevity is in one section and length in another
C) He says that brevity and length are used in different places according to what the context demands
D) He redefines brevity as length

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi's method is to adapt his style to the context, avoiding both extreme brevity and tedious length.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which extreme are you more prone to, and how has that affected others' understanding? Al-Sawi deliberately avoided both. Do you speak with precision and reflect on the impact of your language, or do you fall into careless patterns? What step can you take today to be more deliberate in your speech?
?
The apparent contradiction of being both concise and lengthy was resolved by considering context. Understanding al-Tashyid as a Metaphorical Term
The commentator defines al-tashyid and explains its metaphorical usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'al-tashyid' in this context, its metaphorical construction, and the rhetorical concepts of isti'ara bil-kinaya, takhayyul, and majaz 'aqli.

Core Idea
Al-tashyid is not literal strengthening; it is fixing expressions by applying grammar rules.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks a key term that appears in the commentary tradition. He explains that 'al-tashyid' here does not mean physical construction but rather the correction and beautification of expressions by fitting them to Arabic grammatical rules. He then illustrates this with a vivid metaphor and introduces related rhetorical concepts. This lesson will help you see how even technical terms can carry layers of meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic text once. Notice the key terms: التشييد, الاستعارة بالكناية, التخييل, المجاز العقلي. Then read the English translation to confirm your understanding.
The passage is dense with rhetorical terminology; take your time.
2
Understand the definition of al-tashyid
The commentator clearly defines 'al-tashyid' here as 'correcting expressions and improving them by placing them upon the Arabic grammatical rules.' This is different from its literal meaning of strengthening a building.
This definition is the starting point for the whole discussion.
3
Analyze the simile and the metaphor
The commentator says expressions are like a house needing repair. He explains that the author omitted the thing being compared (the expressions) and only mentioned the necessary concomitant (tashyid). This is 'isti'ara bil-kinaya' (metaphor by allusion). Identify the parts of this figure of speech.
Think of how a metaphor works: one thing is compared to another without using 'like'.
4
Identify the two other rhetorical devices
The passage mentions 'takhayyul' (fictionalization) as a characteristic of the metaphor, and 'majaz 'aqli' (rational metaphor) when ascribing clarification and strengthening to the commentary itself rather than the author. Understand why the latter is a metaphor: because the commentary is not the actual agent.
These terms are common in Arabic rhetoric.
5
Reflect on the overall purpose
Ask yourself: Why did the commentator explain this in detail? Because understanding such rhetorical devices is essential for correctly interpreting classical texts. This passage also shows how scholars used metaphorical language to explain technical concepts.
Consider the importance of precise terminology in Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بـ (التشييد) هنا : تصحيح الألفاظ وتحسينها، بتنزيلها على القواعد العربية، فشبهت الألفاظ المخصوصة من حيث افتقارها لمن ينزلها على القواعد العربية ببيت محتاج للرفع وسد الخلل، وطوى ذكر المشبه به، ورمز له بشيء من لوازمه، وهو التشييد على طريق الاستعارة بالكناية. والتشييد تخييل، وإسناد التوضيح والتشييد للشَّرح مجاز عقلي، وحقه أن يسند للمؤلّف .
And what is meant by (al-tashyid) here: correcting expressions and improving them, by placing them upon the Arabic grammatical rules. So the specific expressions are likened, in terms of their need for someone to place them upon the Arabic rules, to a house in need of raising and repairing the breach. And he omitted mentioning the thing likened to, and alluded to it by something from its necessary concomitants, which is al-tashyid, by way of isti'ara bil-kinaya. And al-tashyid is a fictionalization (takhayyul). And ascribing elucidation (al-tawdih) and al-tashyid to the commentary (al-sharh) is a rational metaphor (majaz 'aqli), while the proper thing is that it be ascribed to the author.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'al-tashyid'. The commentator clarifies that 'al-tashyid' here does not mean literal physical strengthening but rather the act of correcting and improving expressions by fitting them to Arabic grammatical rules. He then presents a metaphorical comparison: the expressions are like a house that needs to be raised and its cracks repaired — that is, they need someone to set them correctly. The author of the original (the matn) omitted explicitly stating the thing being compared (the expressions) and instead alluded to it through its necessary concomitant, which is 'al-tashyid' itself. This rhetorical device is isti'ara bil-kinaya (metaphor by allusion). The commentator further notes that 'al-tashyid' is a takhayyul (fictionalization), and that attributing clarification and strengthening to the commentary (sharh) is a rational metaphor (majaz 'aqli), because the true agent is the author, not the book.

Key Terms
التشييد
الاستعارة بالكناية
التخييل
المجاز العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'al-tashyid' means literal construction or physical strengthening.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that here it means 'correcting and improving expressions by placing them upon the Arabic grammatical rules.' The metaphor uses a building to represent expressions needing correction, but the term itself refers to the action of grammatical correction.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'al-tashyid' in this passage?
A) Physical strengthening of a building
B) Correcting and improving expressions by applying Arabic rules
C) Omitting unnecessary words
D) Introducing new arguments

Key Takeaway
Al-tashyid in this context is a rhetorical term meaning the correction of expressions by applying Arabic grammatical rules, and it is used metaphorically via isti'ara bil-kinaya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator mentions that ascribing clarification to the commentary is a 'rational metaphor'—the true agent is the author, not the text. Do you ever confuse the tool (the book, the teacher) with the source of truth? How can you train yourself to look through the means to the actual Author and His intent?
?
Al-tashyid is a metaphorical repair of expressions by grammar rules. Is your own inner speech—your thoughts and beliefs about Allah—built on firm grammatical foundations of sound ʿAqīdah? Which 'expression' in your heart needs 'raising'? Commit to one concrete step to fix it today.

Avoiding Defective Brevity and Tedious Length
Commentator explains key terms and resolves contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author balances conciseness and expansion in his writing by avoiding defective brevity and tedious length according to context.

Core Idea
A good scholar varies his style according to context, not fixed brevity or length.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the author's claim that his book avoids both defective brevity and tedious length. At first glance, these seem contradictory—how can a book be both concise and lengthy? The commentator clarifies that the avoidance is not of brevity or length per se, but of their defective forms, and explains that the author adapts his style to the needs of each topic. Let's explore how he resolves this apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the definitions of key terms like ikhtisar and tatwil.
The passage is from a commentary on the author's introduction.
2
Identify the apparent contradiction
The author claims his book avoids both defective brevity and tedious length, yet brevity and length are opposites. How can a book be both?
The contradiction is central to understanding the author's methodology.
3
Understand the resolution
The commentator explains that the author uses conciseness in some places and expansion in others, according to the context. So the book as a whole is not uniformly one or the other.
This shows that the qualification 'defective' and 'tedious' are the real targets of avoidance.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why an author would vary his style. This approach ensures clarity and avoids extremes. Think about how this applies to other writings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (اجتنبت) : أي : تباعدت.
قوله : (الاختصار) : هو في الأصل تقليل اللفظ، كثر المعنى أم لا قوله : (المخل) : أي : المضيّع للمعنى، فالاجتناب منصب على القيد، وإِلَّا فأصل الاختصار حاصل .
قوله : (وأعرضت) معطوف على (اجتنبت)، وهو بمعنى : الاجتناب وغاير؛ تفتناً.
.
و(التَّطويل): ضدُّ الاختصار.
وقوله : (الممل) : أي : الموقع في الملل ؛ وهو السَّآمة، فالإعراض منصب على القيد، ومقتضى هذه العبارة أنَّ كتابه هذا مختصر غير مخل،
ومطوّل غير ممل، وهما ضدان لا يجتمعان.
والجواب : أنَّ الاختصار في مواضع والتطويل في مواضع على
حسب ما يقتضيه المقام في كلِّ
His saying: (I avoided) means: I kept away. His saying: (abridgement, ikhtisar) is originally the reduction of wording, whether the meaning is much or not. His saying: (the defective, al-mukhill) means: that which wastes the meaning. So the avoidance is directed at the qualification; otherwise the basic abridgement is achieved. His saying: (and I turned away from, wa a'radtu) is coordinated with 'I avoided', and it is in the sense of avoidance, and he varied the wording for elegance. And (prolixity, al-tatwil) is the opposite of abridgement. His saying: (the tedious, al-mumill) means: that which causes boredom, which is weariness. So the turning away is directed at the qualification. And the implication of this expression is that this book of his is brief but not defective, and lengthy but not tedious, yet they are two opposites that do not combine. The answer: that abridgement is in some places and prolixity is in other places, according to what the context demands in every case.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a running commentary on the author's statement about his methodology. The commentator defines each key term: 'ajtanabtu' (I avoided), 'ikhtisar' (abridgement), 'al-mukhill' (defective, i.e., causing loss of meaning), 'a'radtu' (I turned away from), 'al-tatwil' (prolixity), and 'al-mumill' (tedious, causing boredom). He observes that the avoidance is specifically directed at the qualifying word 'defective' and 'tedious', not at brevity or length per se. Then he raises an apparent contradiction: if the book is both concise and lengthy, these are opposites that cannot coexist. The answer resolves the contradiction: the author employed concision in some sections and expansion in others, adapting to the demands of each topic. Thus the book as a whole is not uniformly one or the other.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A book cannot be both concise and lengthy because they are opposites.
Correction: The author uses conciseness in some places and length in others according to context, so the book as a whole is not uniformly one or the other.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the apparent contradiction between the book being both concise and lengthy?
A) He denies that the book is lengthy
B) He says brevity and length are not opposites
C) He explains that concision is used in some places and length in others as needed
D) He redefines the terms

Key Takeaway
The author skillfully combines brevity and expansion by adapting to the demands of each topic, avoiding both defective conciseness and tedious prolixity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily conversations, do you tend to be too brief (leaving meaning unclear) or too lengthy (causing boredom)? Which one are you more guilty of, and what is one specific change you can make today to imitate the author's balanced approach?
?
The commentator points out that the author avoids defective brevity and tedious length, but uses both where appropriate. Think of a recent interaction where you either wasted words or failed to say enough. How could you have better adapted to the context, and will you commit to doing so next time?
?
The author's method requires self-awareness and sensitivity to the listener. On the Day of Judgment, every word you speak will be accounted for. Are you conscious of the weight of your words, or do you speak carelessly? What concrete habit can you adopt to ensure your speech is neither defective nor tedious?

Proofs and Beneficial Additions
Commentator explains definitions of proofs and benefits and their role in increasing certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's clarification of the terms 'proofs' and 'benefits' and how they relate to certainty in theological discourse.

Core Idea
Proofs build certainty; benefits strengthen it.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the author's concise claim about confining himself to proofs and benefits. He defines key terms, broadens the understanding of 'proof' beyond its strict rational sense, and explains how benefits increase certainty. This methodological note sets the stage for the rest of the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice the grammatical connections and definitions the commentator provides.
The passage begins by linking the phrase 'waqtasartu' to 'ijtanabtu' from earlier.
2
Understand the definition of burhān
The commentator defines 'burhān' as evidence (dalīl) in general, including both rational and transmitted proofs, even though its original meaning is rational proof. This is a key broadening.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أو نقلياً' – transmitted evidence is included.
3
Grasp the meaning of fawā'id
'Benefits' are explained as scholarly issues added after the proof to clarify it further, such as citing transmitted evidences after rational ones.
The commentator distinguishes this from the general meaning of 'benefit' as worldly or otherworldly goods.
4
Connect proofs and certainty
Certainty (yaqīn) is defined as definitive conviction (jazm) regarding beliefs. Proofs provide the foundation of certainty, and benefits increase it.
The phrase 'يزداد اليقين' indicates that certainty can be strengthened.
5
Note the eight attributes
The commentator mentions that the original commentary (al-Kharidat) was described with eight attributes, of which 'latīf' (subtle) is the first and 'with the benefits' is the last.
These attributes are distinct perfections that attract the reader.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (واقتصرت) معطوف على (اجتنبت) والمعنى: جعلت عباراتي مقصورة.
وقوله : ( على تحرير البراهين) أي تخليصها وتبيينها من غير أن أذكر شيئاً (۱) زيادةً عليها . والبراهين جمع برهان والمراد به : الدليل عقليّاً كان أو نقليّاً، وإن كان البرهان في الأصل اسماً للدليل العقلي.
قوله : (مع الفوائد): ظرف متعلّق بمحذوف حال من البراهين؛ أي: حال كون البراهين مصاحبةً للفوائد ... إلخ .
والفوائد: جمع فائدة وهي في الأصل ما استفاده الشَّخص من خيرات الدنيا والآخرة، والمراد بها هنا خصوص المسائل العلمية التي تزاد بعد البرهان زيادةً في إيضاحه ؛ كذكر الأدلة النقلية بعد ذكر البراهين العقلية مثلاً .
قوله : (الَّتي بها يزداد اليقين): صفة لـ(الفوائد)، والمراد باليقين : الجزم بالعقائد، فأصل اليقين يحصل بالبراهين وزيادته بتلك الفوائد، وقد وصف هذا الشرح بأوصاف ثمانية :
أولها : قوله : (لطيف)، وآخرها : قوله : (مع الفوائد)، وكلُّها كمالات متغايرة، تحمل الرّاغب على الاعتناء به
(۱) في المطبوع: (شبهاً).
His saying: (and I confined myself) is coordinated with 'I avoided', and the meaning: I made my expressions restricted. His saying: (to setting forth the proofs, al-barāhīn) means extracting them and clarifying them without mentioning anything (1) additional to them. And al-barāhīn is plural of burhān, and what is meant by it is evidence (dalīl), whether rational or transmitted, even though burhān originally is a name for rational evidence. His saying: (with the benefits, al-fawā'id): a circumstantial adverb modifying 'the proofs', meaning the proofs being accompanied by the benefits ... etc. And al-fawā'id is plural of fā'idah, which originally is what a person uses of the good things of this world and the next, and what is meant by it here is specifically scholarly issues that are added after the proof as an increase in its clarification, such as mentioning transmitted evidences after mentioning rational proofs for example. His saying: (by which certainty increases) is a description of 'the benefits', and what is meant by certainty (al-yaqīn) is definitive conviction (al-jazm) regarding beliefs. So the foundation of certainty is attained by the proofs, and its increase is attained by those benefits. And this commentary has been described with eight descriptions: the first of them is his saying: (subtle, latīf), and the last of them is his saying: (with the benefits), and all of them are distinct perfections that impel the desirous to pay attention to it. (1) In the printed edition: (shabahan).
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's claim that he confined himself to setting forth the proofs along with benefits that increase certainty. The commentator first clarifies grammatical coordination, then defines 'burhan' (proof) as evidence in general (dalil), which includes both rational and transmitted proofs, even though the term 'burhan' originally referred specifically to rational proof. He then explains 'fawa'id' (benefits): originally meaning worldly or otherworldly goods, but here it means scholarly points that further clarify the proof, such as adducing transmitted evidence after rational evidence. Certainty (yaqin) is defined as definitive conviction (jazm) in beliefs; the proofs provide the foundation of certainty, and the benefits increase it. Finally, the commentator notes that the original commentary (al-Kharidat) was described with eight attributes, of which 'latif' (subtle) is the first and 'with the benefits' is the last, all being distinct perfections that attract the reader. A textual variant in the printed edition is noted for the word 'shay'an' (something) as 'shabahan' (a likeness).

الفوائد
اليقين
الجزم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'burhān' only ever means rational proof, never transmitted evidence.
Correction: In this context, the commentator explicitly states that 'burhān' here means evidence (dalīl) in general, whether rational or transmitted, even though originally it referred specifically to rational evidence.

Checkpoint

B) Rational arguments supporting the proofs

Key Takeaway
Proofs provide the foundation of certainty, while beneficial additions deepen and strengthen it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you truly feel a definitive conviction about it, or is there hesitation? What specific 'benefits' — deeper verses, scholarly insights, or reflective practices — could you pursue this week to strengthen that certainty? Write down one actionable step.
?
The commentator notes that the work has eight distinct perfections, including subtlety and benefits. How many of these perfections do you actively look for when you study Islamic texts? Could it be that your learning is shallow because you only skim for main ideas? What would change if you started hunting for these perfections in every lesson you study? Commit to doing this for your next three study sessions.
?
This passage defines burhan broadly to include both rational and transmitted evidence. Yet many debates among Muslims pit reason against revelation. Where in your own life have you given priority to one over the other, perhaps neglecting a proof because it seemed purely rational or purely scriptural? How can you ensure you embrace both as gifts that together build your certainty?

Al-Sawi's Introduction: Proofs, Benefits, and Eight Descriptions
Al-Sawi explains the author's phrase about proofs and benefits, and lists eight descriptions of the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of key terms (proof, benefit, certainty) in Al-Sawi's introduction and the eight descriptions that characterize the commentary.

Core Idea
Proofs give foundation of certainty; benefits increase it; the commentary has eight perfections.

Al-Sawi begins his commentary by clarifying the author's method. He explains that the author restricted himself to editing proofs and adding benefits that increase certainty. This passage defines these terms and tells us that the commentary is described by eight distinct perfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of Al-Sawi's explanation.
Notice how Al-Sawi parses the author's sentence word by word.
2
Define key terms
Identify the meanings of 'برهان' (proof), 'فائدة' (benefit), and 'يقين' (certainty) as explained by Al-Sawi.
Note that 'برهان' includes both rational and transmitted evidence.
3
Understand the relationship
Recognize that proofs provide the foundation of certainty, and benefits increase that certainty.
This is the purpose of the commentary.
4
Note the eight descriptions
The commentary has eight descriptions; the first is 'لطيف' (subtle) and the last is 'مع الفوائد' (with benefits).
They are distinct perfections that attract the reader.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why Al-Sawi highlights these descriptions.
They encourage the student to engage with the commentary.

Source Text

واقتصرت فيه على تحرير البراهين مع الفوائد التي بها يزداد اليقين.
قوله : (واقتصرت) معطوف على (اجتنبت) والمعنى: جعلت عباراتي مقصورة.
وقوله : ( على تحرير البراهين) أي تخليصها وتبيينها من غير أن أذكر شيئاً زيادةً عليها . والبراهين جمع برهان والمراد به : الدليل عقليّاً كان أو نقليّاً، وإن كان البرهان في الأصل اسماً للدليل العقلي.
قوله : (مع الفوائد): ظرف متعلّق بمحذوف حال من البراهين؛ أي: حال كون البراهين مصاحبةً للفوائد ... إلخ .
والفوائد: جمع فائدة وهي في الأصل ما استفاده الشَّخص من خيرات الدنيا والآخرة، والمراد بها هنا خصوص المسائل العلمية التي تزاد بعد البرهان زيادةً في إيضاحه ؛ كذكر الأدلة النقلية بعد ذكر البراهين العقلية مثلاً .
قوله : (الَّتي بها يزداد اليقين): صفة لـ(الفوائد)، والمراد باليقين : الجزم بالعقائد، فأصل اليقين يحصل بالبراهين وزيادته بتلك الفوائد، وقد وصف هذا الشرح بأوصاف ثمانية :
أولها : قوله : (لطيف)، وآخرها : قوله : (مع الفوائد)، وكلُّها كمالات متغايرة، تحمل الرّاغب على الاعتناء به
Introduction of the Commentator
And I restricted myself therein to editing the proofs along with the benefits by which certainty increases.
His statement: (And I restricted myself) is conjoined to (I avoided) and the meaning: I made my expressions confined.
His statement: (to editing the proofs) i.e., purifying them and clarifying them without mentioning anything additional to them. And 'proofs' (البراهين) is plural of 'proof' (برهان) and what is intended by it: the evidence, whether rational or transmitted, even though 'proof' originally is a name for rational evidence.
His statement: (along with the benefits): a circumstantial adverb attached to a deleted word as a state of the proofs; i.e., the proofs being accompanied by benefits ... etc.
And 'benefits' (الفوائد) is plural of 'benefit' (فائدة) and it originally means what a person acquires of the goods of this world and the hereafter. What is intended by it here is specifically the scholarly issues that are added after the proof as an increase in its clarification; such as mentioning transmitted evidences after mentioning rational proofs, for example.
His statement: (by which certainty increases): a description of (the benefits). And what is intended by certainty (اليقين): decisive belief in the tenets of faith. So the foundation of certainty is obtained by proofs, and its increase by those benefits. And this commentary has been described with eight descriptions:
First: his statement: (subtle), and last: his statement: (along with the benefits), and all of them are distinct perfections that encourage the desirous to pay attention to it.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the author's own introductory statement. It begins by defining the key terms: "restricted myself" means the author confined his expressions, "editing the proofs" means purifying and clarifying them without adding anything extra. The word "proofs" (البراهين) is defined as evidence, whether rational or transmitted, though originally it refers to rational evidence. Then "benefits" (الفوائد) are defined as scholarly points that increase clarity, such as adding transmitted proofs after rational ones. "Certainty" (اليقين) is defined as decisive belief in doctrines; its foundation comes from proofs, and its increase comes from these benefits. Finally, the passage states that this commentary has eight descriptions, mentioning the first ("subtle") and last ("along with the benefits") as examples, and notes that all are distinct perfections that attract the reader.

براهين
فائدة
فوائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'برهان' (proof) refers only to rational proofs.
Correction: In this context, 'برهان' includes both rational and transmitted evidence, as Al-Sawi explicitly states: 'الدليل عقلياً كان أو نقلياً'.

Grammatical Analysis of the Supplication
Al-Sawi explains the grammar of the opening supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Arabic grammatical structures like object advancement and verbal noun interpretation convey theological precision in supplication.

Core Idea
Grammar shapes meaning; in supplication, word order shows restriction.

Al-Sawi begins his commentary by dissecting the grammar of the author's supplication. He highlights how advancing the object 'God' serves to restrict the request to Him alone. Understanding these grammatical choices helps us appreciate the theological intentionality behind every word of the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic lines from 'قوله : (والله أسأل ... إلخ)' up to 'وقوله : (كل) معمول لـ(ينفع).' Focus on the grammatical terms like 'حصر' and 'تأويل مصدر'.
Look for the key term 'الحصر' (restriction).
2
Identify the grammatical points
Notice that Al-Sawi explains why the object 'الله' is placed first: to indicate restriction. Also, he interprets 'أن ينفع' as a verbal noun acting as the second object of 'أسأل'.
The original sentence is 'وأسأل الله النفع به' with the object advanced.
3
Relate to supplication
Reflect on why these grammatical details matter for the meaning: the restriction emphasizes that only God is asked, and the verbal noun makes the request concrete.
This precision is typical in Islamic theological texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والله أسأل ... إلخ) : قدَّم المعمول ليفيد الحصر، والسؤال معناه الطَّلب، وأن وما دخلت عليه في تأويل مصدر مفعول ثان لـ ( أسأل)، والأصل وأسأل الله النَّفع به، وقوله : (كل) معمول لـ(ينفع).
قوله : (من تلقاه بقلب سليم) : أي : من طالعه بنفسه أو بواسطة معلّم، خالياً من الاعتراض والأغراض الفاسدة ؛ لأنَّ النَّفع تابع للحب والاعتقاد.
قوله : (وأن يجعله) : معطوف على أن ينفع ) فهو من جملة المسؤول.
وقوله : (خالصاً): معمول لـ ( يجعل).
و (الكريم : صفة للوجه والمراد بالوجه الذات عند الخلف .
وأمَّا السَّلف فيقولون الله وجه لا كالوجوه، منزّه عن صفات الحوادث .
قوله : (إنَّه المولى ... إلخ): إما بكسر الهمزة مستأنفاً، واقعاً في جواب سؤال مقدر ؛ كأنَّه قال : سألته لأنَّه ... إلخ، أو بفتحها تعليل للسُّؤال .
والمولى له معان؛ منها المنعم؛ وهو المناسب هنا
قوله : (الرَّؤوف) : أي : شديد الرَّحمة.
و(الرَّحيم) : ذو الرَّحمة، وفي هذه الأسماء من المناسبة بالمطلوب ما
His statement: (And God I ask ... etc.): He advanced the object to indicate restriction, and 'question' (السؤال) means request. And 'that' (أن) and what enters upon it are in the interpretation of a verbal noun as the second object of 'ask' (أسأل). The original is: And I ask God for benefit through it. And his statement: (every) is an object of 'benefit' (ينفع).
His statement: (whoever encounters it with a sound heart): i.e., whoever reads it himself or through a teacher, free from objection and corrupt intentions, because benefit follows love and belief.
His statement: (and that He makes it): conjoined to 'that He benefits' so it is part of what is asked.
And his statement: (sincere): object of 'makes' (يجعل).
And (the Generous): attribute of 'the Face', and what is intended by 'the Face' is the Essence (الذات) according to the Khalaf.
As for the Salaf, they say: God has a face unlike other faces, transcendent above the attributes of originated things.
His statement: (Indeed He is the Master ... etc.): either with kasra on the hamza as a new sentence, serving as an answer to an implied question, as if he said: I asked Him because He is ... etc., or with fatha on it as a cause for the request.
And 'al-Mawla' (المولى) has meanings; among them: the Bestower of favor; and that is appropriate here.
His statement: (al-Ra'uf) (الرَّؤوف): i.e., extremely merciful.
And (al-Rahim) (الرَّحيم): possessor of mercy. And in these names there is appropriateness to what is requested, ...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage provides grammatical and theological commentary on the author's supplication. First, it explains the word order: advancing the object "God" indicates restriction (حص). The word "أسأل" (ask) means request. The phrase "أن ينفع" is interpreted as a verbal noun acting as the second object. The word "كل" is the object of "ينفع". Then it explains "من تلقاه بقلب سليم": it refers to anyone who reads it directly or via a teacher, free from criticism and corrupt intentions, because benefit depends on love and belief. Next, "وأن يجعله" is conjoined to "أن ينفع", so it is also part of the request. "خالصاً" is the object of "يجعل". The commentary then notes that "الكريم" is an attribute of "الوجه", and "الوجه" here means the Essence according to the Khalaf (later theologians), while the Salaf (early predecessors) affirm a face without modality, transcendent. Then it discusses "إنَّه المولى": the word can be read with a kasra (استئناف) or fatha (تعليل). "المولى" has meanings including "the Bestower of favor". Finally, "الرَّؤوف" means intensely merciful, and "الرَّحيم" means possessor of mercy, and these names are appropriate to the request.

تأويل مصدر

A) For emphasis
