Key Takeaway
Advancing the object in Arabic supplication serves to restrict the request to God alone, a deliberate grammatical choice for theological clarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for something in your du'a, do you genuinely feel that your hope is restricted to Him alone, or is your heart secretly relying on other means? This lesson shows that even word order can reflect tawheed—what does the order of your thoughts reveal? Will you check your heart before your next du'a and actively turn away from all else?
?
The grammatical analysis here is precise: advancing the object forces the meaning of restriction. In your life, what 'objects' do you advance before Allah—your desires, your plans, your fears? Are you unconsciously elevating something alongside Allah in your trust? Commit to examining one area where you depend on creation and strive to redirect that dependence exclusively to Allah.

Conditions for Receiving Benefit
Al-Sawi explains the conditions for benefiting from the supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prerequisites for benefiting from the supplication: a sound heart free from objection and corrupt intentions, and the request for sincerity.

Core Idea
Benefit from supplication depends on inner purity and sincere request.

After the grammatical opening, Al-Sawi turns to the conditions for the supplication to be beneficial. He explains that the one who 'encounters it with a sound heart' is free from objection and corrupt intentions, and then comments on the request for sincerity (خالصاً). This underscores the inseparable link between outward prayer and inward state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic from 'قوله : (من تلقاه بقلب سليم)' up to 'وقوله : (خالصاً): معمول لـ ( يجعل).' Note the terms 'سليم', 'خالصاً'.
Focus on the phrase 'بقلب سليم' (with a sound heart).
2
Understand the conditions
Al-Sawi explains that 'من تلقاه بقلب سليم' means whoever reads it himself or through a teacher, free from objection and corrupt intentions. Benefit follows love and belief.
The heart must be free from spiritual diseases like envy or wrong motives.
3
Analyze the request for sincerity
The phrase 'وأن يجعله خالصاً' is conjoined to 'أن ينفع', so sincerity is also part of what is asked. 'خالصاً' is the object of 'يجعل'.
Sincerity (إخلاص) is a key spiritual quality.

His statement: (And God I ask ... etc.): He advanced the object to indicate restriction, and 'question' (السؤال) means request. And 'that' (أن) and what enters upon it are in the interpretation of a verbal noun as the second object of 'ask' (أسأل). The original is: And I ask God for benefit through it. And his statement: (every) is an object of 'benefit' (ينفع).
His statement: (whoever encounters it with a sound heart): i.e., whoever reads it himself or through a teacher, free from objection and corrupt intentions, because benefit follows love and belief.
His statement: (and that He makes it): conjoined to 'that He benefits' so it is part of what is asked.
And his statement: (sincere): object of 'makes' (يجعل).
And (the Generous): attribute of 'the Face', and what is intended by 'the Face' is the Essence (الذات) according to the Khalaf.
As for the Salaf, they say: God has a face unlike other faces, transcendent above the attributes of originated things.
His statement: (Indeed He is the Master ... etc.): either with kasra on the hamza as a new sentence, serving as an answer to an implied question, as if he said: I asked Him because He is ... etc., or with fatha on it as a cause for the request.
And 'al-Mawla' (المولى) has meanings; among them: the Bestower of favor; and that is appropriate here.
His statement: (al-Ra'uf) (الرَّؤوف): i.e., extremely merciful.
And (al-Rahim) (الرَّحيم): possessor of mercy. And in these names there is appropriateness to what is requested, ...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage provides grammatical and theological commentary on the author's supplication. First, it explains the word order: advancing the object "God" indicates restriction (حص). The word "أسأل" (ask) means request. The phrase "أن ينفع" is interpreted as a verbal noun acting as the second object. The word "كل" is the object of "ينفع". Then it explains "من تلقاه بقلب سليم": it refers to anyone who reads it directly or via a teacher, free from criticism and corrupt intentions, because benefit depends on love and belief. Next, "وأن يجعله" is conjoined to "أن ينفع", so it is also part of the request. "خالصاً" is the object of "يجعل". The commentary then notes that "الكريم" is an attribute of "الوجه", and "الوجه" here means the Essence according to the Khalaf (later theologians), while the Salaf (early predecessors) affirm a face without modality, transcendent. Then it discusses "إنَّه المولى": the word can be read with a kasra (استئناف) or fatha (تعليل). "المولى" has meanings including "the Bestower of favor". Finally, "الرَّؤوف" means intensely merciful, and "الرَّحيم" means possessor of mercy, and these names are appropriate to the request.

Theological Nuances: The Face of God and Divine Names
Al-Sawi discusses the interpretation of 'al-Wajh' and the divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological difference between the Salaf and Khalaf regarding the attribute of 'al-Wajh' (Face), and the meanings of the divine names al-Mawla, al-Ra'uf, and al-Rahim in this supplication.

Core Idea
The divine attribute 'Face' is interpreted differently; the names al-Mawla, al-Ra'uf, al-Rahim indicate God's mercy and favor.

In the final portion of this passage, Al-Sawi delves into theological interpretation. He explains that the phrase 'الكريم' (the Generous) describes 'الوجه' (the Face), and notes that later theologians (Khalaf) understand 'الوجه' as the Essence of God, while the early predecessors (Salaf) affirm a real face unlike creation. He also comments on the names al-Mawla (the Bestower of favor), al-Ra'uf (intensely merciful), and al-Rahim (possessor of mercy), showing their appropriateness to the request.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

The passage ends abruptly; focus on the existing content.
2
Understand the two interpretations of 'al-Wajh'
Al-Sawi reports that 'الوجه' is interpreted by the Khalaf as the Essence (ذات), while the Salaf affirm it as a real attribute without modality.
This reflects the broader theological divergence on divine attributes.
3
Analyze the divine names
The name 'المولى' here means the Bestower of favor. 'الرؤوف' means extremely merciful, and 'الرحيم' means possessor of mercy. Al-Sawi notes that these names are appropriate to what is requested.
The incomplete sentence suggests further explanation.

Grammar and Theology of the Opening Supplication
Al-Sawi explains the grammar and theology of the opening supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical structure of the author's opening supplication and the theological significance of the attribute 'al-Wajh' (the Face) according to the Salaf and Khalaf.

Core Idea
Placing Allah first in supplication restricts all asking to Him alone.

Al-Sawi begins by analyzing the author's supplication phrase by phrase, revealing both grammatical precision and theological depth. He shows how the word order emphasizes that only Allah is asked, and then clarifies that 'the Face' is understood by early and later scholars in complementary ways. This lesson unpacks those layers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical moves
Notice that the object 'Allah' is placed before the verb 'as'alu' to indicate restriction (hasr). Also see how 'an yanfa'a' functions as a verbal noun, the second object of the verb.
This emphasis is lost in translation.
2
Understand 'who encounters it with a sound heart'
This includes both a direct reader and one who learns through a teacher. A 'sound heart' is free from objectionable motives, because benefit from the book depends on love and belief.
3
Compare Salaf and Khalaf on 'al-Wajh'
The Salaf affirm that Allah has a face without modality, while the Khalaf interpret it as the Essence (dhat). Al-Sawi mentions both without preferring one, but notes the Salaf's transcendence affirmation.
This is a key moment of theological nuance.
4
Reflect on why this matters
The discussion shows how early and later scholars maintain tanzih (transcendence) while differing in method. It also impacts how one reads anthropomorphic expressions in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والله أسأل ... إلخ) : قدَّم المعمول ليفيد الحصر، والسؤال معناه الطَّلب، وأن وما دخلت عليه في تأويل مصدر مفعول ثان لـ ( أسأل)، والأصل وأسأل الله النَّفع به، وقوله : (كل) معمول لـ(ينفع). خالياً قوله : (من تلقاه بقلب سليم) : أي : من طالعه بنفسه أو بواسطة معلّم، الاعتراض والأغراض الفاسدة ؛ لأنَّ النَّفع تابع للحب والاعتقاد. من قوله : (وأن يجعله) : معطوف على أن ينفع ) فهو من جملة المسؤول. وقوله : (خالصاً): معمول لـ ( يجعل). و (الكريم : صفة للوجه والمراد بالوجه الذات عند الخلف . وأمَّا السَّلف فيقولون الله وجه لا كالوجوه، منزّه عن صفات الحوادث . قوله : (إنَّه المولى ... إلخ): إما بكسر الهمزة مستأنفاً، واقعاً في جواب سؤال مقدر ؛ كأنَّه قال : سألته لأنَّه ... إلخ، أو بفتحها تعليل للسُّؤال . والمولى له معان؛ منها المنعم؛ وهو المناسب هنا قوله : (الرَّؤوف) : أي : شديد الرَّحمة. و(الرَّحيم) : ذو الرَّحمة، وفي هذه الأسماء من المناسبة بالمطلوب ما لا يخفى، فإنَّ من لطائف الدُّعاء أنَّ الإنسان يخاطب ربَّه بالاسم المناسب لمطلوبه ؛ كدعاء أيوب عليه السَّلام حيث قال : أَنِّي مَسَّنِيَ الضُّرُّ وَأَنتَ أَرْحَمُ الرَّحِمِينَ (١) ، ودعاء يونس حيث قال : سُبْحَنَكَ إِنِّي كُنتُ مِنَ الظَّالِمِينَ (٢)، ودعاء سليمان عليه السَّلام حيث قال : إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْوَهَّابُ (۳) ، ودعاء زكريا عليه السَّلام حيث قال: (وَأَنتَ خَيْرُ الْوَارِثِينَ ) (٤ ) .
His statement: '(And Allah I ask... etc.)': He placed the object first to indicate restriction, and 'asking' means requesting, and 'an' with what enters upon it is interpreted as a verbal noun, the second object of 'ask', and the original is 'And I ask Allah for benefit through it.' His statement: 'everyone' is the object of 'benefit'. 'Free from' - his statement: 'who encounters it with a sound heart' means: whoever reads it himself or through a teacher, from objections and corrupt purposes; because benefit depends on love and belief. His statement: 'and that He make it' is coordinate with 'that it benefit', so it is part of what is asked for. His statement: 'sincere' is the object of 'make'. And 'al-Karim' is an attribute of 'the Face', and what is meant by 'the Face' is the Essence according to the later scholars. As for the early scholars, they say: Allah has a face not like faces, transcendent above the attributes of originated things. His statement: 'Indeed He is the Master... etc.': either with a kasra on the hamza as a new sentence, occurring as an answer to an implied question, as if He said: I asked Him because... etc., or with a fatha as a cause for the request. And 'al-Mawla' has meanings; among them the Benefactor; and it is what is appropriate here. His statement: 'al-Ra'uf' means intensely merciful. And 'al-Rahim' is the one possessing mercy. And in these names there is such appropriateness to what is sought that it is not hidden; for part of the subtleties of supplication is that a person addresses his Lord with the name appropriate to his need, like the supplication of Ayyub (peace be upon him) when he said: 'Trily, adversity has touched me and You are the Most Merciful of the merciful' (1), and the supplication of Yunus when he said: 'Glory be to You, indeed I was of the wrongdoers' (2), and the supplication of Sulayman (peace be upon him) when he said: 'Indeed You are the Bestower' (3), and the supplication of Zakariyya (peace be upon him) when he said: 'And You are the Best of inheritors' (4).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological commentary on the author's opening supplication. Al-Sawi begins by analyzing the syntax: the object 'Allah' is placed before the verb 'as'alu' to indicate restriction (hasr), emphasizing that only Allah is asked. He then explains that the phrase 'an yanfa'a' (that He benefit) is a verbal noun forming the second object of the verb. Next, he clarifies the phrase 'man talaqahu bi-qalb salim' (whoever encounters it with a sound heart): this includes both the direct reader and the one who learns through a teacher, and 'sound heart' means free from objectionable motives. Benefit from the book is contingent on love and belief. He then treats 'wa an yaj'alahu' (and that He make it) as coordinate with the previous request, and 'khalisan' (sincere) as the object. A key theological point follows: the attribute 'al-Karim' is said of 'al-Wajh' (the Face). Here Al-Sawi distinguishes the approach of the later scholars (khalaf), who interpret 'Face' as the Essence (dhat), from the early Salaf, who affirm a face but without modality or resemblance to creatures. He then discusses the phrase 'innahu al-mawla al-ra'uf al-rahim', noting two possible readings of the hamza (kasr or fath) and the meaning of 'mawla' as Benefactor. Finally, he highlights the subtle art of supplication: choosing a name of Allah appropriate to the specific need, illustrated by four prophetic examples—Ayyub asking by Allah's mercy, Yunus by His forgiveness, Sulayman by His bestowal, and Zakariyya by His inheritance. This shows that the author's closing words are not random but deliberately chosen.

Key Terms
قلب سليم
الوجه
السلف
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-Wajh' is a literal physical face of Allah.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that the Khalaf interpret it as the Essence, while the Salaf affirm it but without modality, transcendent above attributes of created things.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, what is the theological difference between the Salaf and the Khalaf regarding the attribute 'al-Wajh' (the Face) of Allah?
A) The Salaf deny it while the Khalaf affirm it.
B) The Salaf affirm it without modality, while the Khalaf interpret it as the Essence.
C) Both groups agree it is metaphorical.
D) The Salaf interpret it as honor, the Khalaf as essence.

Key Takeaway
The author's opening supplication is carefully constructed both grammatically and theologically to affirm that Allah alone is asked and that His attributes are understood without anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you turn to Allah in supplication, do you truly restrict your asking to Him alone, or do your desires depend elsewhere? Examine your heart: are you asking for benefit—for every book you read, every lesson you attend—as from Allah alone?
?
Do you approach knowledge—whether by yourself or through a teacher—with a 'sound heart' free from pride, argumentation, or show? What corrupt purpose has lodged itself in your seeking, and will you purge it for the sake of Allah's acceptance?
?
When you read the divine attributes, do you recoil from affirming what the early Muslims affirmed, or do you confidently interpret them? Is your stance rooted in humility before revelation or in a desire to fit God into your own categories?

The Art of Supplication: Choosing Allah's Names
Al-Sawi explains the appropriateness of Allah's names in the closing of the supplication and cites prophetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that a supplication should address Allah by names relevant to the request, as demonstrated by the prophets.

Core Idea
Call on Allah by the name that matches your need.

Al-Sawi concludes his commentary by highlighting a beautiful subtlety in the author's closing words: the names al-Mawla, al-Ra'uf, al-Rahim are chosen to match the request for benefit and sincerity. He then illustrates this with four prophetic examples, showing that the greatest supplicants always used the most appropriate names. This lesson explores that principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the discussion of 'innahu al-mawla'
Al-Sawi notes two possible readings of the hamza: either as a new sentence (kasr) giving a reason, or as a cause (fath). The meaning of 'al-Mawla' here is the Benefactor.
2
Understand the appropriateness of the names
The names al-Ra'uf (Intensely Merciful) and al-Rahim (Merciful) are perfectly suited to the request for benefit and sincerity.
This is part of the subtlety of supplication.
3
Examine the prophetic examples
Each prophet chose a name of Allah that directly relates to his need: Ayyub (mercy), Yunus (forgiveness), Sulayman (bestowal), Zakariyya (inheritance).
These are models for our own supplication.
4
Apply the principle
Reflect on how the author's choice of names at the end of his supplication is deliberate and instructive for the reader.
We learn to be intentional in our own dua.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والله أسأل ... إلخ) : قدَّم المعمول ليفيد الحصر، والسؤال معناه الطَّلب، وأن وما دخلت عليه في تأويل مصدر مفعول ثان لـ ( أسأل)، والأصل وأسأل الله النَّفع به، وقوله : (كل) معمول لـ(ينفع). خالياً قوله : (من تلقاه بقلب سليم) : أي : من طالعه بنفسه أو بواسطة معلّم، الاعتراض والأغراض الفاسدة ؛ لأنَّ النَّفع تابع للحب والاعتقاد. من قوله : (وأن يجعله) : معطوف على أن ينفع ) فهو من جملة المسؤول. وقوله : (خالصاً): معمول لـ ( يجعل). و (الكريم : صفة للوجه والمراد بالوجه الذات عند الخلف . وأمَّا السَّلف فيقولون الله وجه لا كالوجوه، منزّه عن صفات الحوادث . قوله : (إنَّه المولى ... إلخ): إما بكسر الهمزة مستأنفاً، واقعاً في جواب سؤال مقدر ؛ كأنَّه قال : سألته لأنَّه ... إلخ، أو بفتحها تعليل للسُّؤال . والمولى له معان؛ منها المنعم؛ وهو المناسب هنا قوله : (الرَّؤوف) : أي : شديد الرَّحمة. و(الرَّحيم) : ذو الرَّحمة، وفي هذه الأسماء من المناسبة بالمطلوب ما لا يخفى، فإنَّ من لطائف الدُّعاء أنَّ الإنسان يخاطب ربَّه بالاسم المناسب لمطلوبه ؛ كدعاء أيوب عليه السَّلام حيث قال : أَنِّي مَسَّنِيَ الضُّرُّ وَأَنتَ أَرْحَمُ الرَّحِمِينَ (١) ، ودعاء يونس حيث قال : سُبْحَنَكَ إِنِّي كُنتُ مِنَ الظَّالِمِينَ (٢)، ودعاء سليمان عليه السَّلام حيث قال : إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْوَهَّابُ (۳) ، ودعاء زكريا عليه السَّلام حيث قال: (وَأَنتَ خَيْرُ الْوَارِثِينَ ) (٤ ) .
His statement: '(And Allah I ask... etc.)': He placed the object first to indicate restriction, and 'asking' means requesting, and 'an' with what enters upon it is interpreted as a verbal noun, the second object of 'ask', and the original is 'And I ask Allah for benefit through it.' His statement: 'everyone' is the object of 'benefit'. 'Free from' - his statement: 'who encounters it with a sound heart' means: whoever reads it himself or through a teacher, from objections and corrupt purposes; because benefit depends on love and belief. His statement: 'and that He make it' is coordinate with 'that it benefit', so it is part of what is asked for. His statement: 'sincere' is the object of 'make'. And 'al-Karim' is an attribute of 'the Face', and what is meant by 'the Face' is the Essence according to the later scholars. As for the early scholars, they say: Allah has a face not like faces, transcendent above the attributes of originated things. His statement: 'Indeed He is the Master... etc.': either with a kasra on the hamza as a new sentence, occurring as an answer to an implied question, as if He said: I asked Him because... etc., or with a fatha as a cause for the request. And 'al-Mawla' has meanings; among them the Benefactor; and it is what is appropriate here. His statement: 'al-Ra'uf' means intensely merciful. And 'al-Rahim' is the one possessing mercy. And in these names there is such appropriateness to what is sought that it is not hidden; for part of the subtleties of supplication is that a person addresses his Lord with the name appropriate to his need, like the supplication of Ayyub (peace be upon him) when he said: 'Trily, adversity has touched me and You are the Most Merciful of the merciful' (1), and the supplication of Yunus when he said: 'Glory be to You, indeed I was of the wrongdoers' (2), and the supplication of Sulayman (peace be upon him) when he said: 'Indeed You are the Bestower' (3), and the supplication of Zakariyya (peace be upon him) when he said: 'And You are the Best of inheritors' (4).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological commentary on the author's opening supplication. Al-Sawi begins by analyzing the syntax: the object 'Allah' is placed before the verb 'as'alu' to indicate restriction (hasr), emphasizing that only Allah is asked. He then explains that the phrase 'an yanfa'a' (that He benefit) is a verbal noun forming the second object of the verb. Next, he clarifies the phrase 'man talaqahu bi-qalb salim' (whoever encounters it with a sound heart): this includes both the direct reader and the one who learns through a teacher, and 'sound heart' means free from objectionable motives. Benefit from the book is contingent on love and belief. He then treats 'wa an yaj'alahu' (and that He make it) as coordinate with the previous request, and 'khalisan' (sincere) as the object. A key theological point follows: the attribute 'al-Karim' is said of 'al-Wajh' (the Face). Here Al-Sawi distinguishes the approach of the later scholars (khalaf), who interpret 'Face' as the Essence (dhat), from the early Salaf, who affirm a face but without modality or resemblance to creatures. He then discusses the phrase 'innahu al-mawla al-ra'uf al-rahim', noting two possible readings of the hamza (kasr or fath) and the meaning of 'mawla' as Benefactor. Finally, he highlights the subtle art of supplication: choosing a name of Allah appropriate to the specific need, illustrated by four prophetic examples—Ayyub asking by Allah's mercy, Yunus by His forgiveness, Sulayman by His bestowal, and Zakariyya by His inheritance. This shows that the author's closing words are not random but deliberately chosen.

الرؤوف
الرحيم

Misconception: One might think any name of Allah can be used arbitrarily in supplication.
Correction: The prophetic examples show that choosing an appropriate name is a subtle art that increases the likelihood of acceptance.

Key Takeaway
In supplication, it is a sunna to address Allah by names that directly relate to your request, as demonstrated by the prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you have a pressing need, do you rush to ask Allah with any name, or do you pause to reflect on which of His attributes is most directly responding to that need? How can you train yourself to do this starting today?
?
Consider the last du'a you made that you felt was unanswered. Could it be that you called upon Allah with a name that was not the most appropriate for your request? What would you change if you could make that du'a again?
?
The prophets knew exactly which name to use because they knew their Lord intimately. What does your current knowledge of Allah's names and attributes say about the depth of your relationship with Him? What concrete step will you take this week to learn one new name deeply?

Definition of Tawfiq by Imam al-Haramayn
Al-Sawi defines tawfiq linguistically and terminologically with a key kalam distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'tawfiq' (divine enabling) as defined by Imam al-Haramayn, including its two essential components—ability and motivation—and why the disbeliever lacks tawfiq.

Core Idea
Tawfiq is not just help—it is God creating in you both the ability and the desire to obey.

Al-Sawi, while commenting on a line of poetry, pauses to define a key term: tawfiq. He explains its linguistic root and then gives the technical definition from Imam al-Haramayn, which reveals a crucial nuance in Islamic theology. This passage shows that true success is not merely having the strength to act, but also having the will to act righteously—and both are gifts from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Notice that the phrase 'wa ma tawfiqi illa billah' is a parenthetical blessing. Al-Sawi explains it as an expression of humility and disavowal of personal power.
2
Grasp the two definitions
Read the linguistic definition (making a thing conform to a thing) and then the terminological definition from Imam al-Haramayn. Focus on the two elements: creation of ability (qudra) and motivation (da'iya).
The terminological definition is the key for kalām.
3
Analyze the nuance of ability
Al-Sawi clarifies that 'ability' here means soundness of causes and instruments, not just raw power. This relies on the Ash'ari doctrine that accidents endure for two moments.
This nuance explains why a disbeliever may have physical capability but still lacks tawfiq.
4
Apply to the disbeliever
Consider why the disbeliever is not granted tawfiq: he lacks the divinely-created motivation (da'iya). The Qur'anic verse alludes to this by linking guidance to divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فأقول ... إلخ ) : الظاهر أنَّ الفاء واقعة في جواب شرط مقدر تقديره : إذا تمهد ما ذكرت لك ؛ فأقول ومقول القول قوله : (بسم الرَّحمن الرّحيم ...) إلى آخر الكتاب متناً وشرحاً. قوله: (وما توفيقي إلا بالله ... إلخ) : جملة معترضة، قصد بها التَّبرُّك، والتبري من الحول والقوَّة. والتوفيق معناه لغةً : موافقة الشَّيء للشَّيء، واصطلاحاً : خلق قدرة الطَّاعة والداعية إليها في العبد عند إمام الحرمين ؛ فالمراد بالقدرة عنده سلامة الأسباب والآلات بناءً على أنَّ العرض يبقى زمانين؛ فالكافر غير موفّق؛ لعدم الداعية، ويشهد لذلك قوله تعالى: (فَمَن يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَن يَهْدِيَهُ).
His statement: 'So I say... etc.': Apparently the fa' occurs in the answer to an implied condition whose appreciation is: when what I have mentioned to you is established, then I say; and the content of the saying is his statement: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful...' to the end of the book, text and commentary. His statement: 'And my success is only from Allah... etc.': an intervening sentence, intended for blessing and disavowal of power and strength. And 'tawfiq' linguistically means making a thing conform to a thing, and terminologically: the creation of the ability for obedience and the motivation towards it in the servant according to Imam al-Haramayn; thus what is meant by ability according to him is the soundness of causes and instruments, based on the view that an accident endures for two moments; so the disbeliever is not granted tawfiq due to lack of motivation. And that is witnessed by His statement: 'Whomever Allah wills to guide...'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage provides a definition of 'tawfiq' (divine success or enabling grace) as understood in kalām. Al-Sawi first explains the transitional phrase 'fa-aqulu' (so I say): the letter fa is the answer to an implied condition—'when all has been prefaced, then I begin the commentary.' He clarifies that the author's statement 'bismillah' is the commencement of the actual work. The phrase 'wa ma tawfiqi illa billah' (and my success is only from Allah) is described as a parenthetical clause for blessing and humility, disavowing any personal power. Then comes the core definition: linguistically, tawfiq means making one thing correspond to another. Terminologically, he quotes Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who defines tawfiq as the creation (khalq) in the servant of both the ability (qudra) for obedience and the motivation (da'iya) towards it. This entails that mere physical ability is insufficient; one also needs the internal inclination. Al-Sawi notes that 'qudra' here means soundness of causes and instruments, based on the Ash'ari doctrine that accidents endure for two moments. Consequently, a disbeliever is not 'muwaffaq' (divinely enabled) because, although he may have ability, he lacks the divinely-created motivation. The passage concludes with a Qur'anic indication: 'Whomever Allah wills to guide...' (the complete verse from Surah al-An'am 6:125 continues 'He expands his breast for Islam'), showing that guidance includes both enabling and motivation.

Key Terms
توفيق
قدرة
داعية
إمام الحرمين
عرض
حول
قوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tawfiq is simply God's general help or assistance.
Correction: According to Imam al-Haramayn, tawfiq is the specific creation of both ability and motivation for obedience; mere ability without divinely-given motivation is insufficient for success.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Haramayn as cited by Al-Sawi, what two things are created in the servant for tawfiq?
A) Knowledge and wealth
B) Ability (qudra) for obedience and motivation (da'iya) towards it
C) Free will and determinism
D) Faith and repentance

Key Takeaway
Tawfiq is the creation in the servant of both the ability and the motivation for obedience, so the disbeliever lacks motivation and is not granted success.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim you want to obey Allah, but often you lack the drive to pray or read Quran. After learning that tawfiq is the creation of both ability and desire by Allah, ask yourself: Do you truly rely on Him for the desire, or do you assume it is within your control? This week, before any act of worship, say 'لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله' and mean it as a plea for both ability and motivation. Will you commit to this?
?
If the disbeliever has physical ability but no divinely-given motivation to obey, could it be that your own spiritual laziness is a sign that Allah has withheld tawfiq from you in that area? What specific area of your life are you most struggling to find desire for obedience? Will you beg Allah tonight for the 'da'iya' to turn that area around?
?
The passage says tawfiq is from Allah alone – 'وما توفيقي إلا بالله'. When you succeed in an act of worship, whose glory do you seek? Are you quick to attribute it to your own effort, or do you genuinely feel that Allah created both the ability and the desire in you? Before you finish this reflection, make a sincere dua asking Allah to grant you tawfiq in your next act of obedience.

The Subtlety of Supplicating with Appropriate Divine Names
Al-Sawi explains the virtue of supplication with appropriate divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the virtue of addressing Allah with names appropriate to one's need in supplication, as illustrated by the examples of the prophets.

Core Idea
In supplication, call upon Allah by the name that matches your request.

Al-Sawi begins his commentary by highlighting a profound subtlety of supplication: that a person should address their Lord using the divine name most relevant to what they are asking for. He supports this with four Quranic examples from prophets Ayyub, Yunus, Sulayman, and Zakariyya. This lesson unpacks that teaching and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on supplication
Read the Arabic text from 'فإنَّ من لطائف الدُّعاء' to the end of the prophetic examples. Pay attention to the names used.

2
Identify the four examples
List the four prophets mentioned and the divine attribute each invoked. Consider why each attribute is appropriate to their situation.
3
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: How can I apply this principle in my own supplications? What does it teach us about Allah's names?
This connects to the broader theme of adab (etiquette) in du'a.

The Grammar of Seeking Divine Assistance: Al-Sawi's Opening
Al-Sawi explains the grammatical structure of the opening phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical structure of the opening phrase 'fa-aqulu wa ma tawfiqi illa billah', including the role of the 'fa' as the answer to a condition and the function of the intervening sentence.

Core Idea
The opening phrase embeds a profound reliance on Allah.

Al-Sawi continues by explaining the grammar of the author's opening words 'fa-aqulu' (I say) and the interjected phrase 'wa ma tawfiqi illa billah' (and my success is not but from Allah). He shows that 'fa' is the answer to an implicit condition, and the intervening sentence serves as a declaration of reliance and humility. This lesson unpacks that grammatical and theological nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the grammatical explanation
Read the Arabic text from 'قوله : (فأقول ... إلخ )' to the end. Focus on the grammatical terms.
2
Identify the function of 'fa'
Al-Sawi says the 'fa' is in the answer to an implicit condition. Understand what that condition is and how it connects.
The implicit condition: 'When what I mentioned is prepared, then I say.'
3
Understand the intervening sentence
'Wa ma tawfiqi illa billah' is identified as an intervening sentence. Why is it placed here? What does it express?
It is for blessing and disavowal of power and strength.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this grammatical structure reinforces the author's reliance on Allah even in the act of writing.
This ties back to the theme of tawfiq (divine success).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأقول وما توفيقي إلا بالله العلي العظيم : لا يخفى، فإنَّ من لطائف الدُّعاء أنَّ الإنسان يخاطب ربَّه بالاسم المناسب لمطلوبه؛ كدعاء أيوب عليه السَّلام حيث قال : أَنِّي مَسَّنِيَ الضُّرُّ وَأَنتَ أَرْحَمُ الرَّحِمِينَ (١) ، ودعاء يونس حيث قال : سُبْحَنَكَ إِنِّي كُنتُ مِنَ الظَّالِمِينَ (٢)، ودعاء سليمان عليه السَّلام حيث قال : إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْوَهَّابُ (۳) ، ودعاء زكريا عليه السَّلام حيث قال: (وَأَنتَ خَيْرُ الْوَارِثِينَ ) (٤ ) . قوله : (فأقول ... إلخ ) : الظاهر أنَّ الفاء واقعة في جواب شرط مقدر تقديره : إذا تمهد ما ذكرت لك ؛ فأقول ومقول القول قوله : (بسم الرَّحمن الرّحيم ...) إلى آخر الكتاب متناً وشرحاً. قوله: (وما توفيقي إلا بالله ... إلخ) : جملة معترضة، قصد بها التَّبرُّك، والتبري من الحول والقوَّة.
I say, and my success is not but from Allah, the Most High, the Great: It is not hidden, for among the subtleties of supplication is that a person addresses his Lord with the name appropriate to his request; like the supplication of Ayyub (peace be upon him) when he said: 'Indeed, adversity has touched me, and You are the Most Merciful of the merciful' (21:83), and the supplication of Yunus when he said: 'Exalted are You! Indeed, I have been of the wrongdoers' (21:87), and the supplication of Sulayman (peace be upon him) when he said: 'Indeed, You are the Bestower' (38:35), and the supplication of Zakariyya (peace be upon him) when he said: 'And You are the best of inheritors' (21:89). His saying: ('I say... etc.') : Apparently, the 'fa' occurs in the answer to an implicit condition, its appreciation being: When what I mentioned to you is prepared, then I say. And the object of 'I say' is his statement: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful...' to the end of the book, text and commentary. His saying: ('And my success is not but from Allah... etc.') : An intervening sentence, intended for blessing and disavowal of power and strength.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an introductory commentary by al-Sawi on the opening words of the author. He begins by remarking on the subtlety of invoking Allah with names appropriate to one's need, citing examples from prophets. Then he explains the grammatical structure of 'fa-aqulu' (I say) as the answer to an implicit conditional. He notes the function of the sentence 'wa ma tawfiqi illa billah' as an interjection for blessing and humility.

Key Terms
الفاء
جملة معترضة
التبرك
التبري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opening phrase 'Wa ma tawfiqi illa billah' is merely a formality or rhetorical flourish.
Correction: Al-Sawi explains it is an intervening sentence intended for blessing and to disavow any personal power or strength, highlighting the author's reliance on Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, what is the function of the sentence 'wa ma tawfiqi illa billah'?
A) To indicate the start of the book
B) To serve as an intervening sentence for blessing and disavowal of power
C) To answer a conditional
D) To request divine aid

Key Takeaway
The opening phrase 'fa-aqulu wa ma tawfiqi illa billah' is grammatically structured to express reliance on Allah through an implied condition and an interjection for blessing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you genuinely feel that your successes are from Allah alone, or do you secretly attribute them to your own ability? What would it mean to truly disavow your own power and strength in your daily life?
?
When you begin a task, do you consciously rely on Allah in the way Al-Sawi describes? What would change if you took a moment to recite 'wa ma tawfiqi illa billah' with full understanding?
?
The phrase is an intervening sentence seeking blessing and disavowing power. In what areas of your life are you still holding onto a false sense of self-sufficiency? How can you practically disavow that today?

Opening with the Basmala Despite the Poetic Form
Al-Sawi notes the basmala's use despite poetry and lists ten remembrances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Sawi emphasizes that the author began with the basmala even though the text is poetry, and you will learn the list of ten obligatory remembrances.

Core Idea
The basmala's importance transcends literary form; it is a required remembrance.

Al-Sawi, in his commentary, notes that the author of the poem opened with the basmala despite the work being poetry. This might seem trivial, but it highlights the sanctity of the basmala and its status as one of the obligatory remembrances. He then cites verses enumerating ten such remembrances, placing the basmala among them. This lesson explores that observation and the list.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the poetic style of the listed remembrances.
2
Identify the ten remembrances
The passage lists ten obligatory remembrances. See if you can find each one in the verse.
The list includes: Ta'awwudh, Basmala, Hamdalah, Tahmid, Tasbih, Hawqalah, Takbir, Istighfar, Salat, and Salam.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why the author bothered to mention that the work is poetry. What does this say about the basmala's status?
This connects to the core idea: the basmala is so important it is used even in poetry.

Source Text

This brief passage notes that despite the poetic nature of the work, the author begins with the basmala. Al-Sawi then cites a poetic compilation listing ten obligatory remembrances, including the basmala, emphasizing its importance.

Ruling on Opening Poetry with Basmalah
Ruling on basmalah in poetry and categories of permissible poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on opening poetry with the basmalah and which types of poetry are considered acts of obedience.

Core Idea
Poetry can be a means of worship when its content is good.

In this passage, al-Sawi addresses a practical question for scholars who write in verse: Should one begin a poem with the basmalah? He presents the majority view that it is recommended, provided the poem itself is not forbidden or disliked. He then lists the kinds of poetry that are not only permissible but meritorious, and supports this with the example of the Prophet ﷺ listening to poets in the mosque.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the ruling
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Notice that al-Sawi first states the dispute and then the preponderant view.
2
Identify the categories of permissible poetry
Find the list of topics that make poetry an act of obedience. Count them and note each one.
The categories include prophethood, Islam, judgment, guidance, noble manners, encouraging obedience, and avoiding disobedience.
3
Reflect on the Prophetic example
The passage cites a hadith that the Prophet listened to poets like Hassan ibn Thabit. Consider how this supports the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بسم الله الرّحمن الرّحيم): افتتح كتابه بالبسملة (٢) مع أنَّه شعر، وقع الخلاف في كراهة افتتاحه بها وعدمها والرَّاجح قول الجمهور باستحباب افتتاحه بها ما لم يكن محرَّماً أو مكروهاً، وكل شعر فيه النُّبوَّة أو الإسلام، أو الحكم، أو الهد، أو مكارم الأخلاق، أو حثّ على طاعة، أو اجتناب معصية؛ فإنشاؤه وإنشاده واستماعه طاعة؛ لأنَّه وسيلة إلى طاعة؛ فقد صح أن المصطفى لو كان له شعراء يصغي إليهم في المسجد وغيره ؛ منهم : حسان بن رواحة. وأفرد البسملة عن الشعر ولم يأت بها نظماً كما فعل الشاطبي في [من الطويل] قوله (۱) : بدأتُ ببسم الله في النَّظم أولا تبارك رحماناً رحيماً ومويلا لأنه يعسر الإتيان على هيئتها من غير تغيير، بخلاف الحمدلة، ولأنه خلاف الأولى.
His saying: '(In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful): He opened his book with the basmalah (2) even though it is poetry. There is a dispute about its being disliked to open with it or not, and the preponderant view is the statement of the majority that it is recommended to open with it as long as it is not forbidden or disliked. And every poem that contains prophethood, Islam, judgment, guidance, noble manners, encouragement to obedience, or avoidance of disobedience, its composition, recitation, and listening is an act of obedience because it is a means to obedience. It has been authentically reported that the Chosen One ﷺ had poets to whom he would listen in the mosque and elsewhere, among them: Hassan ibn Thabit. And he singled out the basmalah from the poetry and did not present it in verse as al-Shatibi did in [from al-Tawil?] His saying (1): 'I began with the name of God in the poem first, blessed, merciful, and a protector' because it is difficult to produce it in its form without change, unlike the tahmid, and because it is contrary to what is preferable (khilaf al-awla).
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the basmalah in poetry. It begins by noting that the author opened with the basmalah despite the work being poetry. A point of fiqh follows: the majority hold it recommended to open with it unless the poem's content is forbidden or disliked. The passage then lists permissible categories of poetry. Next, the author explains why he did not put the basmalah in verse form: it would require altering its wording, which is difficult, and doing so would be contrary to what is preferable (khilaf al-awla). He contrasts al-Shatibi who did versify it.

Key Terms
بسملة
شعر
استحباب
حسان بن رواحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All poetry is sinful or at best neutral.
Correction: Poetry with good content is an act of obedience.

Checkpoint
According to the majority, what is the ruling on opening a poem with the basmalah?
A) Disliked
B) Recommended
C) Forbidden
D) Permissible without preference

Key Takeaway
The basmalah is recommended at the start of permissible poetry, and poetry with good content is a means of worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What kinds of poetry do you listen to or recite? Are they among the virtuous categories the author lists? Will you change your habits based on this lesson?
?
If you begin an action with basmalah, do you consciously intend it as an act of worship? How can you make this intention more deliberate in your daily life?
?
The Prophet ﷺ listened to poetry in the mosque. Does this challenge any assumption you had about poetry and spirituality? How will you respond to that challenge?

Why the Author Did Not Versify the Basmalah
Justification for not versifying the basmalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Sawi chose not to versify the basmalah and the principle of avoiding unnecessary change.

Core Idea
Preserving the exact wording of the basmalah takes priority over poetic form.

Having discussed the ruling, al-Sawi now explains his own practice: he wrote the basmalah in prose rather than verse, unlike some other scholars. He gives two reasons: it is difficult to include the full basmalah without altering its wording, and doing so would be contrary to what is preferable (khilaf al-awla). He contrasts with al-Shatibi who did versify it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on singling out basmalah
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that al-Sawi contrasts his method with al-Shatibi's.
2
Understand the difficulty of versifying the basmalah
The passage states that it is difficult to produce the basmalah in its exact form without change when putting it into verse.
3
Note the concept of khilaf al-awla
Even if possible, it would be considered contrary to what is preferable, meaning it is better to preserve the wording exactly.
This shows careful respect for the revealed text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بسم الله الرّحمن الرّحيم): افتتح كتابه بالبسملة (٢) مع أنَّه شعر، وقع الخلاف في كراهة افتتاحه بها وعدمها والرَّاجح قول الجمهور باستحباب افتتاحه بها ما لم يكن محرَّماً أو مكروهاً، وكل شعر فيه النُّبوَّة أو الإسلام، أو الحكم، أو الهد، أو مكارم الأخلاق، أو حثّ على طاعة، أو اجتناب معصية؛ فإنشاؤه وإنشاده واستماعه طاعة؛ لأنَّه وسيلة إلى طاعة؛ فقد صح أن المصطفى لو كان له شعراء يصغي إليهم في المسجد وغيره ؛ منهم : حسان بن رواحة. وأفرد البسملة عن الشعر ولم يأت بها نظماً كما فعل الشاطبي في [من الطويل] قوله (۱) : بدأتُ ببسم الله في النَّظم أولا تبارك رحماناً رحيماً ومويلا لأنه يعسر الإتيان على هيئتها من غير تغيير، بخلاف الحمدلة، ولأنه خلاف الأولى.
His saying: '(In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful): He opened his book with the basmalah (2) even though it is poetry. There is a dispute about its being disliked to open with it or not, and the preponderant view is the statement of the majority that it is recommended to open with it as long as it is not forbidden or disliked. And every poem that contains prophethood, Islam, judgment, guidance, noble manners, encouragement to obedience, or avoidance of disobedience, its composition, recitation, and listening is an act of obedience because it is a means to obedience. It has been authentically reported that the Chosen One ﷺ had poets to whom he would listen in the mosque and elsewhere, among them: Hassan ibn Thabit. And he singled out the basmalah from the poetry and did not present it in verse as al-Shatibi did in [from al-Tawil?] His saying (1): 'I began with the name of God in the poem first, blessed, merciful, and a protector' because it is difficult to produce it in its form without change, unlike the tahmid, and because it is contrary to what is preferable (khilaf al-awla).
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the basmalah in poetry. It begins by noting that the author opened with the basmalah despite the work being poetry. A point of fiqh follows: the majority hold it recommended to open with it unless the poem's content is forbidden or disliked. The passage then lists permissible categories of poetry. Next, the author explains why he did not put the basmalah in verse form: it would require altering its wording, which is difficult, and doing so would be contrary to what is preferable (khilaf al-awla). He contrasts al-Shatibi who did versify it.

Key Terms
بسملة
خلاف الأولى
حمدلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is fine to change the wording of the basmalah for poetic meter.
Correction: The basmalah should be preserved exactly, and versifying it may lead to unintended changes, making it khilaf al-awla.

Checkpoint
Why did al-Sawi not versify the basmalah?
A) He forgot to include it
B) It is difficult to maintain its exact form and it is khilaf al-awla
C) He did not like poetry
D) The basmalah is not allowed in poetry

Key Takeaway
Preserving the exact wording of the basmalah is more important than poetic consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ruling on Opening Poetry with Basmala and Virtuous Themes
Al-Sawi discusses the ruling on basmala in poetry and lists virtuous themes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on opening poetry with the basmala and the types of poetry that are considered acts of obedience.

Core Idea
Poetry's ruling depends on its content; the basmala is recommended when the poem is virtuous.

This lesson addresses a practical question: should a Muslim poet begin with the basmala? Al-Sawi presents the scholarly disagreement and the majority view that it is recommended, provided the poem itself is not sinful. He then lists the virtuous themes that elevate poetry to an act of obedience, supported by the Prophet's own practice of listening to poets like Hassan ibn Thabit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disagreement
Read the passage and notice that there is a disagreement (al-khilaf) about the ruling of opening poetry with the basmala. Some consider it disliked, but the majority's view is discussed next.
2
Understand the condition
The preponderant view is that it is recommended (istihbab) as long as the poem is not haram or makruh. This condition is key.
3
Explore the list of virtuous themes
The passage enumerates themes that make poetry an act of obedience: prophethood, Islam, judgment, guidance, noble manners, encouragement to obey, and avoidance of sin. Notice each one.
These themes are the criterion for virtuous poetry.
4
Reflect on the basmala's unique status
Al-Sawi explains that the basmala itself is not versified in poetry because it is difficult to incorporate without changing its form, unlike the hamdala. This is a notable exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقع الخلاف في كراهة افتتاحه بها وعدمها والرَّاجح قول الجمهور باستحباب افتتاحه بها ما لم يكن محرَّماً أو مكروهاً، وكل شعر فيه النُّبوَّة أو الإسلام، أو الحكم، أو الهد، أو مكارم الأخلاق، أو حثّ على طاعة، أو اجتناب معصية؛ فإنشاؤه وإنشاده واستماعه طاعة؛ لأنَّه وسيلة إلى طاعة؛ فقد صح أن المصطفى لو كان له شعراء يصغي إليهم في المسجد وغيره ؛ منهم : حسان بن رواحة. وأفرد البسملة عن الشعر ولم يأت بها نظماً كما فعل الشاطبي في من الطويل] قوله (۱) : بدأت ببسم الله في النَّظم أولا تبارك رحماناً رحيماً ومويلا لأنه يعسر الإتيان على هيئتها من غير تغيير، بخلاف الحمدلة، ولأنه خلاف الأولى.
The disagreement occurred regarding the dislike of opening with it and its absence; and the preponderant view is the saying of the majority that it is recommended to open with it as long as it is not prohibited or disliked. And every poem that contains prophethood, Islam, judgment, guidance, noble manners, incitement to obedience, or avoidance of disobedience – then its composition, recitation, and listening are obedience, because it is a means to obedience. Indeed, it is authentically reported that the Chosen One used to listen to poets in the mosque and elsewhere; among them: Hassan bin Thabit. And he singled out the basmala from poetry and did not bring it as verse, as al-Shatibi did in [the meter] al-Tawil. He said (1): 'I begin with Bismillah in the verse first, blessed, merciful, and a helper' because it is difficult to bring it in its form without change, unlike the hamdala, and because it is contrary to what is preferable.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on opening poetry with the basmala. It begins by noting the disagreement (al-khilaf) on whether it is disliked (karaha) to open with it. The preponderant view (al-rajih) is that of the majority: it is recommended (istihbab) as long as the content is not prohibited (haram) or disliked (makruh). The author then lists types of poetry that are considered acts of obedience (ta'a): poetry containing themes of prophethood, Islam, judgment (al-hukm), guidance (al-huda), noble morals (makarim al-akhlaq), encouragement to obey, or avoidance of sin. Such poetry – its composition, recitation, and listening – is obedience because it is a means to obedience. This is supported by the authentic report that the Prophet (peace be upon him) listened to poets like Hassan ibn Thabit in the mosque and elsewhere. Finally, the author explains why the basmala itself is not included as a verse in poetry: because it is difficult to incorporate it without changing its form, unlike the hamdala (الحمد لله) which can be versified easily. Al-Shatibi did include it, but doing so is contrary to what is preferable (khilaf al-awla).

Key Terms
بسملة
حمدلة
استحباب
كراهة
طاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the basmala is always to be avoided in poetry and that opening with it is always disliked.
Correction: The majority holds that it is recommended to open with the basmala as long as the poetic content is not prohibited or disliked. The basmala itself is encouraged in permissible poems.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on opening poetry with the basmala according to the majority?
A) Forbidden
B) Recommended
C) Disliked
D) Obligatory

Key Takeaway
The basmala is recommended at the start of poetry, and poetry with virtuous themes is considered obedience due to its content and the Prophet's practice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage with poetry or other creative expressions, do you consciously evaluate their content against the virtuous themes mentioned (prophethood, Islam, noble morals)? If not, how can you start doing so today to turn your listening into an act of obedience?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) listened to poetry in the mosque. Does your own attitude towards poetry reflect that acceptance, or do you dismiss it as something worldly? What specific change in your consumption or creation of poetry would bring you closer to the Prophet's example?
?
The basmala is recommended at the start of poetry, yet many of us begin our creative works without conscious remembrance of Allah. What would it look like for you to intentionally open your writing, speech, or art with 'Bismillah' from now on, and how might that transform your relationship with your own creativity?

Why the Basmala's Verb is Delayed and Specified
Explanation of grammatical reasoning for delay and specification, and enumeration of qasr types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the verb in the Basmala is delayed and specified, and how this grammatical structure refutes polytheistic beliefs through three types of qasr.

Core Idea
The Basmala's grammar fights shirk by delaying the verb and restricting blessing to Allah alone.

The Basmala is not just a phrase; its grammatical structure carries deep theological meaning. In this passage, the commentator explains why the verb is delayed and the action specified, and introduces the three types of qasr used to counter different misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the grammatical terms like 'متأخر' and 'خاص'.
2
Understand the reason for delay
The verb is delayed because presenting the object first indicates exclusivity (اختصاص). This means blessing is restricted to Allah's name.
This refutes the polytheists who began with their gods' names for blessing.
3
Understand the reason for specification
The action is specified so that the Basmala is the beginning of every act, ensuring blessing covers all parts of the action.
There are two reasons given: the Basmala as a start, and comprehensive blessing.
4
Examine the three types of qasr
Learn the three types of restriction (قصر إفراد, قصر قلب, قصر تعيين) and their respective targets: polytheist, believer in opposite, and skeptic.
Each type addresses a specific misconception about who blessing comes from.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this grammatical analysis refutes polytheists and establishes tawhid. The structure of the Basmala itself is a declaration of Allah's exclusive right to be sought for blessing.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومتأخّراً؛ لأن تقديم المعمول يفيد الاختصاص، وخاصّاً؛ لأنَّ كلَّ شارع في شيء ينبغي له أن يقدر ما جعلت البسملة مبدأ له، ولإفادة حصول البركة لجميع أجزاء الفعل.
فهو بطريق الحمل على الفعل، ولما في تقدير الاسم من زيادة الإضمار؛ لأَنَّ المحذوف حينئذ عدة كلمات : المضاف والمضاف إليه، ومتعلق الجار والمجرور ؛ بخلاف أؤلف ؛ فإنَّه مع فاعله المستقر فيه كلمتان.

ما لو قدَّره من مادة الابتداء ؛ فإنَّه ليس خاصاً بالمشروع فيه، ولا عاماً

And delayed because presenting the object benefits exclusivity, and specific because every start-er of something should estimate that the Basmala is made a beginning for it, and for the benefit of obtaining blessing for all parts of the act. So it is by way of attribution to the verb, and because in estimating the noun there is an increase of implication; for the omitted then is several words: the genitive and the genitive-possessed, and the attachment of the preposition and its object; unlike 'I write', for it with its agent settled in it are two words. He said: 'And delayed', i.e. from the Basmala; 'because presenting the object benefits exclusivity', i.e. it benefits restriction of blessing in the work to His name, Exalted; so the ba' enters upon the restricted-to; because the polytheists used to begin with the names of their gods, saying: 'In the name of al-Lat and al-'Uzza' for blessing not exclusivity, due to their acknowledgment of blessing by His name, Exalted, so the monotheist refuted them. And this restriction is either restriction of disjunction, and it is addressed to the believer in association; or restriction of reversal, and it is addressed to the believer in the opposite of the ruling; or restriction of specification, and it is addressed to the sceptic. He said: 'Because every starter of something', i.e. authorship or other. He said: 'And for the benefit of obtaining blessing' is a second cause for estimating it as specific; i.e. in estimating the attachment as specific there is specification of blessing to the commencement of it and generalization of its parts, unlike if he estimated it from the material of beginning; for then it is not specific to the commenced matter, nor general to its parts, but limited to blessing at the beginning, so reflect.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage explains the grammatical reasoning behind the basmala's construction. It begins by stating that the verb is delayed (متأخراً) and the action specified (خاصاً). The delay is because presenting the object (المعمول) indicates exclusivity (الاختصاص). The specification is because every starter of something should make the basmala its beginning, and to ensure blessing covers all parts of the action. The text then contrasts estimating the verb with estimating 'اسم' (name), showing that the latter requires more implied elements. The commentary then clarifies 'متأخراً' by explaining that the delay is relative to the basmala, and the exclusivity is about restricting blessing to Allah's name, refuting the polytheists' practice. The types of قصر (restriction) are then enumerated: قصر إفراد for those who associate partners, قصر قلب for those who hold the opposite, and قصر تعيين for the skeptic. Finally, the commentary expands on the two causes for specifying the action: one is that the basmala is made the start of every act, and the other is to achieve comprehensive blessing for all parts of the act, as opposed to merely beginning with it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Basmala's verb is simply omitted without significance; in fact, its delay and specification carry theological and grammatical weight.
Correction: The delay and specification are deliberate to indicate exclusivity of blessing to Allah and to ensure blessing covers all parts of the action.

Key Takeaway
The grammatical structure of the Basmala is designed to assert that all blessing is from Allah alone, refuting polytheists through three types of restriction.

The Ba' in the Basmala: Isti'ana or Mulabasa?
Discussion on the meaning of the ba' in the basmala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ba' in the basmala is interpreted as indicating accompaniment (mulabasa) rather than help (isti'ana), and the theological concern behind this preference.

Core Idea
The ba' in the basmala is for accompaniment of blessing, not for instrumental help.

When we say 'Bismillah', what does the ba' mean? Scholars have debated whether it indicates using God's name as a means (isti'ana) or accompanying the action with blessing (mulabasa). This lesson explores the arguments and why the latter is preferred to preserve the reverence for Allah's name.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text and its literal translation to grasp the original argument.
Focus on the two interpretations: isti'ana and mulabasa.
2
Understand the critique of isti'ana
Notice the objection: interpreting the ba' as isti'ana implies that Allah's name is used as a means, which is theologically problematic.
3
See why mulabasa is preferred
The alternative of az-Zamakhshari avoids this by making the ba' indicate accompaniment of blessing, not direct instrumentality.
Reflect on the nuance: the companion is the blessing, not the name itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والباء للاستعانة، أو للمصاحبة على وجه التَّبَرُّك .
قوله : ( والباء للاستعانة باء الاستعانة هي الداخلة على الواسطة بين الفاعل ومفعوله ؛ ككتبت بالقلم، قال بعضهم : وفي جعلها للاستعانة إبهام أنَّ اسم الله مقصود لغيره لا لذاته؛ فالأولى قول الزمخشري : إنَّها للملابسة; أي: للمصاحبة (۱) ؛ أؤلف مصاحباً كل بيت ببركة هذا الاسم، فالمصاحب البركة ؛ لأنَّ الاسم لم يصاحب كل بيت، فتدبَّر .
And the ba' is for help, or for accompaniment on the basis of blessing. He said: 'And the ba' for help: the ba' of help enters upon the intermediary between the agent and its object, like “I wrote with the pen.” Some said: In making it for help there is an implication that the name of Allah is intended for other than itself, not for its own sake; so the preferable is the saying of az-Zamakhshari: it is for attachment (mulabasa), i.e. for accompaniment; I write, accompanying every verse with the blessing of this name, so the companion is the blessing, because the name does not accompany every verse, so reflect.'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage discusses the meaning of the letter ba' in the basmala. It first states two possibilities: the ba' is for isti'ana (help) or for musahaba (accompaniment) on the basis of tabarruk (blessing). The commentary then explains the ba' of help: it enters upon the intermediary between the agent and its object, as in 'I wrote with the pen.' Some scholars criticized this interpretation, saying it implies that Allah's name is intended as a means to something else, not for its own sake. Therefore, the preferable view is that of az-Zamakhshari: the ba' is for mulabasa (attachment or close association), i.e., accompaniment. The meaning becomes: 'I write while accompanied by the blessing of this name.' The commentary then clarifies that the one being accompanied is the blessing, not the name itself, because the name does not literally accompany every verse. This preserves the theological propriety of the basmala.

المصاحبة
الملابسة
التبرك
الزمخشري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ba' in the basmala indicates that Allah's name is used as a tool or means (isti'ana), implying the name is intended for something other than itself.
Correction: The ba' actually indicates accompaniment (mulabasa) of the action with the blessing of the name, not instrumental use.

Checkpoint

B) Because isti'ana is grammatically incorrect
C) Because the Prophet recommended it
D) Because it is a shorter construction

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson warns against treating Allah's name as a means to an end. Search your heart: in your prayers or daily life, have you ever used Allah's name as a tool to secure a desired outcome, rather than as an expression of reliance and blessing? What step can you take today to realign your intention?

The author says 'the companion is the blessing,' not the name itself. This is a subtle but profound distinction. How does this affect your understanding of bringing Allah into your actions? Is your relationship with Allah's name one of intimacy or utility? Be honest.

Converting Verbs for Sifa Mushabbaha
Al-Sawi explains the conversion of transitive verbs to intransitive for sifa mushabbaha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how transitive verbs are converted to intransitive to derive the descriptive adjective (sifa mushabbaha) for divine attributes.

Core Idea
To derive a descriptive adjective, the verb must first be made intransitive.

Before we can attribute qualities like 'Merciful' to God, we must understand the grammatical rule for forming the descriptive adjective. Al-Sawi explains that transitive verbs are converted to intransitive form so that the attribute focuses on the agent alone, not an external object. This lesson unpacks that transformation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on verb conversion
Read the Arabic passage that explains making a verb intransitive by transferring it to the form 'fu'ila'. Then read the translation.
The passage uses technical terms like 'tanzil' and 'lazim'.
2
Understand the necessity
The passage states that this conversion is necessary because the sifa mushabbaha is only derived from intransitive verbs. Ask yourself why that matters for describing God.
Ibn Malik's verse is quoted as evidence.
3
Identify the two ways
Notice the two methods: either by intending to affirm the attribute for the agent only, or by changing the verb to intransitive form. Both achieve the same goal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأن يُقصد إثباته للفاعل فقط من غير اعتبار تعلقه بمفعول، وإما بجعله لازماً بأن ينقل إلى فعل - بالضم - وإنَّما احتيج لذلك؛ لأنَّ الصفة المشبَّهة إِنَّما تُصاغ من اللازم.
والرَّحمة رقَّةُ القلب؛ أي رأفته، وهي تستلزم التَّفضَّلَ والإحسان، فهو غايتها وهي مبدؤه، فيراد منها هنا الغاية
قوله : ( بأن يقصد إثباته) بيان وتصوير للتّنزيل .
قوله : (بأن ينقل إلى فعل) : تصوير لجعله لازماً؛ لأنَّ (فعل) بالضَّمِّ، لا يكون إلا لازماً .
قوله : (وإنَّما احتيج لذلك اسم الإشارة عائد على التّنزيل أو التَّحويل .
قوله : (إِنَّما تصاغ من اللازم) : أي : لقول ابن مالك (۱): وصَوْغُـهَـا مـن لازم لـحـاضـر . . . . . . .
قوله : (والرَّحمة : رقّة القلب) : أي : في أصل وضع
قوله : (فهو غايتها) : أي : ثمرتها .
وقوله : (وهي مبدؤه) : أي : منشؤه. اللُّغة.
قوله : ( فيراد منها هنا الغاية) : أي : ففيه مجاز مرسل من إطلاق السَّبب (١) ألفية ابن مالك ( ص ٦٣) ، وتتمته : كطاهر القلب جميل الظاهر).
That he intends to affirm it (the attribute) for the agent only without considering its relation to an object, or by making it intransitive by transferring it to the form 'fu'ila' (passive) with dammah. And indeed there is need for that because the descriptive adjective (al-sifa al-mushabbaha) is only derived from intransitive verbs. And mercy (al-rahma) is tenderness of the heart (riqqat al-qalb); i.e., his compassion, and it necessitates beneficence (al-tafaddul) and benevolence (al-ihsan). So He is its ultimate end (ghayatuha) and it is its origin (mabda'uhu). So what is intended here is the ultimate end (al-ghaya).
His saying: 'that he intends to affirm it' is an explanation and depiction of the tanzil (making transitive into intransitive). His saying: 'by transferring it to the form fu'ila' is a depiction of making it intransitive because the form fu'ila with dammah is only intransitive. His saying: 'and indeed there is need for that' – the demonstrative pronoun refers back to tanzil or transformation. His saying: 'is only derived from intransitive' – i.e., due to the saying of Ibn Malik: 'Its derivation from an intransitive for the agent...' His saying: 'and mercy: tenderness of the heart' – i.e., in the original lexical meaning. His saying: 'so He is its ultimate end' – i.e., its fruit. And his saying: 'and it is its origin' – i.e., its source. Language. His saying: 'so what is intended here is the ultimate end' – i.e., in it there is a synecdoche (majaz mursal) of using the cause for the effect, and the full line of Ibn Malik's Alfiyya: 'like a pure heart and a beautiful appearance.'
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a grammatical and theological point. It begins by explaining the linguistic rule for deriving the descriptive adjective (sifa mushabbaha) from an intransitive verb. To use an attribute like 'merciful' for God, the verb must be made intransitive (lazim) because the active participle of transitive verbs is derived from the object, but here the attribute is to be affirmed for the agent (God) without relation to a specific object. Then it defines 'rahma' (mercy) in its original linguistic sense as 'tenderness of the heart' which implies compassion and leads to beneficence. Since God cannot have a heart or emotional tenderness, the actual meaning intended is the ultimate consequence (al-ghaya), which is the act of bestowing favor (al-tafaddul wa al-ihsan). Thus, the expression is a majaz mursal (synecdoche) where the cause is mentioned but the effect is meant. The hashia elaborates each phrase, confirming the grammatical transformation and citing Ibn Malik's Alfiyya as evidence for deriving adjectives from intransitives.

Key Terms
تنزيل
لازم
صفة مشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it necessary to convert a transitive verb to intransitive when deriving a sifa mushabbaha?
A) Because Arabic does not allow transitive verbs in descriptions
B) Because sifa mushabbaha is only derived from intransitive verbs
C) To make the verb passive
D) To emphasize the object

Key Takeaway
To describe God with attributes derived from transitive verbs, the verb must first be made intransitive so the focus remains on the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Meaning of Rahma When Attributed to God
Al-Sawi explains the metaphorical attribution of rahma to God as its ultimate end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'rahma' (mercy) when attributed to God is interpreted metaphorically as its effect (beneficence), not as emotional tenderness.

Core Idea
God's 'rahma' means beneficence, not tenderness.

When we say 'God is Merciful', we must be careful not to attribute human emotions to Him. Al-Sawi explains that the word 'rahma' literally means 'tenderness of heart', but for God it is used metaphorically to signify the resultant act of bestowing favor. This lesson clarifies that figurative usage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on rahma
Read the Arabic passage defining rahma and its parts. Then read the translation.
Notice the terms 'ghaya' (ultimate end) and 'mabda' (origin).
2
Analyze the relationship
The passage says rahma necessitates beneficence, so rahma is the cause and beneficence is the effect. For God, the cause cannot be literal, so the effect is intended.
This is a majaz mursal (synecdoche).
3
Understand the reason for metaphor
Since God cannot have a tender heart, the literal sense is impossible. The intended meaning is the consequence (al-ghaya), which is beneficence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

When you recite 'Bismillah ir-Rahman ir-Rahim', do you ever imagine a subjective feeling in God? After this lesson, will you pause to mean that He is the Bestower of all favors? How can you train your mind to shift from emotion to action when thinking of His mercy?
?
Consider a time you begged for God's mercy in distress. Did you relate to Him as a sympathetic friend or as the One who actually provides relief? What does this lesson change about how you ask for mercy?
?
The lesson shows that God's mercy is His giving, not His feeling. Does your trust in Him depend more on your perception of His 'kindness' or on the sheer fact that He is the source of every good? How will you act differently today to rely on the reality of His beneficence?
