Interpreting the Basmala: Majaz Mursal vs Isti'ara Tamthiliyya
Al-Sawi presents the objection to and defense of isti'ara tamthiliyya in the Basmala and recommends majaz mursal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between interpreting the Basmala as an allegorical metaphor (isti'ara tamthiliyya) versus a synecdoche (majaz mursal), and why the latter is preferred.

Core Idea
The safest interpretation of the Basmala is as a synecdoche, not an allegorical metaphor.

When we say 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim', how should we understand it? Is it a metaphorical scene likening God to a king, or a simpler figurative transfer? This passage from al-Sawi presents an objection against the allegorical metaphor interpretation and its answer, ultimately recommending the synecdoche as the better approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it begins with an objection and then provides an answer.
The footnote identifies the scholar mentioned.
2
Understand the objection
The objection raises three points against isti'ara tamthiliyya: it requires compound expressions, the term 'state' is not permitted for God, and it implies disrespect by comparing God to a weaker entity.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer defends the metaphor by focusing on the main part of the compound, and allows the use of 'state' for teaching. It also argues that the comparison is not absolutely disrespectful.
What does 'أغلبي' mean?
4
Note the final recommendation
Despite the defense, the text recommends majaz mursal as the safer interpretation. Reflect on why this might be.
Majaz mursal is a type of figurative language where the connection is not similarity but another relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على المسبب، وذكر حفيد السعد (۱) أنَّ في الكلام استعارة تمثيليَّة؛ بأن يقال : شبَّه حال المولى مع خلقه في الإنعام بجلائل النعم ودقائقها بحال مَلِكٍ مع رعيَّته واستعيرت الهيئة الدالة على المشبه به للمشبَّه .
وأورد عليه : أنَّ الاستعارة التَّمثيليَّة لا تكون إلَّا في المركَّبات، وإطلاق الحال على الله لم يرد إذن به، وأنَّ الرَّحمن لم يستعمل في غيره تعالى، وأنَّ المشبه به أقوى؛ وهو إساءة أدب
وأجيب بأنَّه اقتصر على الجزء الأهم من المركَّب؛ إذ هو مركب : بحسب الأصل : فإنَّ الأصل : ملك رحمن رحيم، وإطلاق الحال جائز لضرورة التّعليم، والحقُّ ثبوت مجازات لا حقائق لها، وكون المشبه به أقوى أغلبي، وبعد هذا كله فالأحسن الاقتصار على كونه مجازاً مرسلاً .
تعالى .
قوله : (لاستحالتها) : أي : رقّة القلب.
قوله : ( أي : الثَّابت له التَّفضُّل ... إلخ) : بيان للمعنى المراد اللائق به تعالى .
(۱) هو شيخ الإسلام أحمد بن يحيى بن محمد التفتازاني يعرف بـ(حفيد السعد)، من فقهاء الشافعية كان قاضي هراة ثلاثين سنة، له مصنفات عدة؛ منها: «الدر النضيد في مجموعة الحفيد»، و«حاشية على شرح التلخيص، وغيرها، توفي الله سنة (٩١٦هـ) ينظر «الأعلام» (۲۷۰/۱).
Upon the caused (al-musabbab). And the grandson of al-Sa'd mentioned that in the speech there is an allegorical metaphor (isti'ara tamthiliyya): that it is said: the state of the Master with His creation in bestowing great and delicate favors is likened to the state of a king with his subjects, and the figure indicating the compared-to is borrowed for the compared. And it is objected to him that allegorical metaphor occurs only in compound expressions, and applying the term 'state' (al-hal) to God has not been permitted. And that 'al-Rahman' is not used for other than Him, and that the compared-to is stronger, and that is disrespectful. And it is answered that he limited himself to the most important part of the compound, because originally it is a compound: 'a merciful king'. And applying the term 'state' is permissible due to the necessity of teaching. And the truth is that there are metaphorical expressions without literal meanings, and the fact that the compared-to is stronger is not absolute. After all this, the best is to restrict it to being a synecdoche (majaz mursal). Exalted is He. His saying: 'Due to its impossibility for Him' – i.e., tenderness of heart. His saying: 'i.e., the one who has beneficence and benevolence abundantly' – an explanation of the intended meaning befitting Him, Exalted. (1) He is Shaykh al-Islam Ahmad ibn Yahya ibn Muhammad al-Taftazani, known as 'Hafid al-Sa'd', among the Shafi'i jurists, he was the qadi of Herat for thirty years, he has many works including 'al-Durr al-Nadid fi Majmu'at al-Hafid' and 'Hashiya 'ala Sharh al-Talkhis', etc., he died in the year 916 H. See 'al-A'lam' 1/270.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion about how to interpret the phrase 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim'. The commentator mentions that Hafid al-Sa'd (the grandson of al-Sa'd) proposed that the phrase is an allegorical metaphor (isti'ara tamthiliyya), where the entire scene of God's generosity is likened to a king's generosity. An objection is raised on three grounds: (1) allegorical metaphors only apply to compound expressions, not single words like 'al-Rahman'; (2) calling it a 'state' (hal) of God is not permissible; (3) 'al-Rahman' is unique to God, so comparing Him to a king is disrespectful because the object of comparison (the king) is weaker. The answer is that the metaphor is not fully compound but focuses on the most important part, and the original compound is 'a merciful king'. The term 'state' is allowed for educational necessity. Moreover, many metaphorical usages have no literal counterpart, and the superiority of the object compared is not absolute. Finally, the author recommends that the safest interpretation is to treat it as a synecdoche (majaz mursal), i.e., using a cause to denote the effect. The footnote identifies Hafid al-Sa'd as Ahmad ibn Yahya al-Taftazani.

Key Terms
استعارة تمثيلية
مجاز مرسل
مركب
حال
إساءة أدب
أغلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Basmala can only be interpreted literally or that any figurative interpretation is disrespectful.
Correction: This passage shows that figurative interpretations like isti'ara tamthiliyya and majaz mursal are considered, and the latter is recommended as safe.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the recommended interpretation of the Basmala?
A) It is a literal expression
B) It is an allegorical metaphor (isti'ara tamthiliyya)
C) It is a synecdoche (majaz mursal)
D) It is not possible to classify

Key Takeaway
The Basmala is best understood as a synecdoche (majaz mursal) rather than an allegorical metaphor, to avoid potential disrespect while acknowledging figurative language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Basmala, do you ever pause to consider whether your mental image of God's mercy might inadvertently diminish His transcendence? What safer understanding from this lesson will you actively adopt starting today?
?
The scholars in this passage took the objection of 'disrespect' (isa'at adab) so seriously that they chose a less vivid interpretation. Where in your own speech about Allah have you prioritized rhetorical impact over reverent caution, and what will you change?
?
You have now seen that a metaphorical interpretation of God's attributes can be defended but the safer path is preferred. Are you willing to examine your own theology and set aside emotionally appealing but potentially risky analogies for the sake of protecting God's honor?

Understanding the Nickname 'al-Mashhur' and al-Dardir's Lineage
Explains the nickname 'al-Mashhur' and its relation to al-Dardir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and pronunciation of the nickname 'al-Mashhur' as it applies to the author al-Dardir.

Core Idea
The nickname 'al-Mashhur' refers to the author's famous grandfather, and even the grandchildren are known by it.

This lesson clarifies a common epithet used for the author of our text. Before diving into theological discussions, the commentator explains who 'al-Mashhur' is and why the author is called that. Understanding this lineage helps us appreciate the scholarly tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation to understand the basic claim about the nickname.
2
Analyze the pronunciation
Note how the name 'al-Dardir' is pronounced with specific vowel markings.
The commentary gives fatha and kasra positions.
3
Reflect on lineage
Consider why the author is known by his grandfather's nickname rather than his own name.
This reflects common scholarly practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(المشهور) أي الذي اشتهر (بـ) لقب جَدِّه (الدَّردير) بفتح الدال الأولى وكسر الثانية بينهما راء ساكنة وكذا اشتهر أولاد الجدِّ كلّهـ بهذا اللقب. مطلب في بيان معنى الحمد (الحمد الله) هو وما بعده إلى آخر الكتاب قوله : (عطف بيان :أي: لأنَّ نعت المعرفة إذا تقدم عليها يعرب بحسب العوامل، فلذا أعرب (راجي) فاعل (يقول) وتعرب هي منه بدلاً أو عطف بیان وحكمة تقديم ! على المنعوت الاعتناء برجاء رحمة الله، ففي الحديث: «إنَّ عافيتك أوسعُ لي، ورحمتك أرجـى عـنـدي مـن عملي» (١)، وإنما ذكر اسمه على عادة جمهور المؤلفين من تسميتهم من تسميتهم أنفسهم أوائل كتبهم ؛ ليرغب الطَّالب في الكتاب؛ لأنَّ الكتاب المجهول في صاحبه غير مرغوب فيه ولا موثوق به. قوله: (الحمد الله): لما افتتح بالبسملة افتتاحاً حقيقياً افتتح بالحمدلة افتتاحاً إضافياً، وهو ما تقدَّم على الشروع في المقصود بالذات؛ جمعاً بين حديثي البسملة والحمدلة.
(al-Mashhur) i.e., the one who became famous by the nickname of his grandfather al-Dardir, with the first daal having a fatha and the second a kasra and between them a saakin raa, and likewise all the grandfather's children became famous by this nickname. A section on explaining the meaning of al-hamd: (al-hamd li-Allah) is what follows until the end of the book. His saying: (as an explanatory apposition) i.e., because the attribute of a definite noun when it precedes it is declined according to grammatical factors, therefore (raajin) is declined as the subject (fa'il) of (yaqool), and it is declined as a replacement (badal) or explanatory apposition from it. And the wisdom of giving priority to the first over the described is to pay attention to hoping for the mercy of Allah, as in the hadith: 'Indeed Your forgiveness is more spacious for me, and Your mercy is more hopeful to me than my own action.' And he mentioned his name following the custom of most authors who name themselves at the beginnings of their books so that the student will desire the book; because a book whose author is unknown is neither desirable nor trustworthy. His saying: (al-hamd li-Allah): since he opened with the basmala as a real opening, he opened with the hamdala as an additional opening, which precedes the beginning of the intended subject in order to combine between the two hadiths of the basmala and the hamdala.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is an editorial and grammatical commentary on the opening words of the book. First, it explains the nickname 'al-Mashhur' as referring to someone known by his grandfather's name al-Dardir, with pronunciation details. Then it introduces a section (matalib) on the meaning of 'al-hamd li-Allah'. The commentary clarifies that 'al-hamd li-Allah' is the text under discussion and that the author's phrase 'raaji' (hoping) is in the nominative as the subject of 'yaqool' (he says). The wisdom of placing 'raaji' before the described noun is to emphasize hope in God's mercy, citing a hadith. The author mentions his own name at the beginning following the common practice of authors to make the book appealing and trustworthy. Finally, it explains that the basmala is the 'real' opening, while the hamdala is an 'additional' opening placed before the main subject, done to follow both prophetic traditions about beginning with the basmala and with praise.

الدردير

Key Takeaway
The author is called 'al-Mashhur' because he is known by his grandfather al-Dardir's famous nickname, and this applies to all descendants.

The Meaning and Grammar of 'Al-Hamd li-Allah'
Grammatical and theological explanation of 'al-hamd li-Allah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical analysis of 'al-hamd li-Allah' and the wisdom of prioritizing 'raaji' (hoping) in the author's phrase.

Core Idea
The phrase 'al-hamd li-Allah' is followed by a grammatical explanation and a hadith emphasizing hope in Allah's mercy.

This lesson dives into the first substantive words of the text after the basmala. The commentator explains that 'al-hamd li-Allah' is an explanatory apposition and discusses the case of the word 'raaji'. He then quotes a prophetic hadith to show why hope in Allah's mercy is prioritized over self-reliance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to identify the key grammatical points.
2
Understand the grammatical analysis
Note how 'raaji' is in the nominative and how the phrase functions as an explanatory apposition.
The term 'عطف بيان' means explanatory apposition.
3
Analyze the hadith
Reflect on the hadith's message: Allah's mercy is more hopeful than one's own actions.
This justifies the placement of 'raaji' before 'al-kitab'.
4
Connect to theology
Consider how this grammatical choice reflects a theological emphasis on divine mercy.

The Author's Naming Custom and the Relationship Between Basmala and Hamdala
Explains author's self-naming and the dual opening with basmala and hamdala.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author mentions his name at the beginning of the book and how the basmala and hamdala are related as real and additional openings.

Core Idea
Authors mention their names to make their books desirable, and the hamdala serves as an additional opening to combine two prophetic traditions.

This lesson covers two distinct but related points: the author's practice of naming himself at the start, and the reason for beginning with the basmala (real opening) and then the hamdala (additional opening). Both reflect scholarly conventions and a desire to follow the Prophet's guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the two topics.
2
Analyze the author's custom
Note why the author includes his name: to make the book desirable and trustworthy.
This is a common practice among scholars.
3
Understand the relationship between basmala and hamdala
Learn that the basmala is the real opening, while the hamdala is an additional opening that precedes the main subject.
This combines two hadiths: one about starting with basmala, another about starting with hamd.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider the importance of following both traditions and how it sets the tone for the book.

Correction: Both are openings, but basmala is real (iftitah haqiqi), hamdala is additional (iftitah idafi).

Origin and Recommendation of 'Al-Hamdu Lillah'
Al-Sawi traces the derivation of 'al-hamdu lillah' from a verbal sentence and cites al-Fakahani's recommendation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' and the recommended contexts for using it according to al-Fakahani.

Core Idea
The phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' evolved from a verbal sentence to a nominal one to express permanence.

In this passage, al-Sawi explains the grammatical origin of the well-known phrase 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'. He traces its evolution from a fuller verbal construction to its current fixed form, highlighting the grammatical changes that convey deeper meaning. Then he quotes al-Fākahānī on the broad recommendation to begin with this phrase in various significant contexts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the sequence of changes described.
2
Trace the grammatical derivation
Identify each step: the original verbal sentence, deletion of the verb, accusative then nominative shift, and addition of the definite article. Understand why each change occurs.
3
Note the recommended contexts
Identify the roles and situations mentioned by al-Fakahani for beginning with the phrase. Reflect on how this practice extends beyond mere recitation.
Also note the inclusion of salawat upon the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصل الحمد الله : أحمد حمد الله؛ ثم حذف الفعل لدلالة المصدر عليه، فبقي (حمد الله) ثمَّ عدل به من النَّصب إلى الرفع؛ لدلالة الثبوت والدوام فصار (حمد الله ثمَّ أدخلت الألف واللام، قال الفاكهاني في شرح الرسالة»: (ويستحبُّ الابتداء بها لكل مصنف، ومدرس، وخطيب، وخاطب ومتزوّج، ومزوّج، وبين يدي سائر الأمور المهمة، وكذا الصَّلاة على رسول الله ) .
And the origin of 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' is 'aḥmadu ḥamda lillāh' (I praise a praising for God); then the verb was deleted due to the indication of the verbal noun upon it, so 'ḥamda lillāh' remained; then it was changed from the accusative to the nominative to indicate permanence and continuity, so it became 'ḥamdu lillāh'; then the definite article was inserted. Al-Fākahānī said in his commentary on al-Risāla: 'It is recommended to begin with it for every author, teacher, preacher, suitor, groom, and marriage officiant, and before all important matters, and likewise the prayer upon the Messenger of God.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic derivation of the phrase 'الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ' (praise be to God). Al-Sawi explains that the original form was a complete verbal sentence: 'أَحْمَدُ حَمْدَ اللهِ' (I praise a praising for God). The verb is then dropped because the verbal noun (ḥamd) indicates the action. Thus it becomes 'حَمْدَ اللهِ' (with ḥamd in the accusative as a verbal noun). Then the case ending is changed from accusative (naṣb) to nominative (rafʿ) to indicate permanence and continuity (thubūt wa dawām), leading to 'حَمْدُ اللهِ'. Finally, the definite article 'al-' is added, resulting in 'الْحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ'. The passage then quotes al-Fākahānī from his commentary on al-Risāla, stating that it is recommended (mustaḥabb) to begin with the basmalah and hamdalah for various roles: authors, teachers, preachers, suitors, grooms, marriage officiants, and all important matters, and also to send prayers upon the Messenger of God (i.e., to include the ṣalāh).

Key Terms

حذف الفعل
مصدر
نصب
رفع
ثبوت
دوام
استحباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-hamdu lillah' is directly from the Quran without grammatical evolution.
Correction: This passage shows it evolved from a verbal sentence through deletion and case changes.

Checkpoint

B) Changing from accusative to nominative
C) Removing the article
D) Adding a conjunction

Key Takeaway

Preference of Basmalah over Hamdalah
Author explains preference of basmalah over hamdalah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that basmalah (بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم) is the primary opening over hamdalah (الحمد لله), and how to harmonize apparent conflicts between traditions.

Core Idea
Basmalah is the real beginning; hamdalah is additional.

This lesson examines a key methodological issue in Islamic composition: which formula should open a book or speech? The author presents arguments for preferring basmalah over hamdalah, citing Quranic practice and hadith strength. Despite the apparent conflict, he notes there are other ways to reconcile different traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrases
Basmalah (بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم) and hamdalah (الحمد لله) are both common openings. Notice how the author distinguishes them by type of beginning.
The author calls basmalah the 'real beginning' and hamdalah 'additional'.
2
Understand the author's interpretation
The author says basmalah is the true, essential opening, while hamdalah is a secondary or additional praise.
This resolves the apparent conflict between the two formulas.
3
Examine the evidence
Two pieces of evidence are given: the Quran begins with basmalah, and the hadith about basmalah is stronger than the one about hamdalah. Read the hadith quoted: 'Every speech not beginning with praise of God is cut off.'
The hadith mentions 'praise' (hamd), yet the author uses it to support basmalah—this needs careful understanding.
4
Note the other solutions
The author says there are other well-known ways to resolve the contradiction. This shows the issue is not absolute, and scholars have multiple valid approaches.
This reflects the methodological richness of Islamic scholarship.

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why the author (or the original text) leads with basmalah rather than hamdalah. The hadith quoted says that any speech that does not begin with praise of God is 'cut off' or defective. Despite this, there are other well-known ways to reconcile the apparent conflict between different narrations on this topic.

Derivation of 'Al-Hamdu Lillah' and Its Recommended Use
Author traces the derivation of الحمد لله and quotes al-Fakahani on its recommended use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' and why it is recommended to begin important matters with it.

Core Idea
A simple phrase carries a deep linguistic history of permanence and praise.

The phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' is more than a mere expression—it is a verbal gem refined through Arabic grammar to convey eternal praise. This lesson unpacks its derivation from a verb sentence and highlights the scholarly recommendation to use it as an opening for all important affairs.

The Skillful Opening (Barāʻat al-Istihlāl)
Al-Sawi defines barāʻat al-istihlāl and applies it to the poem's opening.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what barāʻat al-istihlāl is and how the opening of the poem indicates its main theme of tawhid.

Core Idea
A skillful opening hints at the author's purpose from the very first words.

Before diving into theological arguments, the poet begins with an elegant literary device that foreshadows the content. Al-Sawi unpacks this device, showing how the names mentioned at the start are not arbitrary but point directly to the central theme of tawhid. This lesson explores the meaning and significance of barāʻat al-istihlāl.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on barāʻat al-istihlāl
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the first part that defines the literary device and connects it to the names given in the poem.
Notice how al-Sawi explains that the names 'hint at tawhid'.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
Learn the root meaning of 'barāʻah' (excellence) and 'istihlāl' (appearance). This helps grasp the full sense of the term.
Derivations are explained explicitly in the passage.
3
Consider the purpose of the device
Reflect on why an author might use such an opening. The passage says it is to 'indicate his purpose'. How does this prepare the reader?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (من براعة الاستهلال) :أي: لأنَّ هذه الأسماء تشعر بالتّوحيد الَّذي هو شارع فيه؛ لتضمنها العقائد، وبراعة الاستهلال هي أن يذكر المؤلّف أو غيره في أوّل كلامه ما يدلُّ على مقصوده، والبراعة من برع إذا تفوق على غيره، والاستهلال الظهور . قوله : (وأفضل الصَّلاة ... إلخ) : لمَّا حمد الله تعالى شكراً للنعمة؛ صلى على حبيبه ، لأنَّه الواسطة لنا في جميع النعم، أداء لبعض ما ؛ يجب له ، وعملاً بقوله عليه الصَّلاة والسلام : كلُّ كلام لا يُذكرُ اللهُ فيه فيبدأ به وبالصَّلاةِ عليَّ ؛ فهوَ أقطع ممحوقٌ من كل بركة» (١) ، والجملة خبريَّة لفظاً إنشائية معنى، فالمقصود بها إنشاء الدُّعاء بأنَّ الله يعظم سيّدنا محمّداً ويشرفه ويحيّيه بتحيَّة لائقة به كما يحيي بعضنا بعضاً، ولا يجوز أن تكون خبريَّة لفظاً ومعنى ؛ لأنَّ المخبر بأنَّ الله صلى عليه – أي : (۱) أورده الملا علي القاري في مرقاة المفاتيح (۳/۱) وعزاه للرهاوي عن أبي هريرة
His saying: (from skillful opening) i.e., because these names hint at tawhid which he is entering into, because they contain beliefs. And skillful opening is that the author or other mentions at the beginning of his speech what indicates his purpose. And 'barāʻah' is from 'baraʻa' meaning he excelled others, and 'istihlāl' means appearance. His saying: (And the most excellent of prayers ... etc.): When he praised Allah the Exalted out of gratitude for the blessing, he sent blessings upon His beloved, because he is the intermediary for us in all blessings, fulfilling some of what is due to him, and acting upon his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every speech in which Allah is not mentioned and begun with and with prayer upon me, is cut off, deprived of every blessing.' The sentence is declarative in form but performative in meaning. So what is intended by it is the performance of supplication that Allah magnify our master Muhammad, honor him, and greet him with a greeting befitting him, just as we greet one another. And it is not permissible for it to be declarative in form and meaning, because the one who reports that Allah blessed him—that is: (1) Reported by Mulla Ali al-Qari in Mirqat al-Mafatih (3/1) and attributed to al-Rahawi from Abu Hurayra.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage explains two topics: the concept of 'barāʻat al-istihlāl' (skillful opening) and the nature of the sentence 'wa-afḍalu al-ṣalāh ...' (and the most excellent of prayers).

First, al-Sawi comments on the poet's phrase 'min barāʻat al-istihlāl'. He explains that the names listed at the beginning of the poem (al-‘Aalim, etc.) hint at tawhid, which is the main subject of the poem. This is a literary device where the author's opening words indicate his intended purpose. 'Barāʻah' means excellence or surpassing others, and 'istihlāl' means appearing or beginning loudly. Thus, it is a 'skillful opening' that foreshadows the content.

Second, on the line about sending blessings upon the Prophet: al-Sawi explains that after praising Allah, the poet prays upon His beloved because the Prophet is the intermediary (al-wāsiṭah) for all blessings. This act fulfills part of the rights due to the Prophet and follows the hadith that every speech lacking mention of Allah and prayer upon the Prophet is devoid of blessing.

Crucially, al-Sawi clarifies that the sentence 'wa-afḍalu al-ṣalāh' is declarative in form (khabariyyah lafẓan) but performative in meaning (inshā'iyyah ma‘nan). It is not a report that God has blessed the Prophet, but rather a supplication asking God to bless and honor him. It is incorrect to interpret it as a mere statement of fact, because that would imply that the speaker is merely informing, whereas the context demands a petition.

Key Terms
براعة الاستهلال
البراعة
الاستهلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The poem's opening is a skillful literary device that immediately signals its primary theme of tawhid.

The Meaning of Salat and Salam: A Supplication
Al-Sawi analyzes the sentence structure and meaning of the blessing upon the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'And the most excellent of prayers' is grammatically a declaration but functionally a supplication.

Core Idea
The prayer upon the Prophet is a request for divine honor, not a factual statement.

After praising Allah, the poet sends blessings upon the Prophet. But is he simply reporting that Allah blesses him, or is he invoking blessings? Al-Sawi clarifies that the sentence is 'declarative in form, performative in meaning' — it is actually a supplication. This lesson unpacks that grammatical nuance and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the sentence 'wa-afḍalu al-ṣalāh...'
Focus on the part after the hadith. Read how al-Sawi describes the sentence as 'khabariyyah lafẓan inshā'iyyah maʿnan'.
This is the key grammatical point.
2
Understand why it is not a simple report
Al-Sawi gives two reasons: the context of supplication and the absurdity of merely reporting a known fact. Grasp why a report would be inappropriate here.
He says 'فالمقصود بها إنشاء الدعاء'.
3
Reflect on the role of the Prophet as intermediary
The passage states that the Prophet is 'الواسطة لنا في جميع النعم'. How does this relate to sending blessings?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (من براعة الاستهلال) :أي: لأنَّ هذه الأسماء تشعر بالتّوحيد الَّذي هو شارع فيه؛ لتضمنها العقائد، وبراعة الاستهلال هي أن يذكر المؤلّف أو غيره في أوّل كلامه ما يدلُّ على مقصوده، والبراعة من برع إذا تفوق على غيره، والاستهلال الظهور . قوله : (وأفضل الصَّلاة ... إلخ) : لمَّا حمد الله تعالى شكراً للنعمة؛ صلى على حبيبه ، لأنَّه الواسطة لنا في جميع النعم، أداء لبعض ما ؛ يجب له ، وعملاً بقوله عليه الصَّلاة والسلام : كلُّ كلام لا يُذكرُ اللهُ فيه فيبدأ به وبالصَّلاةِ عليَّ ؛ فهوَ أقطع ممحوقٌ من كل بركة» (١) ، والجملة خبريَّة لفظاً إنشائية معنى، فالمقصود بها إنشاء الدُّعاء بأنَّ الله يعظم سيّدنا محمّداً ويشرفه ويحيّيه بتحيَّة لائقة به كما يحيي بعضنا بعضاً، ولا يجوز أن تكون خبريَّة لفظاً ومعنى ؛ لأنَّ المخبر بأنَّ الله صلى عليه – أي : (۱) أورده الملا علي القاري في مرقاة المفاتيح (۳/۱) وعزاه للرهاوي عن أبي هريرة
His saying: (from skillful opening) i.e., because these names hint at tawhid which he is entering into, because they contain beliefs. And skillful opening is that the author or other mentions at the beginning of his speech what indicates his purpose. And 'barāʻah' is from 'baraʻa' meaning he excelled others, and 'istihlāl' means appearance. His saying: (And the most excellent of prayers ... etc.): When he praised Allah the Exalted out of gratitude for the blessing, he sent blessings upon His beloved, because he is the intermediary for us in all blessings, fulfilling some of what is due to him, and acting upon his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every speech in which Allah is not mentioned and begun with and with prayer upon me, is cut off, deprived of every blessing.' The sentence is declarative in form but performative in meaning. So what is intended by it is the performance of supplication that Allah magnify our master Muhammad, honor him, and greet him with a greeting befitting him, just as we greet one another. And it is not permissible for it to be declarative in form and meaning, because the one who reports that Allah blessed him—that is: (1) Reported by Mulla Ali al-Qari in Mirqat al-Mafatih (3/1) and attributed to al-Rahawi from Abu Hurayra.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage explains two topics: the concept of 'barāʻat al-istihlāl' (skillful opening) and the nature of the sentence 'wa-afḍalu al-ṣalāh ...' (and the most excellent of prayers).

First, al-Sawi comments on the poet's phrase 'min barāʻat al-istihlāl'. He explains that the names listed at the beginning of the poem (al-‘Aalim, etc.) hint at tawhid, which is the main subject of the poem. This is a literary device where the author's opening words indicate his intended purpose. 'Barāʻah' means excellence or surpassing others, and 'istihlāl' means appearing or beginning loudly. Thus, it is a 'skillful opening' that foreshadows the content.

Second, on the line about sending blessings upon the Prophet: al-Sawi explains that after praising Allah, the poet prays upon His beloved because the Prophet is the intermediary (al-wāsiṭah) for all blessings. This act fulfills part of the rights due to the Prophet and follows the hadith that every speech lacking mention of Allah and prayer upon the Prophet is devoid of blessing.

Crucially, al-Sawi clarifies that the sentence 'wa-afḍalu al-ṣalāh' is declarative in form (khabariyyah lafẓan) but performative in meaning (inshā'iyyah ma‘nan). It is not a report that God has blessed the Prophet, but rather a supplication asking God to bless and honor him. It is incorrect to interpret it as a mere statement of fact, because that would imply that the speaker is merely informing, whereas the context demands a petition.

السلام
خبرية لفظاً إنشائية معنى

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the phrase 'wa-afḍalu al-ṣalāh...' is a factual statement reporting that Allah blesses the Prophet.
Correction: Al-Sawi explicitly states that the sentence is performative in meaning—it is a supplication, not a report. It would be impermissible to interpret it as a mere statement of fact.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The sentence 'And the most excellent of prayers' is grammatically a declaration but functionally a heartfelt supplication for the Prophet's honor.

Definition of Barā‘at al-Istihlāl and Its Role
Commentator defines barā‘at al-istihlāl and explains its use in the opening line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of barā‘at al-istihlāl and how it functions to indicate the author's purpose in the opening of a theological text.

Core Idea
Barā‘at al-istihlāl is the art of signaling your main topic from the very first words.

The commentator takes a moment to explain a rhetorical device that appears in the first line of the matn. This device, called barā‘at al-istihlāl, is not just decoration—it is a deliberate technique for orienting the reader. Understanding it helps you see how the author sets up his entire theological argument from the outset.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the commentator's structure: he first points out the device, then defines it, then gives etymology.
2
Locate the definition
Find the sentence where the commentator defines barā‘at al-istihlāl. It is the line beginning with 'وبراعة الاستهلال هي...'
The definition is: 'the author or someone else mentions at the beginning of his speech what indicates his purpose.'
3
Explore the etymology
Notice the commentator derives البراعة from 'excelling' and الاستهلال from 'manifesting'. This reinforces the idea that this device shows excellence in making the purpose clear from the start.
4
Connect to the book's theme
The commentator says the names in the opening line indicate tawḥīd because they contain the doctrines. Thus the device functions to hint at the main subject of the book.
This shows how barā‘at al-istihlāl is not just a stylistic ornament but a meaningful signal.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : الشَّريف العظيم . ولا يخفى ما في هذا البيت من براعة الاستهلال. وقوله : (وقيل : الشَّريف ... إلخ) : أي : فيكون من الأسماء الجامعة، وعلى كل هو نتيجة الاسم الذي قبله. قوله : (من براعة الاستهلال) :أي: لأنَّ هذه الأسماء تشعر بالتّوحيد الَّذي هو شارع فيه؛ لتضمنها العقائد، وبراعة الاستهلال هي أن يذكر المؤلّف أو غيره في أوّل كلامه ما يدلُّ على مقصوده، والبراعة من برع إذا تفوق على غيره، والاستهلال الظهور.
It was said: 'the noble, the great.' And it is not hidden that this verse has barā‘at al-istihlāl. And his saying: ('And it was said: the noble...etc.') means: it is among the comprehensive names, and in any case it is the result of the name before it. His saying: ('From barā‘at al-istihlāl') means: because these names indicate the tawḥīd which he is embarking upon, since they contain the doctrines. And barā‘at al-istihlāl is that the author or someone else mentions at the beginning of his speech what indicates his purpose. And al-barāʿa is from baraʿa, when he excelled others, and al-istihlāl is manifestation.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening line of the matn. The commentator explains the phrase 'وقيل: الشريف العظيم' and then notes that the line exhibits the rhetorical device of 'براعة الاستهلال'. He defines this device as the author indicating his purpose at the very start of his work. Here, the names mentioned (al-sharīf al-ʿaẓīm) hint at tawḥīd, which is the subject of the book, because they imply the doctrines (ʿaqāʾid). So the device functions to alert the reader to the main topic from the outset. The commentator also provides the etymology of the term: 'البراعة' from excelling, and 'الاستهلال' from appearing or manifesting.

The Demonstrative in 'And This is a Creed' Explained
Commentary on the grammar and metaphor of the demonstrative in 'وهذه عقيدة'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author of the creed uses the near demonstrative pronoun and explicit metaphor to indicate that the creedal statements are mentally present and easily accessible.

Core Idea
The near demonstrative 'this' signals that the creed is near in attainment and easy to grasp.

In this passage, the commentator Al-Sawi unpacks the first words of the creed after the praise: 'And this is a creed.' Every word is chosen with purpose. The demonstrative 'this' is not a casual pointer; it is a deliberate rhetorical device. The author explains that the demonstrative refers to the preceding statements that are present in the mind, treating them as if they were physically visible, and the nearness of the demonstrative emphasizes that these creedal statements are readily accessible to the student. This lesson will walk through the grammatical and metaphorical dimensions of this key phrase.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the commentary breaks down each phrase.
2
Identify the demonstrative and its referent
Find the phrase 'وهذه عقيدة' and observe that the demonstrative refers to 'the preceding statements mentally', not a physical book.
3
Understand why the near demonstrative is used
The author says the near demonstrative (هذه) was chosen to show that the creedal statements are near and easy to obtain. Reflect on how this affects the reader's perception.
4
Analyze the metaphor
The passage explains that there is an explicit metaphor (استعارة تصريحية أصلية) likening mental concepts to physical objects. Consider why this metaphor is appropriate.
5
Consider the debate about derivative metaphor
Al-Mawlawi and al-Isam proposed alternative views. The passage refutes them. Think about why the author insists on the explicit metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وهذه عقيدة) عطف على جملة (الحمد لله)، واسم الإشارة عائد على العبارات المتقدمة ذهناً، نزّلها منزلة الحاضر المحسوس بالبصر، فأطلق عليها لفظ الإشارة الموضوع لكلِّ حاضر محسوس، واختار اللفظ الموضوع للقريب للتنبيه على أنها قريبة التناول سهلة الحصول، ولذا أفرد الخبر مع أنَّها في نفسها عقائد كثيرة. قوله : (على العبارات المتقدمة ذهناً) : أي : وهو الكلام النَّفسي المخيل على هيئة الخارج. قوله : (المحسوس بالبصر ) : أي : مثلاً فالمحسوس بباقي الحواس مثله على التحقيق. قوله : فأطلق عليها لفظ الإشارة . . . إلخ ) : أي : ففي الكلام استعارة تصريحيَّة أصلية، حيث شبه ما في الذهن بالمحسوس خارجاً بجامع كمال الاستحضار في كلِّ، واستعير المشبه به للمشبَّه، هذا هو المشهور. وذهب المولوي في تعريب الرِّسالة الفارسيّة» إلى أنَّها تبعيَّة(۱) ؛ لأنَّ اسم الإشارة يتضمَّن معنى الحرف والاستعارة في معنى الحرف تبعيَّة، ورد بأنَّه لا يلزم من كون الشَّيء بمعنى الشَّيء أنَّه يعطى حكمه، وبهذا يردُّ قول العصام إنّها تبعيَّة ؛ لأنَّ اسم الإشارة مؤوّل بالمشتق ؛ لأنَّه في تأويل مشار إليه، تأمل . قوله : (ولذا أفرد الخبر ) تعليل لما قبله . وقوله : (مع أنَّها في نفسها عقائد كثيرة ) : أي : فأطلق البعض وأراد
('And this is a creed') is an apposition to the sentence ('Praise be to God'), and the demonstrative pronoun refers back to the preceding statements mentally, treating them as if they were present and perceptible by sight. So the word of indication, which is assigned to every present and perceptible thing, was applied to them, and the word assigned to the near was chosen to indicate that they are near in attainment and easy to obtain. For this reason, the predicate is singular despite the fact that in themselves they are many creeds. His saying: ('to the preceding statements mentally') means: it is the mental speech (al-kalam an-nafsi) imagined in the form of the external. His saying: ('perceptible by sight') means: for example, and the perceptible by the other senses is like it according to verification. His saying: ('So he applied to them the word of indication ... etc.') means: so in the speech there is an original explicit metaphor (istiʿara tasrihiyya aṣliyya), where what is in the mind is likened to what is perceptible externally, with the common factor of complete presence in each, and the thing likened to is used for the thing likened. This is the well-known view. Al-Mawlawi, in his Arabization of al-Risala al-Farisiyya, held that it is a derivative metaphor (tabiʿiyya), because the demonstrative pronoun contains the meaning of a particle, and the metaphor in the meaning of a particle is derivative. This was refuted by saying that just because a thing has the meaning of another thing, it does not necessarily take its ruling. By this, the statement of al-Isam that it is derivative is also refuted, because the demonstrative pronoun is interpreted as derived from the verb, since it is in the interpretation of 'pointed to'. Reflect. His saying: ('And for this reason the predicate is singular') is an explanation of what preceded. And his saying: ('despite the fact that in themselves they are many creeds') means: so he used the part meaning the whole, and intended ...
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase وَهَٰذِهِ عَقِيدَة (And this is a creed), which is an apposition to the opening praise. The author explains that the demonstrative pronoun هَٰذِهِ refers back to the preceding statements that are present in the mind, treating them as if they were physically visible objects. The choice of the near demonstrative (هَٰذِهِ) instead of the far (تِلْكَ) is deliberate: it indicates that these creedal statements are easily accessible and readily understood. This is why the predicate (خَبَر) is singular (عَقِيدَة) even though the creed consists of multiple beliefs—the singular form emphasizes the unified concept as a single, easily grasped entity. The author then breaks down the key phrases: 'on the preceding statements mentally' refers to the internal speech (الكَلَام النَّفْسِيّ) that is structured like external speech. 'Perceptible by sight' is just an example; perception by other senses is analogous. The application of the demonstrative is an original explicit metaphor (استِعَارَة تَصْرِيحِيَّة أَصْلِيَّة): mental concepts are likened to external objects due to their complete presence in the mind. The well-known view holds this. However, al-Mawlawi argued that the metaphor is derivative (تَبَعِيَّة) because the demonstrative contains a particle's meaning, and metaphor in particles is derivative. This is refuted: a thing having the meaning of another does not automatically take its ruling. Similarly, al-Isam's claim that the demonstrative is derived (مُؤَوَّل بِالمُشْتَقّ) is also refuted because the demonstrative essentially indicates 'pointed to'. Finally, the author reiterates that the singular predicate is a means of referring to the whole by its part.

Key Terms
اسم الإشارة
استعارة تصريحية
استعارة تبعية
الكلام النفسي
المحسوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The demonstrative 'this' refers to the physical book in hand.
Correction: In fact, it refers to the preceding statements that are mentally present, treated as if physically visible.

Checkpoint

A) Because the creed is far from easy.
B) To indicate that the creedal statements are near in attainment and easy to obtain.
C) Because the author is physically present.
D) To show that the creed is multiple.

Key Takeaway
The use of the near demonstrative 'this' in the creed is a deliberate rhetorical choice to emphasize that the creedal statements are mentally present, easily accessible, and unified as a single, graspable concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator says the near demonstrative 'this' was chosen to show that the creed is 'near in attainment and easy to obtain'. The passage describes mental concepts being treated as if they were physically present. How vividly present are the truths of your faith in your mind right now? Are they as real to you as the objects you see? If not, what is one belief you need to make more 'present' through reflection today?
?
The refutation in this lesson teaches that a thing having the meaning of another does not automatically take its ruling. Where in your life have you mistakenly judged someone's actions based on an assumption about their intention, without considering that intention alone does not determine the ruling? Metaphor in 'Ishara and Refutation of Derivative Views
Al-Sawi explains the isti'ara in 'ishara and refutes derivative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two competing views on the type of metaphor in the term 'ishara for mental concepts and the key arguments in favor of the original explicit metaphor.

Core Idea
The metaphor in 'ishara is original and explicit, not derivative—context determines meaning.

Al-Sawi delves into the rhetorical device behind the author's use of 'ishara to refer to mental representations. He explains why this is considered an original explicit metaphor (tasrihiyya asliyya) and refutes alternative arguments that it is derivative (taba'iyya). This sharpens our understanding of how kalam texts employ figurative language with precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to get the gist of the arguments.
Pay attention to the terms 'استعارة تصريحية أصلية' and 'استعارة تبعية'.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Sawi states the well-known view that the metaphor is an original explicit metaphor. Identify how he describes the analogy: likening internal concepts to external perceived objects.
3
Understand the alternative views
Al-Malawi and al-'Isam argue for a derivative metaphor because of the particle-like nature of the demonstrative pronoun. Note their reasoning.
The common misunderstanding is that being in the meaning of a particle necessitates a derivative metaphor.
4
See the refutations
Al-Sawi refutes al-Malawi by arguing that being in the meaning of something does not entail its ruling. He refutes al-'Isam by stating that the demonstrative pronoun is interpreted as a derivative (مشتق), not a particle.
Ends with 'تأمل' (reflect), encouraging deeper thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (على العبارات المتقدمة (ذهناً : أي : وهو الكلام النَّفسي المخيل على هيئة الخارج. قوله : (المحسوس بالبصر ) : أي : مثلاً فالمحسوس بباقي الحواس مثله على التحقيق. قوله : فأطلق عليها لفظ الإشارة . . . إلخ ) : أي : ففي الكلام استعارة تصريحيَّة أصلية، حيث شبه ما في الذهن بالمحسوس خارجاً بجامع كمال الاستحضار في كلِّ، واستعير المشبه به للمشبَّه، هذا هو المشهور. وذهب المولوي في تعريب الرِّسالة الفارسيّة» إلى أنَّها تبعيَّة(۱) ؛ لأنَّ اسم الإشارة يتضمَّن معنى الحرف والاستعارة في معنى الحرف تبعيَّة، ورد بأنَّه لا يلزم من كون الشَّيء بمعنى الشَّيء أنَّه يعطى حكمه، وبهذا يردُّ قول العصام إنّها تبعيَّة ؛ لأنَّ اسم الإشارة مؤوّل بالمشتق ؛ لأنَّه في تأويل مشار إليه، تأمل .
His saying: (upon the preceding expressions mentally): that is, it is the internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) imagined in the form of the external. His saying: (perceived by sight): that is, for example, and what is perceived by the other senses is like it according to verification. His saying: (so he applied to them the word 'ishara ... etc.): that is, in the speech there is an original explicit metaphor (isti'ara tasrihiyya asliyya), because he likened what is in the mind to the external perceived thing, with the common factor of complete mental presence in each, and the thing likened to (mushabbah bihi) is borrowed for the thing likened (mushabbah), this is the well-known view. And al-Malawi in his Arabization of the Persian treatise went to the view that it is derivative (taba'iyya), because the demonstrative pronoun (ism al-ishara) contains the meaning of a particle, and metaphor in the meaning of a particle is derivative. It is refuted by saying that it does not follow from something being in the meaning of something else that it is given its ruling, and by this the saying of al-'Isam that it is derivative is refuted, because the demonstrative pronoun is interpreted as a derivative (mushtaqq), because it is in the interpretation of 'pointed to' (mushar ilayhi). Reflect.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the author's use of the term 'الإشارة' for mental concepts. It begins by glossing the phrase 'على العبارات المتقدمة ذهناً' as referring to internal speech (الكلام النفسي) imagined in external form. Then it explains the phrase 'المحسوس بالبصر' as an example, with other senses being similar. The main point is the identification of an original explicit metaphor (استعارة تصريحية أصلية) in the expression: mental concepts are likened to physical objects, and the word for pointing (الإشارة) is borrowed. The commentator notes that this is the well-known view. He then presents an alternative view by al-Malawi, who considered it a derivative metaphor because the demonstrative pronoun includes the meaning of a particle. The commentator refutes this, arguing that being in the meaning of something does not entail its ruling. He further refutes al-'Isam's similar view, stating that the demonstrative pronoun is interpreted as a derived word (مشتق) because it is in the interpretation of 'pointed to'. The passage ends with 'تأمل' (reflect), inviting deeper thought.

Key Terms
استعارة تصريحية أصلية
استعارة تبعية
اسم الإشارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a word being in the meaning of a particle necessarily makes its metaphor derivative.
Correction: Being in the meaning of a particle does not entail the ruling of a particle; context overrides. Moreover, the demonstrative pronoun is interpreted as a derivative, not a particle.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi's commentary, what type of metaphor (isti'ara) is primarily being used in the author's expression 'أطلق عليها لفظ الإشارة'?
A) Taba'iyya (derivative)
B) Tasrihiyya asliyya (original explicit)
C) Makniyya
D) Tamthiliyya

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi affirms that the metaphor in 'ishara for mental concepts is an original explicit metaphor, refuting claims that the particle-like nature of the demonstrative pronoun makes it derivative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a complex religious concept, do you immediately judge it based on its surface form (like a word's grammatical category) or do you pause to reflect on its deeper function? What might you be missing because you haven't reflected enough?
?
The commentator ends with 'tamal' (reflect). What is one area of your faith where you have been too quick to accept a surface-level explanation instead of delving into its reality? Commit to spending time this week to study that area more deeply.
?
This passage shows that being in the meaning of something does not give it the ruling. In your own life, are there judgments you've made about others based on appearances rather than their true nature? How can you imitate the commentator's careful reasoning to avoid misjudgment?

The Comprehensive Content of al-Kharidat
Al-Sawi enumerates the contents of al-Kharidat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of al-Kharidat as described by al-Sawi: its coverage of divine and prophetic attributes, decisive proofs, the goal of emerging from imitation to verification, and its refutation of deviant groups.

Core Idea
Al-Kharidat covers everything needed for sound faith: divine attributes, prophetic attributes, decisive proofs, and refutations.

Al-Sawi's commentary on the opening of al-Kharidat explains what makes this book comprehensive and foundational. He clarifies that it addresses not only the attributes of Allah but also those of the prophets, provides rational and transmitted proofs, and aims to move the believer from blind imitation to verification. This lesson unpacks that description.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for its structure. Then read the full translation to grasp Al-Sawi's explanation.
The passage includes both the matn quotation and Al-Sawi's glosses.
2
Identify the four main components
Notice that Al-Sawi enumerates: divine attributes (obligatory, impossible, permissible), prophetic attributes, decisive proofs, and refutation of misguidance. List them.
This shows the book's comprehensive scope.
3
Understand the key terms
Pay attention to 'taqlid' (imitation) and 'tahqeeq' (verification). Al-Sawi highlights that the decisive proofs help one emerge from imitation to verification.
4
Reflect on the goal
Ask yourself: Why is it important to emerge from imitation? How do decisive proofs (both rational and transmitted) achieve this?
The goal is certainty in faith, leaving no room for doubt.
5
Consider the discussion on the imitator's faith
Al-Sawi mentions that later he will discuss whether the faith of an imitator is valid. Think about why this is a relevant issue.
This anticipates a common theological debate.

المدلولة لها؛ وذلك لأنَّها اشتملت على بيان ما يجب لله تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وعلى مثل ذلك في حقِّ رُسُله عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام، وعلى البراهين القطعية التي يخرج بها المكلف من ربقة التقليد إلى نور التحقيق، حتى لا يكون في إيمانه خلاف، وسيأتي بيان الخلاف في إيمان المقلّد إن شاء الله تعالى وعلى الرَّدِّ على أهل الضَّلال
قوله : (المدلولة لها : الضّمير عائد على العقيدة باعتبار كونها ألفاظاً.
قوله : (وذلك ) : شروع في توجيه كونها كبيرة في العلم .
قوله : (وعلى مثل ذلك) : المماثلة في مطلق وجوب واستحالة وجواز، لا في حقيقة كلِّ ؛ لوجوب التباين بين أوصاف الحادث والقديم .

That which is indicated by it; and that is because it includes an explanation of what is obligatory for Allah Most High, what is impossible, and what is permissible, and similarly regarding His messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, and the decisive proofs by which the responsible person emerges from the noose of imitation to the light of verification, so that there is no doubt in his faith, and the discussion of the difference of opinion concerning the faith of the imitator will come later, God willing, and the refutation of the people of misguidance. He said: (That which is indicated by it: the pronoun refers to the creed considering it as words.) He said: (And that): commencement in directing that it is great in the science. He said: (And similarly): the similarity is in the absolute obligation, impossibility, and permissibility, not in the reality of each; due to the necessity of distinction between the attributes of the contingent and the eternal. He said: (And the decisive proofs): i.e., transmitted or rational. He said: (By it): i.e., because of it. He said: (To the light of verification): the addition is either explanatory or of the comparison; and verification according to them is mentioning a thing in its true manner. He said: (So that there is no ... etc.): purpose for his saying (he emerges). He said: (Concerning the faith of the imitator): i.e., is it valid or not? He said: (Upon the people of misguidance): i.e., the beliefs that oppose the Ahl al-Sunnah, have they committed disbelief through them or not?
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Sawi on the initial statement of the matn describing the content of al-Kharidat. It begins by quoting the matn: 'That which is indicated by it; and that is because it includes an explanation of what is obligatory for Allah Most High, what is impossible, and what is permissible, and similarly regarding His messengers, and the decisive proofs by which the responsible person emerges from imitation to verification, so that there is no doubt in his faith, and the discussion of the faith of the imitator will come later, and the refutation of the people of misguidance.' Then al-Sawi explains each phrase. First, 'That which is indicated by it' means the pronoun refers to the creed (al-ʿaqīda) considered as words. Second, 'And that' begins the explanation of why this book is great in the science. Third, 'And similarly' indicates that the similarity between divine and prophetic attributes is in the absolute categories (obligation, impossibility, permissibility), not in the specific realities, because there must be a distinction between the attributes of the contingent and the Eternal. Fourth, 'And the decisive proofs' are both transmitted (naqliyya) and rational (ʿaqliyya). Fifth, 'By it' means because of these proofs. Sixth, 'To the light of verification' — the grammatical construction is either explanatory or a comparative metaphor; verification (taḥqīq) is defined as mentioning a thing in its true manner. Seventh, 'So that there is no doubt' is the purpose of emerging from imitation, and it raises the question of the imitator's faith (is it valid or not?). Eighth, 'Upon the people of misguidance' refers to beliefs opposing Ahl al-Sunnah, and the question is whether such beliefs constitute disbelief.

التقليد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that blind imitation (taqlid) is sufficient for faith without needing proofs.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that decisive proofs are provided to emerge from imitation to verification, implying that mere imitation without proof is insufficient for certainty in faith.

Modes of Presentation in al-Kharidat
Al-Sawi categorizes the book's content into explicit, implicit, samiyyat, and tasawwuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the book al-Kharidat presents its teachings through explicit and implicit statements, and how it covers revelation-based matters (samiyyat) and spiritual conduct (tasawwuf).

Core Idea
Al-Kharidat uses explicit and implicit modes, covers revelation-based truths, and includes spiritual conduct.

In this passage, al-Sawi explains the methodological structure of al-Kharidat. The book does not only present rational arguments; it also employs explicit and implicit statements, delves into matters that rely solely on revelation (samiyyat), and touches upon the science of spiritual conduct (tasawwuf). This lesson unpacks these modes and shows how they shape the theological discourse of the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the modes
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the three categories: explicit, implicit, and samiyyat, plus tasawwuf.
2
Understand explicit statements
Look at the example given for explicit statements: verses that clearly label certain beliefs as disbelief or innovation.
Explicit statements leave no ambiguity.
3
Understand implicit statements
See the example for implicit statements: the verse implying the world's originatedness without stating it directly.
Implicit statements require inference.
4
Grasp samiyyat and tasawwuf
Learn that samiyyat depend on revelation, not reason, and that tasawwuf is about spiritual conduct. Note the addition of rational judgment.
The book also covers rational judgment and its divisions.

ما تضمن الكتاب
تصريحاً تارة وتلويحاً أخرى وعلى السَّمعيَّات، وعلى شيء من التصوف الذي هو حياة النفوس، كما سترى ذلك كله إن شاء الله تعالى مفصلاً، ولذا قال مستأنفاً في جواب سؤال مقدَّر نشأ مما قبله تقديره: هل تكفي
قوله : (تصريحاً تارة) : أي : كما في قوله :
ومن يقل بالطبع أو بالعلة فذاك كفر عند أهل الملة ومن يقل بالقوة المودعة فذاك بدعي فلا تلتفت ومن يقل فعل الصَّلاح وجبا على الإله قد أساء الأدبا

ويلزم الإيمـان بـالـحـســاب
... إلخ .

What the book contains: explicitly sometimes and implicitly at other times, and on the samiyyat (matters known only through revelation), and on something of tasawwuf (Sufism) which is the life of souls, and you will see all of that, God willing, in detail; therefore he said, resuming in answer to an implied question arising from what preceded, its estimation: Is sufficient. He said: (Explicitly sometimes): i.e., as in his saying: 'And whoever says by nature or by cause, that is unbelief according to the people of the religion, and whoever says by the implanted power, that is innovation, so do not pay attention, and whoever says that doing good is obligatory upon God, he has been rude.' And he said: (And implicitly at other times): i.e., as in his saying: 'Then know that this world, meaning everything other than God the Exalted, the Knower, is without doubt, originated, in need...' etc. He said: (And on the samiyyat): i.e., those that depend on hearing and transmission, in which the intellect has no scope; like his saying: 'And faith in the Reckoning is necessary...' etc. He said: (And on something of tasawwuf): i.e., from the art of spiritual conduct. He said: (Which is the life of souls): i.e., spirits. He said: (As you will see that): i.e., you will learn it; rather, he added to what the commentator said: the rational judgment and its divisions.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage continues the hashya, now focusing on the modes of presentation in al-Kharidat. The commentator quotes a section of the matn that begins 'What the book contains: explicitly sometimes and implicitly at other times, and on the samiyyat, and on something of tasawwuf...' and then explains each part. First, 'explicitly sometimes' is illustrated by verses that explicitly state that belief in causality or obligation upon God is disbelief or innovation. Second, 'implicitly at other times' is illustrated by verses that imply the originatedness of the world. Third, 'samiyyat' refers to matters that depend solely on revelation (samʿ and naql) where reason has no scope, such as belief in the Reckoning. Fourth, 'tasawwuf' is defined as the art of spiritual conduct, and 'life of souls' means spirits. Finally, the commentator notes that the book adds the topic of 'the rational judgment and its divisions' beyond what the original commentator (al-shāriḥ) mentioned.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tasawwuf in this context refers to formal Sufi orders with specific practices and initiations.
Correction: Here tasawwuf is defined as 'the art of spiritual conduct' (fann al-taṣarruf) and is described as 'the life of souls,' not as organized Sufi orders.

The Book's Contents: Explicit, Implicit, Sam'iyyat, and Tasawwuf
Al-Sawi enumerates the book's contents: explicit, implicit, sam'iyyat, and tasawwuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main categories of content in the poem: explicit statements, implicit statements, and matters based on revelation, as well as the inclusion of tasawwuf.

Core Idea
Kalam covers explicit reasoning, implicit hints, revealed truths, and spiritual purification.

Al-Sawi begins by outlining what the poem contains. He explains that the book addresses three types of content: explicit doctrinal statements, implicit hints, and sam'iyyat (matters known only through revelation). It also includes a discussion of tasawwuf, which purifies the soul. This lesson will introduce these categories and provide examples from the poem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation.
2
Identify the three content types
Find where Al-Sawi lists explicit (tasrih), implicit (talwih), and sam'iyyat. Notice how he defines each.
Explicit statements are clear rulings; implicit are hinted; sam'iyyat rely on revelation.
3
Examine the examples
For each type, Al-Sawi gives an example from the poem. Understand what each example teaches.
Examples show how the poem conveys theological claims.
4
Understand the role of tasawwuf
The passage includes tasawwuf as 'life of souls'. Note that it's not the later Sufi orders but spiritual purification.
This connects theology to inner purification.

ما تضمن الكتاب تصريحاً تارة وتلويحاً أخرى وعلى السَّمعيَّات، وعلى شيء من التصوف الذي هو حياة النفوس، كما سترى ذلك كله إن شاء الله تعالى مفصلاً، ولذا قال مستأنفاً في جواب سؤال مقدَّر نشأ مما قبله تقديره: هل تكفي ... قوله : (تصريحاً تارة) : أي : كما في قوله : ومن يقل بالطبع أو بالعلة فذاك كفر عند أهل الملة ومن يقل بالقوة المودعة فذاك بدعي فلا تلتفت ومن يقل فعل الصَّلاح وجبا على الإله قد أساء الأدبا وقوله : (وتلويحاً أخرى) : أي : كما في قوله : ثمَّ اعلمن بأنَّ هذا العالما أي ما سوى الله العلي العالما من غير شكٍّ حادث مفتقر ... إلخ . قوله : (وعلى السَّمعيَّات) : أي : التي تتوقف على سمع ونقل، مما ليس للعقل فيها مجال ؛ كقوله : ويلزم الإيمـان بـالـحـســاب ... إلخ . قوله : ( وعلى شيء من التّصوف) أي من فنّ التَّصرُّف. قوله : (الَّذي هو حياة النفوس) : أي : الأرواح. قوله : (كما سترى ذلك) : أي : تعلمه، بل وزاد على ما قال الشارح الحكم العقلي وأقسامه.
What the book contains: explicitly at times and implicitly at other times, and on the sam'iyyat (things based on hearing), and on something of tasawwuf (spirituality) which is the life of souls, as you will see all of that, God willing, in detail, and therefore he said, resuming in answer to an implied question arising from what preceded, its implied meaning: 'Does it suffice...' His saying: 'explicitly at times' means as in his saying: 'And whoever says by nature or by cause, that is unbelief according to the people of the religion, and whoever says by the inherent power, that is an innovator so do not pay attention, and whoever says that doing what is beneficial is obligatory upon God, has behaved badly.' And his saying: 'and implicitly at other times' means as in his saying: 'Then know that this world, that is everything besides God the Most High, is without doubt originated and dependent... etc.' His saying: 'and on the sam'iyyat' means those which depend on hearing and transmission, having no scope for reason; as in his saying: 'And faith in the Reckoning is necessary... etc.' His saying: 'and on something of tasawwuf' means from the art of spiritual purification. His saying: 'which is the life of souls' means the spirits. His saying: 'as you will see that' means you will know it, and he even added to what the commentator said: the rational judgment and its divisions.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on the poem's introduction, detailing its contents. Al-Sawi states that the book covers three types of content: explicit statements (tasrih), implicit statements (talwih), and sam'iyyat (matters based on transmission), as well as some tasawwuf (spiritual purification). He provides examples from the poem for each. Explicit statements are illustrated by verses condemning those who attribute causality to nature, deem power inherent in created things, or obligate God to perform beneficial acts as such views are considered unbelief or innovation. Implicit statements are illustrated by the verse affirming the world's originatedness. Sam'iyyat are topics that fall outside rational deduction, such as the reality of the Reckoning. Tasawwuf is defined as the art of spiritual purification, which enlivens the soul. Al-Sawi concludes by noting that the author added the rational judgment and its divisions beyond what the commentator (the sharh) mentioned.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Here, tasawwuf means the art of spiritual purification (tazkiya), not the institutionalized Sufism.

Al-Sawi's Analysis of 'Takfika 'Ilman' and the Metaphor of Zubda
Al-Sawi analyzes the phrase 'takfika 'ilman' grammatically and rhetorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi uses grammatical and rhetorical analysis to clarify the poem's claim of sufficiency.

Core Idea
The poem's sufficiency is conditional and figurative, not absolute.

This lesson unpacks Al-Sawi's sharp grammatical and rhetorical lens. He questions whether the creed literally suffices, then shows how 'takfika 'ilman' functions as a tamyiz and how the metaphor of 'zubda' subtly qualifies the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms used by Al-Sawi.
The passage includes ellipses; focus on the complete analytical units.
2
Identify the grammatical analysis
Find where Al-Sawi explains 'takfika 'ilman' as a tamyiz muhawwal 'an al-fa'il. Understand what this means.
This is a specification that clarifies the subject.
3
Note the conditional nuance
Look at the conditional sentence 'in turid an taktafi'. See how it limits the sufficiency to those who wish to be content with basics.
The apodosis is omitted but implied.
4
Recognize the figurative language
Notice Al-Sawi's mention of majaz (figurative attribution) and majaz mursal (metonymy) in 'zubda'.
The cream of milk represents the core of the art.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه العقيدة المكلَّف في دينه كما يدلُّ عليه هذا الوصف الذي قدمته؟ أو هذا من باب المبالغة ؟ ( تكفيكَ عِلماً ) تمييز محول عن الفاعل؛ أي: يكفيك العلم المستفاد منها في دينك إن تُرِد أن تكتفي) أي : بها عن غيرها من المطولات، وذلك لأنها بزبدة) أي : بخلاصة ومحصل (الفنّ) المؤلفة فيه، وهو فن عقائد الإيمان، .... قوله : (أو هذا): مقابل قوله : ( وتكفي . . . إلخ)، فقد أتى لها بمعادل إجراء لها مجرى همزة الاستفهام، وإلا فهل لا يؤتى لها بمعادل؛ لأنَّها لطلب التّصديق . قوله : (تكفيك علماً : إسناد الكفاية لها مجاز . قوله : (إن ترد أن تكتفي : إن : حرف شرط، وترد: فعل الشرط، وإن وما دخلت عليه في تأويل مصدر مفعول لـ (تردَّ) وجواب الشرط محذوف دلّ عليه الجملة قبله والمعنى : إن ترد أن تقتصر ومفهومه : أنَّ من يريد الزيادة في العلم على أصل الواجب عليه فلا تكفيه بل لا بد له من المطوَّلات وهو كذلك . قوله : (وذلك) : قدَّر اسم الإشارة دخولاً على التعليل وإيضاحاً له. قوله : (أي: بخلاصة ... إلخ) أي ففي الكلام مجاز مرسل حيث أطلق الزبدة التي هي خلاصة اللبن، وأريد منها خلاصة الفنّ .
Is this creed sufficient for the obligated person in his religion as indicated by this description that I have presented? Or is this from the category of hyperbole? 'It suffices you as knowledge' (takfika 'ilman) is a specification (tamyiz) converted from the subject; i.e., the knowledge derived from it suffices you in your religion if you wish to be content with it, i.e., with it instead of other lengthy works. And that is because it is the essence (zubda), i.e., the summary and core (muhassal) of the art (al-fann) in which it was composed, and that is the art of the beliefs of faith. ... His saying: 'or this' is in contrast to his saying: 'and it suffices... etc.', for he has provided it with a counterpart, treating it like the interrogative hamza, otherwise the interrogative particle 'hal' is not given a counterpart because it seeks affirmation. His saying: 'it suffices you as knowledge': attributing sufficiency to it is figurative. His saying: 'if you wish to be content' (in turid an taktafi): 'in' is a conditional particle, 'turid' is the protasis, and 'in' with its clause is interpreted as a verbal noun that is the object of 'turid', and the apodosis is omitted and indicated by the preceding sentence. The meaning: if you wish to confine yourself [to it], and its implication: that he who desires increase in knowledge beyond the obligatory minimum, then it does not suffice him; rather he must use the lengthy works, and that is the case. His saying: 'and that' (wa dhalika) – he has estimated the demonstrative pronoun as entering for the purpose of causation and clarification. His saying: 'i.e., the cream... etc.' means that in the expression there is a metonymy (majaz mursal) where 'zubda' (cream/essence) which is the essence of milk is mentioned and the essence of the art is intended.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage provides a grammatical and rhetorical analysis of the poem's claim that it suffices. Al-Sawi begins with a question: Is the creed literally sufficient for the obligated person, or is this hyperbole? He then explains that 'takfika 'ilman' is a tamyiz muhawwal 'an al-fa'il (specification converted from the subject), meaning the knowledge derived from it suffices. He adds that it suffices only for those who wish to be content with it; those seeking more must turn to longer works. The reason is that the poem is the 'zubda' (cream/essence) of the art of Islamic beliefs. He then dissects the grammar: the phrase 'or this' serves as a counterpart to 'and it suffices', treating it like the interrogative hamza. He notes that attributing sufficiency to the poem is figurative (majaz). The conditional 'in turid an taktafi' is broken down: 'in' is conditional, 'turid' is the protasis, and the apodosis is omitted but implied by the preceding sentence. The implication is that the poem does not suffice those who seek more. Finally, he explains that 'zubda' is a metonymy (majaz mursal) where the cream of milk metaphorically represents the core of the art.

Key Terms
تمييز
مجاز
زبدة
شرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the phrase 'takfika 'ilman' is a literal and unconditional claim of absolute sufficiency.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows it is a specification (tamyiz) and includes a condition ('if you wish to be content'), and also involves figurative language (majaz), so it is not absolute.

Checkpoint
What grammatical category does Al-Sawi assign to 'takfika 'ilman'?
A) Hyperbole
B) Specification converted from the subject
C) Metonymy
D) Conditional sentence

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi's meticulous analysis shows that the poem's sufficiency is not absolute but conditional and figurative, emphasizing the need for proper understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage shows that the poem suffices only for those who wish to confine themselves to the basics. Are you willing to accept that there may be areas where you must be satisfied with the 'zubda' and not the lengthy works? How does your ego react to the idea of being limited?
?
If the sufficiency of this poem is figurative and conditional, what does that reveal about your own reliance on summaries and shortcuts in your faith? Are you using them as a foundation or as an excuse to avoid deeper engagement with the sources?
