The Ten Principles and the First Four
Al-Sawi introduces the ten principles and lists the first four from the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the science of kalam is built on ten foundational principles and you will learn its first four: name, definition, subject, and purpose.

Core Idea
Every science needs ten principles; kalam's first four define what it is and what it studies.

Before diving into any science, a student must first grasp its basic framework. Imam al-Sawi explains that Kalam has ten principles, but the commentary only mentions four. This lesson covers the names of the science and the first four principles that set the stage for the rest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the names
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how al-Sawi gives three alternative names for kalam: 'Ilm al-Tawhid, Usul al-Din, and 'Ilm al-'Aqa'id.
These names highlight different aspects of the science.
2
Understand the importance of the ten principles
Al-Sawi states that every beginner must know these ten principles; otherwise, his study is in vain. Reflect on why a meta-framework is necessary for any discipline.
3
Identify the first four principles
The commentary mentions only four: name (al-ism), definition (al-hadd), subject (al-mawdu'), and purpose (al-ghayah). Write them down and think about what each covers.
These are the most basic descriptors of any science.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the quiz question about the principles mentioned by the commentator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبادئ العشرة ويسمَّى علم التَّوحيد وعلم أصول الدين وعلم العقائد . قوله : (ويسمى : علم التَّوحيد :أي ويسمّى أيضاً علم الكلام، ووجه تسميته بهذه الأسماء ظاهر، وهو أحد المبادئ العشرة التي لا بدَّ لكلِّ شارع في فن من معرفتها، وإلا كان شروعه عبثاً . ذكر الشارح منها أربعة : وهي الاسم والحد، والموضوع، والغاية، : وبقي واضعه وحكمه ونسبته ومسائله واستمداده، وفائدته . فواضعه: الأشاعرة والماتريديَّة؛ أي: الَّذين دوّنوا كتبه وردُّوا على فرق الضَّلال، وإلَّا فالتَّوحيد جاء به كلّ نبي من آدم إلى سيدنا محمد . وحكمه: الوجوب العيني على كل مكلف بالدليل ولو إجمالياً ، والكفائي بخصوص التفصيلي. ونسبته : أنَّه أصل العلوم الدينية وما سواه فرع. ومسائله : الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات. و استمداده من الكتاب والسُّنَّة والعقل . وفائدته : معرفة العقائد الصّحيحة والفاسدة.
The ten principles, and it is called the science of Oneness (al-tawhid), the science of the foundations of religion (usul al-din), and the science of beliefs (al-'aqa'id). His statement: 'And it is called ... the science of Oneness', i.e., it is also called the science of discourse (ilm al-kalam), and the reason for naming it with these names is apparent, and it is one of the ten principles which every beginner in a discipline must know, otherwise his beginning would be in vain. The commentator (al-sharh) mentioned four of them: the name, the definition, the subject, and the purpose. There remain: its founder, its ruling, its relation, its problems, its sources, and its benefit. Its founder: the Ash'aris and the Maturidis, i.e., those who compiled its books and refuted the sects of error; otherwise, Oneness (al-tawhid) was brought by every prophet from Adam to our master Muhammad. Its ruling: individual obligation (wajib 'ayni) upon every morally responsible person with evidence, even if general, and collective obligation (wajib kifa'i) specifically with detailed evidence. Its relation: that it is the origin of the religious sciences, and whatever else is a branch. Its problems: (discussion of) the necessary, the impossible, and the possible. Its sources: the Book, the Sunnah, and reason. Its benefit: knowledge of correct and false beliefs.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the ten principles (al-mabadi' al-ashara) that every student of a science must know before delving into it. The commentator begins by stating that the science of kalam is also called 'ilm al-tawhid (science of divine oneness), 'ilm usul al-din (science of the foundations of religion), and 'ilm al-'aqa'id (science of beliefs). He then notes that the sharh (the commentary by al-Dardir or similar) only mentioned four of the ten principles: the name (al-ism), definition (al-hadd), subject (al-mawdu'), and purpose (al-ghayah). The remaining six are supplied here: the founder (wadi'), the ruling (hukm), the relation (nisbah), the problems (masa'il), the sources (istimdad), and the benefit (fa'idah). The founder is identified as the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools—those who systematized the science and refuted deviant sects; otherwise, the fundamental truth of tawhid was taught by all prophets. The ruling (hukm) is that knowledge of kalam is an individual obligation (wajib 'ayni) for every responsible person to know the basics, and a collective obligation (wajib kifa'i) for the detailed arguments. The relation (nisbah) is that kalam is the root science of religion; all other religious sciences are branches. The problems (masa'il) are the three categories of judgments: what is necessary (wajib) for God, what is impossible (mustahil), and what is possible (ja'iz). The sources (istimdad) are the Qur'an, Sunnah, and reason. The benefit (fa'idah) is distinguishing correct beliefs from false ones.

Key Terms
المبادئ العشرة
الاسم
الحد
الموضوع
الغاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the ten principles are the actual content of kalam (e.g., discussions of God's attributes).
Correction: The ten principles are the meta-framework—the introductory concepts—not the subject matter itself.

Checkpoint
How many principles does the commentator (al-sharh) mention according to the passage?
A) Ten
B) Four
C) Six
D) Two

Key Takeaway
The first four principles—name, definition, subject, and purpose—form the initial scaffolding for studying 'Ilm al-Kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are about to study 'Ilm al-Kalam. Have you ever paused to ask yourself what its name, definition, subject, and purpose truly are? Or are you rushing into it without this foundation?
?
Think of a time you studied something—any subject—without understanding its purpose. Did you feel lost or did it feel like 'عبث' (futility)? How will you ensure that your study of kalam is not the same?
?
If you honestly evaluate your current pursuit of Islamic knowledge, are you clear on the 'غاية' (purpose) of each science you learn? If not, what one step will you take today to remedy that?

The Remaining Six Principles of Kalam
Al-Sawi lists the remaining six principles not covered by the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six remaining principles of kalam: its founder, ruling, relation, problems, sources, and benefit.

Core Idea
Kalam's founder, obligation, primacy, problems, sources, and benefit complete the ten principles.

After the commentator lists four principles, al-Sawi fills in the other six. These cover who founded kalam as a discipline, its legal ruling, its relationship to other sciences, its main topics, its sources, and its ultimate benefit. Understanding these gives a complete picture of the science's identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the six principles
Read the Arabic passage for this lesson. It covers the remaining six principles not mentioned by the commentator.
2
Examine each principle one by one
Go through each of the six: founder, ruling, relation, problems, sources, benefit. Note how each is defined or described.
Use the guided breakdown for reference.
3
Reflect on the relation principle
Al-Sawi says kalam is the origin of religious sciences and all others are branches. Think about why that is important for Islamic studies.
4
Test your knowledge with the checkpoint
Answer the quiz question to ensure you grasp the founder of kalam as a discipline.

The Goal of Kalam and the Meaning of Hope
Al-Sawi states the goal of kalam and defines hope with its requirement of taking means.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold goal of kalam and the Islamic definition of hope as requiring both trust in Allah and the use of means.

Core Idea
Hope in Allah requires action; wishful thinking is blameworthy.

Al-Sawi explains that the science of kalam has a double purpose: to know Allah in this life and to attain eternal happiness in the next. He then comments on the author's prayer and clarifies what genuine hope means—distinguishing it from empty wishful thinking that neglects the means Allah has ordained.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the author's words settle before analyzing.
Notice the linguistic note about 'tufi' and the emphasis on 'wa Allāhu arjū'.
2
Identify the two goals of kalam
Al-Sawi states that kalam has both a worldly goal (knowledge of Allah) and an otherworldly goal (eternal happiness). Understand why both are necessary.
3
Understand the definition of hope
Hope (raja') is defined as the heart's attachment to a desired future outcome while taking the means. Note that this definition includes action.
The opposite, wishful thinking (tama'), lacks the taking of means and is blameworthy.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why taking means is essential. Al-Sawi mentions that denying causes is ignorance, as Allah's wisdom links effects to their causes.
This connects to everyday life: we hope for success but must work for it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغايته : معرفة الله سبحانه وتعالى، والفوز بالسعادة الأبدية.
(تفي) أي : توفي به لما تقدم.
(والله أرجو) قدَّم الاسم الأعظم لإفادة الاختصاص، إذ تقديم المعمول يفيد ذلك؛ أي: لا أرجو إلا الله تعالى.
والرجاء : تعلُّق القلب بحصول مرغوب فيه في المستقبل مع الأخذ في الأسباب - وهو ممدوح شرعاً – فإن لم يأخذ في الأسباب فطمع وهو مذموم شرعاً.
قوله: (وغايته : معرفة الله ... إلخ) أي فله غايتان؛ غاية دنيوية، وغاية أخروية .
قوله : (أي: توفي): أشار بذلك إلى أنَّ عين الكلمة محذوفة وهي الواو؛ لوقوعها بين عدوتيها كما هو معلوم.
قوله: (لما تقدَّم أي من تبيين الشارح ما احتوت عليه .
قوله : (الاسم الأعظم) : أي : الذي هو لفظ الجلالة على التحقيق.
قوله : (إذ تقديم المعمول . . . إلخ) : تعليل لما قبله .
قوله : (مرغوب فيه أي من خير الدُّنيا والآخرة.
قوله : (وهو مذموم أي شرعاً ؛ لأنَّ حكمة الله تعالى اقتضت ترتب الأشياء على أسبابها ، فمن أنكر الأسباب فهو جهول
And its goal: knowledge of Allah, glorified and exalted, and attaining eternal happiness. (Tufi) means: may you be granted success by it for what has preceded. (And Allah I hope) he presented the Greatest Name to indicate specification, because presenting the object of the verb indicates that; meaning: I hope only from Allah, exalted. And hope (al-raja') is the heart's attachment to obtaining something desired in the future while taking the means - and it is praiseworthy legally – but if one does not take the means, it is wishful thinking (tama') and it is blameworthy legally. His statement: 'And its goal is knowledge of Allah... etc.' meaning it has two goals: a worldly goal and an otherworldly goal. His statement: 'meaning: tufi' he indicated by that that the root letter of the word is deleted, which is the waw, because it falls between its two ends as is known. His statement: 'for what preceded' meaning from the commentator's clarification of what it contained. His statement: 'the Greatest Name' meaning which is the word of majesty (lafz al-jalala) according to verification. His statement: 'because presenting the object... etc.' is a reason for what preceded. His statement: 'desired' meaning from the good of this world and the Hereafter. His statement: 'and it is blameworthy' legally, because the wisdom of Allah, exalted, necessitated that things are arranged according to their causes, so whoever denies the causes is ignorant.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the goal (ghāya) of the science of kalām and explains a prayer of the author. It first states that the goal is twofold: a worldly goal—knowledge of Allah—and an otherworldly goal—attaining eternal happiness. Then it comments on the phrase 'wa Allāhu arjū' (and Allah I hope). The author prefixed the object 'Allah' to indicate exclusivity: he hopes only in Allah. This is followed by a definition of hope (rajā'): the heart's attachment to a future desired outcome while taking the necessary means. This kind of hope is praiseworthy in Islamic law. However, if one does not take the means, it becomes blameworthy wishful thinking (ṭamaʿ). The passage then explains that the verb 'tufī' is missing a wāw, and clarifies that 'the Greatest Name' refers to the word 'Allah'. It ends by justifying why wishful thinking is blameworthy: Allah's wisdom has established that things are linked to their causes, so denying causes is ignorance.

Key Terms

معرفة الله
السعادة الأبدية
الرجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hope in God means passive waiting without effort.
Correction: This passage shows that hope (rajā') requires taking means; without action, it is wishful thinking (ṭamaʿ), which is blameworthy.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes praiseworthy hope (raja') from blameworthy wishful thinking (tama')?
A) Raja' is for worldly matters, tama' for religious ones
B) Raja' involves taking the means, tama' does not
C) Raja' is directed only to God, tama' to creatures
D) Raja' is general, tama' is specific

Key Takeaway
The goal of kalam is both knowledge of Allah and eternal happiness, and true hope combines trust in Allah with active pursuit of means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I hope in Allah' when facing a difficulty. But have you actually taken the means that are within your power—or are you passively waiting, hoping without action? Which areas of your life right now reveal that your 'hope' might actually be blameworthy wishful thinking? What step will you take today to transform that into true hope?
?
In this lesson, hope is defined as heart's attachment while taking means. Is your heart truly attached to Allah, or to the outcome itself? When you pray for something, do you take practical steps, or do you rely solely on supplication while neglecting effort? Examine your current du'ā and actions—where do you need to align your means with your hope?
?
The goal of kalam is knowledge of Allah and eternal happiness. How much of your study of Islamic knowledge is aimed at actually knowing Allah and gaining the Hereafter, versus merely accumulating information? If you were to honestly evaluate your learning today, would it lead to the worldly goal (knowledge) and the otherworldly goal (happiness)? What one change in your study habits would align you more with that purpose?

Imam al-Dardir's Supplication for Acceptance and Benefit
Imam al-Dardir supplicates for acceptance and benefit, with commentary defining terms and extending the supplication to readers, students, owners, and writers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of Imam al-Dardir's supplication for his creedal work, including the concepts of divine acceptance and benefit, and the categories of people who partake in that benefit.

Core Idea
Acceptance from God means reward, and the supplication extends to all who engage with the text.

Imam al-Dardir opens his creedal work with a heartfelt supplication, asking Allah to accept his effort and to make it a source of benefit. The commentary unpacks the key terms and clarifies that the supplication covers not just readers but also those who study, acquire, or write the text. This lesson examines the supplication and its generous scope.

Guided Path
1
Read the supplication and translation
Read the Arabic supplication once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the structure: the supplication is followed by commentary phrases in parentheses.
2
Understand 'acceptance' (qabul)
The commentator explains that acceptance for humans means being pleased and not rejecting, but for God it means rewarding. Reflect on why this distinction is important.
This prevents anthropomorphism regarding God's attributes.
3
Grasp the meaning of 'benefit' (taf'a)
Benefit is defined as the opposite of harm, i.e., conveying good to others. Notice how 'minha' is interpreted as 'biha' (by it).
The supplication asks God to benefit others through this creed.
4
Identify the four categories of beneficiaries
The supplication lists: whoever reads (by memorization), studies (teaching/learning), acquires (by ownership), or writes (for self or payment). Consider how inclusive this is.
The commentator adds that this applies even more to the commentary itself.

A supplication of Imam al-Dardir [110] (for the acceptance of the work from which this creed (aqida) is composed; and acceptance (qabul) of a thing: being pleased with it and not rejecting it, (and) I hope from Him, the Exalted, (al-taf'a) which is the opposite of harm (darr), (from it) meaning from this creed; i.e., by it; i.e., I hope from Him the Exalted that He benefit by it everyone who reads it, or studies it, or acquires it, or writes it. His saying: (in the acceptance of the work): 'fi' is extra; evidence is the conjunction of 'al-naf' (the benefit) in the accusative after 'qabul'. His saying: (being pleased with it and not rejecting it): this meaning is with respect to created beings; but with respect to the Lord, the meaning of His being pleased with it is His rewarding for it. His saying: (it is the opposite of harm): i.e., it is conveying good to others, and harm is conveying evil to others. His saying: (i.e., by it) meaning that 'min' is in the sense of the preposition 'bi' of transitivity. His saying: (everyone who reads it): he clarifies by this the object of benefit. And his saying: (who reads it): i.e., by memorization. And his saying: (or studies it (tala'aha)) i.e., teaching or learning. And his saying: (or acquires it (hassalaha)) i.e., by possession. And his saying: (or writes it): i.e., for himself, or otherwise even for payment, and this supplication, although it is for those who engage with the matn (text), then with the commentary is more deserving of that, because it has been mentioned that he supplicated for those who receive the commentary with a sound heart).
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage contains the supplication (du'a) of Imam al-Dardir at the beginning of his work on the creed (aqida). The author asks God to accept his work and to benefit others through it. The commentator explains each part. First, 'acceptance' (qabul) of a thing usually means being pleased with it and not rejecting it, but when applied to God, it means rewarding for it. Then the author hopes for 'al-taf'a' which is the opposite of harm (darr), meaning benefitting others. The phrase 'minha' is interpreted as 'biha' (by it). The supplication extends to four categories: whoever reads it (by memorization), whoever studies it (whether teaching or learning), whoever acquires it (by ownership), and whoever writes it (for himself or even for payment). The commentator concludes that although the supplication is for those who engage with the matn, it applies even more so to those who engage with the commentary, since the author earlier supplicated for those who receive the commentary with a sound heart.

Correction: The commentator clarifies that for God, acceptance means rewarding the action, not an emotional response.

?

Examine your own intention right now: is there any trace of showing off? What step can you take today to purify your intention solely for Him?
?
The author included even those who write for payment. If you ever engage with sacred knowledge for worldly gain, how does that affect the spiritual benefit? Ask yourself: Is there any part of your religious learning that you treat as a means for status or money? What would it cost you to abandon that and seek only His pleasure?

The Meaning of Qabul in al-Dardir's Prayer
Al-Sawi explains the meaning of 'qabul' with a theological distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical and theological meaning of 'qabul' (acceptance) as explained by al-Sawi, and how it differs when applied to God versus creation.

Core Idea
God's acceptance means rewarding, not emotional satisfaction.

In this lesson, we dive into al-Sawi's commentary on the opening words of Imam al-Dardir's prayer: 'concerning the acceptance of the deed.' Al-Sawi clarifies that acceptance, for created beings, means being pleased and not rejecting, but for God it signifies rewarding the deed. This subtle theological distinction is crucial for understanding how we attribute actions to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the prayer and commentary excerpt
Read the Arabic passage once to get familiar with the language. Then read the English translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the structure: the prayer text is followed by al-Sawi's gloss.
2
Understand the definition of qabul
Al-Sawi first defines qabul as 'being pleased with it and not rejecting it.' Identify this definition and consider how it applies to everyday human interactions.
3
Grasp the theological shift for God
Al-Sawi immediately specifies that regarding God, 'being pleased' means 'rewarding for it.' Understand why this change is necessary when speaking of Allah.
This safeguards against anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the extra preposition
The commentator notes that the preposition 'fi' in 'fi qabul' is extra, as shown by the accusative case of 'al-naf'. This is a grammatical point that reinforces the meaning.

دعاء للإمام الدردير
(في قبولِ العَمَلِ الذي منه تأليف هذه العقيدة؛ وقبولُ الشيء: الرضا به وعدم ردّه، (و) أرجوه تعالى (التَّفْعَ) هو ضدُّ الضُّرِّ، (منها) أي من هذه العقيدة؛ أي بها ؛ أي أرجوه تعالى أن ينفع بها كلَّ من قرأها أو طالعها أو حصَّلها أو كتبها .
قوله: (في قبول العمل): في زائدة؛ يدليل عطف النفع بالنصب على(قبول).
قوله: (الرضا به وعدم ردّه) : هذا المعنى في حقِّ الحوادث، وأما في حقِّ المولى؛ فمعنى رضاه به : إثابته عليه .
قوله : (هو ضدُّ الضُّرِّ ) : أي : وهو إيصال الخير للغير، والضُّرُّ : إيصال الشَّرِّ للغير

(١) انظر (ص٥٤).
ويصح أن تكون من ابتدائية، هي ومجرورها حال من النفع؛
أي حال كون النفع حاصلاً و ناشئاً منها .
A prayer of Imam al-Dardir: (Concerning the acceptance of the deed from which the composition of this creed is derived; and the acceptance of a thing: being pleased with it and not rejecting it, (and) I hope from Him, exalted, (the benefit) which is the opposite of harm, (from it) i.e. from this creed; i.e. by it; i.e. I hope from Him, exalted, that He benefits by it everyone who reads it or peruses it or acquires it or writes it.) His saying: (in acceptance of the deed): the (في) is extra; evidence is the conjunction of benefit in the accusative on (acceptance). His saying: (being pleased with it and not rejecting it): this meaning applies to created beings, but regarding the Lord, the meaning of His being pleased with it is His rewarding for it. His saying: (it is the opposite of harm): i.e. it is conveying good to others, and harm is conveying evil to others. His saying: (i.e. by it, i.e. (من) is in the sense of the preposition of transitivity. His saying: (everyone who reads it): by this he clarified the object of benefit. And his saying: (who reads it): i.e. by memorization. And his saying: (or peruses it): i.e. teaching or learning. And his saying: (or acquires it): i.e. by ownership. And his saying: (or writes it): i.e. for himself, or otherwise even for a fee; and this supplication, although it is for whoever engages with the text, with the commentary it is more deserving of that, as previously mentioned that he prayed for whoever receives the commentary with a sound heart.) (1) See (p. 54). And it is possible that (من) is partitive, and (من) and its object are a circumstantial qualifier of benefit; i.e. the state of benefit being attained and arising from it.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (hashiya) on the opening part of a prayer by Imam al-Dardir, which appears in a creedal work. The commentator, al-Sawi, explains the meaning of key phrases word by word.

First, he glosses the phrase فِي قَبُولِ الْعَمَلِ (concerning the acceptance of the deed). He notes that the preposition فِي is extra (زائدة), as shown by the fact that the word النفع (benefit) is conjoined in the accusative case to قَبُول (acceptance). He then defines قَبُول (acceptance) as 'being pleased with something and not rejecting it' (الرضا به وعدم ردّه). However, he immediately adds a theological nuance: this definition applies to created beings; with respect to God (المولى), His 'being pleased' means His rewarding (إثابته) for the deed, not emotional satisfaction.

Next, he explains التَّفْع (benefit) as the opposite of ضُرّ (harm), i.e., conveying good to others.

Then he turns to the word مِنْهَا (from it), which in the prayer refers to this creed. He gives two possible interpretations: (1) مِنْ is the preposition of transitivity (باء التعدية), meaning 'by it'; (2) مِنْ is partitive (ابتدائية, meaning 'from it as a source'), and the phrase is a circumstantial qualifier (حال) of the word نفع (benefit), indicating that the benefit arises from the creed.

The commentator then lists those included in the prayer: everyone who reads (i.e., memorizes), peruses (teaching or learning), acquires (by ownership), or writes the creed, even for a fee. He adds that this supplication applies primarily to those engaged with the text, but even more so to those studying the commentary, as the author prayed for those who receive the commentary with a sound heart.

Finally, a footnote references page 54, likely the original prayer location.

Key Terms
قبول
رضا
رد
إثابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's acceptance implies emotional pleasure or change of state.
Correction: Al-Sawi explains that for God, acceptance means rewarding the deed, not an emotional response, preserving divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, what does 'qabul' (acceptance) mean when attributed to God?
A) Being pleased and not rejecting
B) Rewarding for the deed
C) Emotional satisfaction
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
When we ask for divine acceptance, we ask for reward, not emotional approval.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah to 'accept' your deeds, are you seeking His emotional approval or His reward? How will you consciously shift your intention from feeling good to earning His reward from now on?
?
Have you ever abandoned a good deed because you didn't 'feel' spiritual pleasure? What does this lesson teach you about the true nature of divine pleasure, and what one deed will you recommit to today purely for His reward?
?
Your du'a 'taqabbal' often asks for acceptance. After learning that divine acceptance is reward, how will you change the way you make this du'a? Write one specific phrase you will add to your supplication tonight.

Benefit and the Prayer's Reach
Al-Sawi explains 'taf'', 'minha', and the prayer's inclusiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'taf'' (benefit) and the two interpretations of 'minha' (from it), as well as the categories of people included in al-Dardir's prayer.

Core Idea
Benefit is the opposite of harm, and the prayer's benefit arises from the creed itself.

Continuing al-Sawi's commentary, we now explore the words 'al-taf'' (benefit) and 'minha' (from it). Al-Sawi explains that benefit is conveying good, and harm is conveying evil. He then offers two grammatical analyses of 'minha': either as 'by it' (باء التعدية) or as a partitive 'from it' indicating source. Finally, he lists the types of people included in the prayer: readers, students, acquirers, and writers, even for a fee.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on taf' and minha
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the definitions of taf' (benefit) and darr (harm).
2
Analyze the two meanings of minha
Al-Sawi gives two possibilities for 'minha': either 'by it' (باء التعدية) or 'from it' as a source (ابتدائية). Understand how each changes the meaning.
The commentary states both are valid.
3
Review the list of beneficiaries
He lists four categories: those who read (memorize), peruse (teach/learn), acquire (own), and write (even for a fee). Notice the detail.
4
Note the special mention of the commentary
The prayer applies especially to those who study the commentary with a sound heart.

Three Rational Judgments: Preamble to Kalam Books
Explains why the three rational judgments are a preamble to kalam, not a part of it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the three rational judgments are considered a preamble specific to kalam books, not a general preamble to all sciences.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are a book-specific preamble, not a universal scientific foundation.

When studying kalam, you will encounter the three rational judgments: necessary, impossible, and possible. But are these a part of the science of kalam itself, or merely a preparatory tool? The author clarifies that they are not a science in their own right, but a preface placed before the main subject matter due to their relevance. In this lesson, we explore the distinction between a general scientific preamble and a book-specific preamble.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction
The passage distinguishes between 'muqaddimat al-ilm' and 'muqaddimat al-kitab'. Read carefully to find the defining criteria.
2
Understand the ten principles
The author mentions 'al-mabadi al-asharah' as an example of a general preamble. Consider why these are universal.
3
Grasp the specificity
The three judgments are specific to kalam books. Why? Because they are tied to the subject matter of this art.
4
Reflect on the benefit
The preamble is placed before the intended object due to its connection and benefit. How does knowing these judgments help in kalam?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (تتوقف . . . إلخ ) : اعلم : أنَّ معرفة هذه الأقسام الثلاثة لا تسمى علمًا؛ لأنَّ مقدَّمة العلم تكون عامة في كلِّ علم؛ كالمبادئ العشرة، وإِنَّما تسمى مقدمة كتاب وهي : ما قدمت أمام المقصود بالذات لارتباط له بها وانتفاع بها فيه ؛ لأنَّ أقسام الحكم العقلي مخصوصة بالكتب المؤلفة في هذا الفن.
His saying: 'It depends ... etc.': Know that knowledge of these three divisions is not called a science ('ilm), because the preamble to a science is general to every science, like the ten principles (al-mabadi' al-'asharah). Rather, it is called a preamble of a book, and it is: what is placed before the primary intended object, due to its connection to it and benefit from it in that [science], because the divisions of rational judgment are specific to books authored in this art.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage explains the status of the three rational judgments (wujub, istihalah, jawaz) in relation to the science of kalam. The author comments on the phrase 'it depends ... etc.' from the sharh. He states that knowledge of these three divisions is not called an 'ilm (science) on its own, because the preamble (muqaddimah) of a science is general to every science, like the ten principles (al-mabadi' al-'asharah) which are common to all sciences. Instead, it is called a 'muqaddimah kitab' (preamble of a book), which is defined as something placed before the primary intended object due to its relevance and utility for that subject. The reason these divisions are treated as a preamble specific to books written in this art (kalam) is that they are particular to the subject matter of kalam, unlike more general preambles.

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments are not a science but a book-specific preamble that prepares students for kalam.

Understanding 'Aqsam Hukm al-Aql': Grammar and Rational Judgment in Kalam
Al-Sawi analyzes the phrase 'aqsam hukm al-aql' grammatically and justifies focusing on rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical structure of the phrase 'Aqsam hukm al-aql' and why kalam focuses on rational judgment.

Core Idea
The judgment of the intellect is the key to all theological reasoning.

Al-Sawi begins his commentary by dissecting the opening phrase 'Aqsam hukm al-aql' to clarify its meaning and scope. This grammatical analysis is not mere pedantry; it establishes that rational judgment is the exclusive foundation of kalam, distinct from legal and customary judgments. Here, we'll unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the grammatical terms like 'mubtada' and 'khabar'. Then read the English translation to understand the flow.
The passage is dense with technical language; take your time.
2
Understand the grammatical analysis
Al-Sawi explains that 'aqsam hukm al-aql' is a subject with an omitted predicate 'three'. Identify the implied predicate and how the following clause functions.
This shows how kalam authors use concise expression.
3
Distinguish the types of judgment
Notice how Al-Sawi distinguishes rational judgment from legal and customary judgments. Understand why he restricts the discussion to rational judgment: because all topics in kalam fall under it.
Legal and customary judgments are mentioned only to complete the picture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام حُكم العقل) مبتدأ خبره محذوف؛ أي: ثلاثة، يدلُّ عليه قوله الآتي : (ثالث الأقسام)، وجملة (هي الوجوب . . . إلخ) استئنافية لبيان الأقسام، ويصح أن تكون هي الخبر.

مقدمة
علم؛ ؛ لأنَّ مقدَّمة العلم تكون عامة في كلِّ علم؛ كالمبادئ العشرة، وإِنَّما تسمى مقدمة كتاب وهي : ما قدمت أمام المقصود بالذات لارتباط له بها وانتفاع بها فيه ؛ لأنَّ أقسام الحكم العقلي مخصوصة بالكتب المؤلفة في هذا الفن.

قوله : (حكم العقل) : نسبته للعقل من نسبة الشَّيء لآلته؛ أي: فالحكم آلته العقل، والحاكم هو النَّفس، فقول الشارح: (والحاكم به: أمَّا العقل . . . إلخ) . . فيه تسمح ، بل الحاكم النَّفس بواسطة ذلك، وتقييد الحكم بالعقل لإخراج الحكم الشَّرعيّ والعادي؛ فإنَّهما لا ينحصران في الأمور الثلاثة المذكورة.

وإنَّما اقتصر المصنّف كغيره من المتكلمين على الحكم العقلي؛ لأنَّ مباحث هذا الفن لا تخرج عنه، وإنَّما ذكر الشارح الشَّرعي؛ لأنَّ أصل التكليف به معرفة وغيرها، وأدلة بعض الصفات كذلك؛ كالسمع والبصر والكلام، وذكر العادي تتميماً للأقسام.

قوله : (يدلُّ عليه ) : أي : على خصوص تقديره ثلاثة .

قوله : (استئنافيَّة) : أي : استئنافاً بيانياً ؛ لوقوعها في جواب سؤال مقدَّر تقديره ما هي :
"'Aqsam hukm al-aql (divisions of the judgment of the intellect)' is a subject whose predicate is omitted, i.e., 'three,' as indicated by his later saying: 'the third of the divisions.' And the clause 'they are obligation... etc.' is an explanatory resumptive clause to clarify the divisions, and it is also permissible for it to be the predicate.

An introduction to the science; for the introduction of a science is general to every science, like the ten principles. It is only called an introduction to the book, and it is: what is placed before the intended subject to be connected to it and to benefit from it in it, because the divisions of rational judgment are specific to books authored in this art.

His saying: 'hukm al-aql (the judgment of the intellect)': its attribution to the intellect is the attribution of a thing to its instrument; i.e., the judgment's instrument is the intellect, and the judge is the soul. So the commentator's saying: 'and the judge by it is either the intellect... etc.' is a looseness, rather the judge is the soul by means of that. And the restriction of judgment to the intellect is to exclude legal (shar'i) and customary ('adi) judgment, for they are not confined to the three mentioned matters.

And the author, like other theologians, confined himself to rational judgment because the investigations of this art do not go beyond it. The commentator mentioned the legal judgment because the basis of taklif (religious obligation) is knowledge of it and other things, and likewise the proofs of some attributes such as hearing, sight, and speech; and he mentioned the customary judgment to complete the divisions.

His saying: 'indicates it': i.e., specifically indicates its estimation as three.

His saying: 'resumptive': i.e., an explanatory resumption, because it occurs in answer to an implied question whose estimation is: what are they?"
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and conceptual analysis of the opening phrase 'Aqsam hukm al-aql' (Divisions of the judgment of the intellect). Al-Sawi first explains that the phrase is a subject whose predicate is omitted, implying 'three,' as confirmed by the author's later mention of 'the third division.' The clause that follows (beginning with 'they are obligation...') is an explanatory resumptive clause that can also serve as the predicate.

He then discusses the nature of the introduction (muqaddima) to a science. Just as every science has general introductory principles, this section is placed before the main subject to provide necessary context. Notably, the divisions of rational judgment are specific to books in this field (kalam).

Al-Sawi addresses the attribution of 'judgment' to 'intellect' as a relation of instrument—the intellect is the instrument, while the true judge is the soul. He corrects an earlier commentator who said 'the judge is either the intellect...,' insisting instead that the soul judges via the intellect. Restricting judgment to the intellect excludes legal (shar'i) and customary ('adi) judgments, which are not limited to the three rational categories.

The author limits the discussion to rational judgment because all topics in kalam fall under it. The commentator introduced legal judgment because the basis of religious obligation involves knowledge of it and other matters, and also because proofs for attributes like hearing, sight, and speech involve legal considerations. Customary judgment is added to complete the set of judgment types.

Finally, Al-Sawi clarifies that 'indicates it' refers specifically to the estimation of the predicate as 'three,' and 'resumptive' means the clause is an explanatory resumption answering an implied question: 'What are they?'

أقسام
مقدمة

الشارح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the intellect itself is the judge of rational judgments.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that the intellect is merely the instrument; the true judge is the soul, which uses the intellect.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, why does the author confine himself to rational judgment?
A) Because legal and customary judgments are irrelevant to theology.
B) Because all topics in kalam (this art) fall under rational judgment.
C) Because the commentator only mentioned rational judgment.
D) Because rational judgment is the easiest to define.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'Aqsam hukm al-aql' is grammatically structured to signal three rational judgments, and kalam focuses on rational judgment because all its topics fall under it, while legal and customary judgments are only supplementary.

Definition of al-Hukm al-Shar'i (Revealed Judgment)
Al-Sawi defines al-hukm al-shar'i and explains its intended meaning and scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of revealed judgment as divine address and its scope including all divine utterances, not just commands.

Core Idea
Revealed judgment is Allah's eternal speech, not a temporal address.

Sanusi opens his discussion of judgments by defining the revealed type. The definition may seem simple, but the author carefully explains why 'address' must be understood in a specific way to preserve Allah's eternity. Then he broadens the definition to include many kinds of divine speech. This passage sets the foundation for understanding how Allah communicates with creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its Arabic
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key term 'khitab Allah' and the initial phrase 'wa al-hukm imma shar'i'.
The passage is from al-Sawi's commentary, so the first line is the matn (text) and the rest is sharh (explanation).
2
Understand the correction about 'address'
The commentary explains that 'khitab' here is used in the sense of the passive participle, not the infinitive. This is crucial to avoid implying that Allah's speech is temporal. Reflect on why this distinction matters for the doctrine of the eternity of Allah's speech.
The common misunderstanding is that address implies temporality.
3
See the examples that show the scope
Al-Sawi gives three examples of divine speech that fall under the definition: verses about the essences of people, verses about Allah's essence and attributes, and verses about His actions. These show that revealed judgment includes more than just commands and prohibitions.
Each example is accompanied by a Qur'anic verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحكم: إما شرعي، وهو: خطاب الله تعالى. قوله : ( والحكم إما شرعيٌّ :أي من حيث هو قوله: (خطاب الله): :أي: كلامه تعالى المخاطب به من إطلاق المصدر وإرادة اسم المفعول، وليس باقياً على مصدريَّته من أنَّه توجيه الكلام إلى مخاطب لعدم صحته هنا ؛ لأنَّه تعريف للأزليّ، هذا كالجنس فيدخل فيه كلامه تعالى المتعلّق بغير أفعال المكلَّفين؛ كالمتعلّق بذواتهم (١) والمتعلّق بذاته تعالى وصفاته (٢) وأفعاله (٣). (١) كقوله تعالى: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ) [آل عمران: ١١٠]. (٢) كقوله سبحانه : الله خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ) [الرعد: ١٦]. (٣) كقوله جل جلاله : وَنَزَّلْنَا مِنَ السَّمَاءِ مَاءً مُبَرَكَا) [ ق : ۹ ] .
And judgment: either revealed (shar'i), and it is: the address (khitab) of Allah Most High. He said: (And judgment is either revealed:) i.e., in terms of it being — His saying: (address of Allah): i.e., His speech (kalam) Most High, addressed with it, by using the infinitive (masdar) intending the passive participle (ism maf'ul), and it does not remain on its infinitive meaning of directing speech to an addressee because that is not correct here; because it is a definition of the Eternal. This is like a genus, so it includes His speech Most High related to other than the actions of the legally responsible, like that related to their essences (1) and that related to His Essence Most High and His attributes (2) and His actions (3). (1) Like His saying Most High: 'You were the best nation brought forth for mankind' [Al 'Imran:110]. (2) Like His saying, Exalted: 'Allah is the Creator of everything' [Ra'd:16]. (3) Like His saying, Majestic: 'And We sent down from the sky blessed water' [Qaf:9].
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the revealed judgment (al-hukm al-shar'i) and clarifies the intended meaning of 'address of Allah'. The author first states that judgment is divided into revealed and rational (though only revealed is discussed here). The revealed judgment is defined as 'the address of Allah Most High'. The commentary explains that 'address' here is not used in its usual sense of directing speech to an addressee, because that would imply temporality, whereas Allah's speech is eternal. Instead, 'khitab' is used with the meaning of the passive participle, i.e., 'that which is addressed', effectively meaning 'speech' (kalam). This definition serves as a genus, encompassing all divine speech—not only that which pertains to the actions of the legally responsible (such as commands and prohibitions), but also speech related to the essences of people (e.g., 'you were the best nation'), speech related to Allah's own essence and attributes (e.g., 'Allah is the Creator of everything'), and speech related to His actions (e.g., 'We sent down water'). Thus, the revealed judgment includes a broad range of divine utterances. This passage is important for understanding the foundational concept of 'hukm' in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Misconception: One might think that 'address of Allah' implies that Allah actually speaks to someone in a temporal sense.
Correction: The commentary clarifies that 'address' here is understood as eternal speech (the passive participle meaning 'that which is addressed'), because directing speech to an addressee would imply temporality, which cannot be attributed to the Eternal.

Distinguishing Division of a Whole from Division of a Universal
Explains the signs for dividing a whole vs. dividing a universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of logical division and how to tell them apart using the sign of whether the divided term can be predicated of the resulting parts.

Core Idea
Dividing a whole vs. dividing a universal: predication is the key test.

Logical division comes in two distinct forms: splitting a whole into its constituent parts and classifying a universal into its particular instances. This passage from Sanusi's commentary presents clear signs to distinguish them, using the simple test of whether the divided term can be truthfully predicated of each resulting item.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice that the passage defines two types of division with their respective signs.
The Arabic uses technical terms like 'انحلال' (resolution) and 'حمل المقسم' (predication of the divided term).
2
Identify the two signs
The first sign is for dividing a whole into parts: the whole must be analyzable into its parts, and the whole cannot be predicated of any part. The second sign is for dividing a universal into particulars: the universal can be predicated of each particular.
The examples clarify: 'thread' is not a 'mat' (whole-part), but 'Zayd is human' (universal-particular).
3
Apply the test to new examples
Think of other examples. For instance, 'book divided into chapters' is a whole-part division (you don't say 'chapter is a book'), while 'animal divided into human, horse' is universal-particular (you say 'human is an animal').
This solidifies the conceptual distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلامة تقسيم الكلِّ إلى أجزائه صِحَّةُ انحلاله إلى الأجزاء التي تركبت منها، وعدم صحة حمل المقسم على الأقسام. وعلامة تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته صحة حمل المقسم على كلِّ من الأقسام :نحو زيد إنسان وعمرو إنسان. قوله : (صحة انحلاله :أي تفصيله بأن تحلَّ الحصير إلى خيط وسمر، بحيث يكون كل منهما على حدته قوله : (وعدم صحة ... إلخ) : معطوف على (صحَّة) أي : لا يصحُ الإخبار بالمقسم عن أحد الأقسام، فلا تقول : الخيط حصير، ولا اليد أو الرجل إنسان مثلاً . قوله : (نحو زيد إنسان أي فزيد مثلاً جزئي من جزئيات الإنسان لا جزء
And the sign of dividing the whole into its parts is the validity of its resolution into the parts from which it was composed, and the invalidity of predicating the divided term on the divisions. And the sign of dividing the universal into its particulars is the validity of predicating the divided term on each of the divisions: like Zayd is a human and Amr is a human. His saying: (the validity of its resolution) i.e. its detailing, by dissolving the mat into thread and nail, such that each of them is separate. His saying: (and the invalidity of predicating... etc.) is coordinated with (validity) i.e. it is not valid to inform with the divided term about one of the divisions, so you do not say: the thread is a mat, nor the hand or foot is a human, for example. His saying: (like Zayd is a human) i.e. Zayd, for example, is a particular of the particulars of human, not a part.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage explains the logical distinction between two types of division: dividing a whole into its constituent parts (taksim al-kull ila ajza'ihi) and dividing a universal into its particular instances (taksim al-kulli ila juz'iyyatihi). The author gives two signs to distinguish them. For the division of a whole, the sign is that the whole can be resolved into its parts and the whole cannot be predicated of any of its parts (e.g., you cannot say 'the thread is a mat'). For the division of a universal, the sign is that the universal can be predicated of each of its particulars (e.g., 'Zayd is a human' and 'Amr is a human'). The commentator then explains each phrase: 'resolution' means detailing, like dissolving a mat into thread and nails, each separate. The invalidity of predication means you cannot say the part is the whole. In the example of the universal, Zayd is a particular (juz'i) of the universal 'human', not a part (juz') of a whole.

Key Terms
تقسيم الكل
تقسيم الكلي
انحلال
حمل المقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any division is the same, but the sign of predication distinguishes division of a whole from division of a universal.
Correction: In whole-part division, the whole cannot be predicated of a part; in universal-particular division, the universal is predicated of each particular.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of dividing a universal into its particulars?
A) Dividing a house into rooms
B) Dividing 'human' into Zayd and Amr
C) Dividing a book into chapters
D) Dividing a sentence into words

Key Takeaway
The sign of predication distinguishes division of a whole from division of a universal: if the divided term applies to each result, it's universal-particular; otherwise, it's whole-part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever treat a person as merely a 'part' of a group (like a cog in a machine) rather than as a 'particular' instance of humanity worthy of individual dignity? How can you consciously shift your view to the latter, as the universal 'human' is truly predicated of each individual?
?
When you read theological statements like 'Allah is just' or 'the Quran is Allah's speech', do you ever confuse the part-whole relationship (e.g., thinking a specific attribute is a 'part' of Allah) with the universal-particular relationship? How does this lesson's test of predication help you avoid such error in your understanding of Allah's names and attributes?
?
Reflect on a time you made a sweeping judgment about a group of people based on an experience with a few individuals. Did you commit the error of treating a particular (those few) as if they were a 'part' defining the whole, rather than as instances of a universal? What concrete step will you take this week to apply the correct logical distinction in your interactions?

The Division of Judgment: Intellectual and Customary
Al-Sawi defines customary judgment and enumerates its four logical forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how judgment is classified into intellectual and customary, and the definition of customary judgment including its four possible forms.

Core Idea
Customary judgment is based on repeated observation, not rational necessity.

Al-Sawi begins by distinguishing two types of judgment: one based on reason and one based on custom. The intellectual judgment needs no sensory repetition, but customary judgment depends on repeated sense-experience. This passage defines customary judgment and lays out all logical combinations of existence and non-existence between subject and predicate.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage carefully to see how judgment is divided into intellectual and customary. Notice the author's gloss about the mental trope.
The actual judge is the soul, not the intellect or custom itself.
2
Understand the definition of customary judgment
The passage defines customary judgment as the perception of the subsistence of a predicate for a subject (or its negation) based on repeated sense-perception.
The definition includes both affirmation and negation.
3
Identify the four forms
The passage enumerates four combinations of existence and non-existence between subject and predicate. Find each and note the examples given.
The forms are: existence-existence, non-existence-non-existence, existence-non-existence, non-existence-existence.
4
Reflect on the examples
Consider the examples like predator and eating, hunger and eating. How do they illustrate the four forms?
The examples show correlations observed in everyday life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحكم
إما العقل وإما العادة.
فإن كانت العادة فعادي، والحكم العادي : إثبات أمر لأمر أو نفيه عنه بواسطة التكرُّر بينهما على الحسّ ، كإثبات أنّ النّار محرقة، وأنَّ
قوله : (إما العقل) : فيه مجاز عقليّ؛ لأنَّ الحاكم النفس كما علمت. قوله : (وإمَّا العادة): هي ما اعتاده النَّاس، وفيه مجاز الحذف؛ أي: أهلها، أو مجاز عقليّ، وإلا فالعادة ليست حاكمة وإنَّما الحاكم أهلها . قوله : ( والحكم العادي : إثبات أمر لأمر المراد به هنا إدراك ثبوت المحمول للموضوع، أو نفيه عنه، فالأمر الأوّل : هو المحمول، والثَّاني : هو الموضوع، فالصُّور أربع ربط وجود بوجود؛ كربط وجود السبع بوجود الأكل، وربط عدم بعدم ؛ كربط عدم السبع بعدم الأكل، وربط وجود بعدم ؛ كربط وجود الجوع بعدم الأكل، وربط عدم بوجود؛ کربط عدم الجوع بالأكل.
قوله : (بواسطة التَّكرُّر ) : الإضافة للبيان والباء بمعنى (مع) ، والتَّكرر يتحقق بمرتين، فإذا قيل : اللحم الصَّاني يزكِّي الفهم، فإن تكرر ذلك مرَّتين ؛ فهو حكم عادي، وأمَّا إن حصل مرَّة ؛ فلا يقال له : حكم عادي. قوله : ( على الحسّ) متعلّق بـ ( تكرر )، والمراد بـ(الحسّ) ما يشمل الظاهري (١) والباطني (۲) ، فربط الإحراق بالنَّار؛ أي: اقترانها يتكرر على
(١) أي : كالسمع والبصر والشم والذوق. «تقريرات بصيلة».
(۲) كقيام الجوع بك أو العطش أو الشبع أو الري. «تقريرات بصيلة».
Divisions of judgment: either intellectual ('aql) or customary ('adah). If it is customary, then it is customary judgment. The customary judgment: affirmation of something for something or negation of it from it by means of repetition (takarrur) between them based on sense-perception (hiss), like the affirmation that fire burns, and that... His saying: (either intellectual): there is in it a mental trope, because the judge is the soul (nafs) as you know. His saying: (or customary): it is what people are accustomed to, and there is in it a trope of deletion, i.e., its people, or a mental trope, otherwise custom is not a judge but rather its people are the judge. His saying: (and the customary judgment: affirmation of something for something) what is intended here is the perception of the subsistence of the predicate for the subject, or its negation from it. So the first thing is the predicate, and the second is the subject. Thus the forms are four: linking existence to existence, like linking the existence of a predator to the existence of eating; linking non-existence to non-existence, like linking the non-existence of a predator to the non-existence of eating; linking existence to non-existence, like linking the existence of hunger to the non-existence of eating; linking non-existence to existence, like linking the non-existence of hunger to eating. His saying: (by means of repetition): the genitive construction is for clarification, and the ba' (preposition) is in the sense of 'with'; repetition is realized by two times. So if it is said: 'A certain type of meat (al-lahm al-sani) sharpens the intellect', if that repeats twice, it is a customary judgment; but if it occurs once, it is not called a customary judgment. His saying: (based on sense-perception) is attached to 'repetition', and what is meant by sense-perception (hiss) includes the external senses (1) and the internal senses (2). So linking burning to fire, i.e., its correlation repeats based on... (1) i.e., like hearing, sight, smell, taste. (from Taqrirat Basila) (2) like the occurrence of hunger for you, or thirst, or fullness, or satisfaction. (from Taqrirat Basila)
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage classifies judgment (hukm) into two types: intellectual ('aqli) and customary ('adi). The commentary explains that 'intellectual' involves a mental trope because the actual judge is the soul (nafs). For customary judgment, the trope is either deletion (referring to 'people of custom') or mental trope, since custom itself is not a judge. The definition of customary judgment is then given: perception of the subsistence of a predicate for a subject (or its negation) based on repeated observation through the senses. The author breaks down the possible relations between the subject and predicate into four combinations based on existence and non-existence, with examples. Next, the condition of repetition (takarrur) is explained: it requires at least two occurrences; a single occurrence does not produce a customary judgment. Finally, the sense-perception (hiss) includes both external senses (e.g., sight, hearing) and internal senses (e.g., hunger, thirst). The example of linking burning to fire is introduced but cut off.

Key Terms
hukm 'aqli
hukm 'adi
mahmool
mawdoo'
wujud
'adam

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Customary judgments are certain and universal.
Correction: They are probabilistic and depend on repeated observation; they may change if new observations arise.

Checkpoint
How many logical forms of customary judgment does Al-Sawi enumerate?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Customary judgment requires repeated sense-experience and has four possible logical structures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that in customary judgments, the judge is not custom itself but the people. In your own life, to what extent do you let social customs dictate your judgments without critically examining them? Can you identify one custom you follow that you have never questioned, and commit to examining it with your intellect this week?
?
Al-Sawi distinguishes between intellectual and customary judgments. Consider one of your deep-seated beliefs that you have always considered rational. Is it possible that it is actually based on repeated observation or cultural habit? What would it take for you to test it against purely rational criteria?
?
The four forms of customary judgment cover all habitual correlations. Think of a relationship you assume to be necessary (e.g., success requires hard work). How can you remind yourself that it is merely customary and breakable by divine decree? What practical step will you take to avoid mistaking a customary pattern for an absolute law?

The Condition of Repetition and the Role of Sense-Perception
Al-Sawi explains the requirement of repetition and the scope of sense-perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why repetition (takarrur) is necessary for customary judgments and how sense-perception (hiss) includes both external and internal senses.

Core Idea
A single event does not make a customary judgment; only repeated correlation over the senses does.

Al-Sawi now clarifies the two pillars of customary judgment: repetition and sense-perception. He specifies that repetition requires at least two occurrences, and that 'sense' includes both what we perceive externally and internally. This solidifies the empirical basis of customary reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the condition of repetition
Read the passage about repetition carefully. Note that it is defined as occurring at least twice.
The example of meat sharpening intellect is used to illustrate.
2
Understand the scope of sense-perception
The passage explains that 'hiss' includes both external senses (like sight) and internal senses (like hunger).
Internal senses are feelings such as hunger, thirst, etc.
3
Apply to the fire example
The passage mentions the correlation of fire and burning as an example that repeats based on sense-perception.
This is a classic case of customary judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

How might this lesson prepare your heart for the possibility of divine interruption?

Types of Evidence: Mujmal and Mufassal
Al-Sawi defines two types of evidence and their obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between summary and detailed evidence and the different obligations each carries in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Summary evidence suffices for every Muslim; detailed evidence is a collective duty.

Al-Sawi explains that while every Muslim is required to have basic knowledge of God's existence and attributes through summary evidence, the detailed evidence necessary to refute opponents is only a collective obligation on the community. This passage clarifies who is responsible for deep theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the definitions sink in.
Notice the contrasting phrasing: 'ما يكفي' vs 'لا يجب عينياً'.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
Find the terms 'الجملي' and 'التفصيلي' and note their definitions as given in the passage.
The definition of summary evidence includes 'المعجوز عن تفصيله' (incapable of detailing).
3
Contrast the obligations
Observe that summary evidence is sufficient (يكفي) unanimously, while detailed evidence is not individually obligatory but a collective duty (فرض كفاية).
The word 'اتفاقاً' (unanimously) emphasizes the consensus on summary evidence.
4
Understand the rationale
Note the reason given for the collective obligation: 'لصون الدين بدفع الخصوم' (for protection of religion by refuting opponents).
This shows that detailed knowledge is needed for defense against criticisms.
5
Study the commentary
Read the commentator's clarifications on phrases like 'كأن يعرف وجوده تعالى' and 'على ما ذكر' to deepen understanding.
The commentary expands the example to include all of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يكفي في المعرفة الدَّليلُ الجملي اتفاقاً، وهو (المعجوز عن تفصيله وحلّ الشُّبَه عنه) ، كأن يعرف وجوده تعالى بكونه خالقاً للعالم، وأمَّا التَّفصيلي وهو (المقدور فيه على ما ذكر) فلا يجب عينيّاً، بل وجوباً كفائياً لصون الدين بدفع الخصوم. قوله : (كأن يعرف وجوده تعالى) : أي : وباقي صفاته. قوله : (على ما ذكر ) وهو تفصيله وحلّ شبهه. قوله : (لصون الدين): علَّة لكونه واجباً كفائيّاً . قوله : (بدفع الخصوم) متعلّق بقوله : (صون الدين)، والمراد بالدفع : الرَّدُّ والإبطال.
And what suffices for knowledge is the summary evidence (al-dalīl al-jumalī) unanimously, and it is that which one is incapable of detailing and resolving doubts about, such as knowing His existence, exalted, by virtue of His being the Creator of the world. As for the detailed evidence (al-tafṣīlī), and it is that which one is capable of regarding what was mentioned, it is not individually obligatory, but rather a collective obligation (fard kifāya) for the protection of religion by refuting opponents. His saying: 'such as knowing His existence, exalted' means: and the rest of His attributes. His saying: 'regarding what was mentioned' means: its detailing and resolving its doubts. His saying: 'for the protection of religion' is the cause for it being a collective obligation. His saying: 'by refuting opponents' is attached to his saying: 'protection of religion', and what is meant by refutation is rebuttal and nullification.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of evidence for knowledge of God: the summary (mujmal) and the detailed (mufassal). The summary evidence is sufficient for everyone by consensus; it is the evidence that one cannot explain in detail or resolve doubts from, like knowing God exists because He created the world. The detailed evidence, on the other hand, is obligatory only as a collective duty (fard kifaya), not individually (fard 'ayn). The reason is to protect the religion by refuting opponents, meaning the scholars must be able to provide detailed proofs and answer doubts. Then the commentator explains each phrase: 'such as knowing His existence' includes all His attributes; 'regarding what was mentioned' refers to detailing and resolving doubts; and 'for the protection of religion' gives the rationale for the collective obligation.

Key Terms
دليل جملي
دليل تفصيلي
فرض كفاية
فرض عين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that detailed evidence is required of every individual Muslim (fard 'ayn).
Correction: The passage explicitly states that detailed evidence is not individually obligatory but a collective obligation (fard kifāya), meaning only some members of the community need to master it for the purpose of defending the religion.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on detailed evidence (al-dalīl al-tafṣīlī)?
A) It is individually obligatory (fard 'ayn)
B) It is a collective obligation (fard kifāya)
C) It is forbidden
D) It is recommended but not obligatory

Key Takeaway
Summary evidence (mujmal) is sufficient for every Muslim's faith, but detailed evidence (mufassal) is a collective duty on scholars to defend the religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own conviction in God's existence, do you rely only on the simple fact that He created the world, or have you ever wrestled with deeper doubts? If your faith were challenged, would you be able to give a detailed defense, or would you feel helpless? This lesson shows that detailed evidence is a collective duty—are you content to leave it to others, or do you feel a personal responsibility to learn more?
?
Imagine tomorrow someone presents a sophisticated argument against God's existence. Would your summary knowledge be enough to keep your faith intact, or would you feel shaken? If you would feel shaken, then perhaps you have not truly internalized that summary evidence is sufficient. Are you willing to trust the consensus that it is enough, or will you seek detailed knowledge for your own certainty?
?
The protection of religion from opponents is a serious task. If all scholars abandoned their duty of detailed rebuttals, would you step up to learn and defend the faith? Or do you assume someone else will handle it? This lesson calls you to reflect: are you part of the problem or part of the solution in safeguarding the truth?

Why Blind Imitation of Scripture is Insufficient
Al-Sawi critiques the claim that taqlid of the decisive texts suffices, arguing it can lead to kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere taqlid (blind imitation) of even the decisive texts of the Quran and Sunnah is insufficient and can lead to disbelief if the apparent meaning is taken without proper interpretation.

Core Idea
Blind imitation of scripture can lead to anthropomorphism and disbelief.

Al-Sawi begins by refuting a dangerous claim: that it is enough to simply accept the apparent meanings of the Quran and Sunnah without rational inquiry. He warns that such an approach can actually lead to kufr, citing examples of verses that, if taken literally, imply anthropomorphism. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Al-Sawi responds to the claim that taqlid suffices.
2
Identify the problem with blind imitation
Al-Sawi says that mere taqlid of the apparent (zahir) of the Book and Sunnah is from the foundations of disbelief. Why?
He gives examples of anthropomorphic verses that, if imitated literally, lead to error.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the verses about Allah's hand, His being in heaven and earth, and His descent. Why would blind acceptance of these be problematic?
This shows the need for proper theological interpretation (ta'wil) guided by reason and revelation.
4
Apply the lesson
Understand that faith requires not just acceptance but also correct understanding. Taqlid alone is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : يكفي إن قلد القرآنَ والسُّنَّة القطعية، وفيه نظر . وذهب بعضهم إلى تحريم النظر؛ لأنَّه مَظنَّةُ الوقوع في الشُّبَه والضَّلال، وليس بشيء . الأصول، وهذا القول لأبي هاشم الجبائي من المعتزلة، وذكره السنوسي في كبراه، وهو ضعيف. قوله : ( وفيه نظر) : أي : لأنَّ مجرَّد تقليد ظاهر الكتاب والسُّنَّة من أصول الكفر؛ كتقليد : يَدُ اللهِ فَوْقَ أَيْدِيهِمْ، (وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ إِلَهُ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَهُ ) ، عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ، وكتقليد : ينزل ربُّنَا كل ليلةٍ إلى سماء الدُّنيا . قوله : (وليس بشيء) : أي : لأنَّ بالنَّظر ينتقل الشَّخص من التقليد إلى المعرفة، فهو يزيل الشَّبه فكيف يوقع فيها : ولورود الأمر به.
And it is said: It suffices if he imitates (qallada) the decisive Qur'an and Sunna, and there is consideration in it. And some went to prohibiting speculative reasoning (nazar), because it is a place of falling into doubts and misguidance, and it is nothing. The fundamentals, and this saying is attributed to Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila, and al-Sanusi mentioned it in his 'Kubra', and it is weak. His saying: 'and there is consideration in it' meaning: because mere imitation (taqlid) of the apparent (zahir) of the Book and Sunna is among the foundations of disbelief; like imitating: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands', 'And He is the one who is in the heaven a god and in the earth a god', 'He established Himself upon the Throne', and like imitating: 'Our Lord descends every night to the heaven of the world'. His saying: 'and it is nothing' meaning: because through speculative reasoning (nazar) a person transfers from imitation (taqlid) to knowledge (ma'rifa), so it removes doubts, so how could it cause falling into them? And because the command to do it has come.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two erroneous views. The first part critiques the claim that mere taqlid (imitation) of the Qur'an and Sunna is sufficient for faith, noting that blind acceptance of apparent texts like anthropomorphic verses can lead to kufr. The second part refutes the prohibition of nazar (rational inquiry), arguing that nazar actually removes doubts and is commanded. Al-Sawi endorses the Sunni view that rational inquiry is necessary.

Key Terms
تقليد
ظاهر
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that taqlid of the apparent meaning of scripture is sufficient for faith.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that such taqlid can lead to kufr, as the apparent meaning may imply anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Sawi consider the view that taqlid of the Qur'an and Sunna suffices as problematic?
A) Because it leads to abandoning prayer
B) Because mere taqlid of apparent texts can lead to disbelief by accepting anthropomorphic interpretations
C) Because it contradicts the consensus of the scholars
D) Because it is a Mu'tazilite innovation

Key Takeaway
Blind imitation of scripture without proper interpretation can lead to error; true faith requires correct understanding guided by reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a religious belief simply because you heard it from a scholar or read it in a book, without verifying it against your own reasoning and the deeper principles of Islam? Which specific belief might that be, and what would it take today to re-examine it with humility and intellectual honesty?
?
This lesson warns that even reciting Quranic verses literally can lead to grave error. Which verse or hadith have you taken at face value without considering its proper interpretation? Will you commit to researching its authoritative explanation this week?
?
If your current understanding of Allah's attributes is based purely on the apparent meaning of scripture, are you prepared to accept that you might be falling into anthropomorphism? What step will you take to ensure your faith is built on sound knowledge, not blind imitation?

The Virtue of Rational Inquiry (Nazar)
Al-Sawi refutes the view that nazar is prohibited, arguing it transfers from taqlid to knowledge and is commanded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why nazar (rational inquiry) is not only permissible but necessary for attaining certainty in faith, and why the claim that it leads to doubt is unfounded.

Core Idea
Nazar removes doubts and leads to knowledge, not error.

Some theologians claimed that engaging in rational speculation (nazar) is prohibited because it leads to falling into doubts and misguidance. Al-Sawi refutes this, showing instead that nazar is the means by which a person moves from blind imitation to true knowledge (ma'rifa). It is a commanded act that dispels confusion. This lesson examines his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the claim that nazar is prohibited and Al-Sawi's response.
2
Understand the claim
Some said nazar is a place of falling into doubts and misguidance. This implies it should be avoided.
3
Analyze Al-Sawi's refutation
He gives two reasons: 1) Nazar transfers a person from taqlid to knowledge, so it removes doubts. 2) There is a command to engage in nazar. Therefore, how could it cause falling into error?
4
Connect to the broader theme
This lesson shows that Islam values reason and evidence, not blind acceptance. Rational inquiry is essential for sound faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : يكفي إن قلد القرآنَ والسُّنَّة القطعية، وفيه نظر . وذهب بعضهم إلى تحريم النظر؛ لأنَّه مَظنَّةُ الوقوع في الشُّبَه والضَّلال، وليس بشيء . الأصول، وهذا القول لأبي هاشم الجبائي من المعتزلة، وذكره السنوسي في كبراه، وهو ضعيف. قوله : ( وفيه نظر) : أي : لأنَّ مجرَّد تقليد ظاهر الكتاب والسُّنَّة من أصول الكفر؛ كتقليد : يَدُ اللهِ فَوْقَ أَيْدِيهِمْ، (وَهُوَ الَّذِي فِي السَّمَاءِ إِلَهُ وَفِي الْأَرْضِ إِلَهُ ) ، عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ، وكتقليد : ينزل ربُّنَا كل ليلةٍ إلى سماء الدُّنيا . قوله : (وليس بشيء) : أي : لأنَّ بالنَّظر ينتقل الشَّخص من التقليد إلى المعرفة، فهو يزيل الشَّبه فكيف يوقع فيها : ولورود الأمر به.
And it is said: It suffices if he imitates (qallada) the decisive Qur'an and Sunna, and there is consideration in it. And some went to prohibiting speculative reasoning (nazar), because it is a place of falling into doubts and misguidance, and it is nothing. The fundamentals, and this saying is attributed to Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i from the Mu'tazila, and al-Sanusi mentioned it in his 'Kubra', and it is weak. His saying: 'and there is consideration in it' meaning: because mere imitation (taqlid) of the apparent (zahir) of the Book and Sunna is among the foundations of disbelief; like imitating: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands', 'And He is the one who is in the heaven a god and in the earth a god', 'He established Himself upon the Throne', and like imitating: 'Our Lord descends every night to the heaven of the world'. His saying: 'and it is nothing' meaning: because through speculative reasoning (nazar) a person transfers from imitation (taqlid) to knowledge (ma'rifa), so it removes doubts, so how could it cause falling into them? And because the command to do it has come.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two erroneous views. The first part critiques the claim that mere taqlid (imitation) of the Qur'an and Sunna is sufficient for faith, noting that blind acceptance of apparent texts like anthropomorphic verses can lead to kufr. The second part refutes the prohibition of nazar (rational inquiry), arguing that nazar actually removes doubts and is commanded. Al-Sawi endorses the Sunni view that rational inquiry is necessary.

Key Terms
نظر
تقليد
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think nazar leads to doubt and misguidance and should be avoided.
Correction: Al-Sawi argues nazar actually removes doubts and is commanded, so it is beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, why is the view that nazar is prohibited 'nothing'?
A) Because it is a Mu'tazilite innovation
B) Because nazar transfers a person from taqlid to knowledge and is commanded, thus removing doubt
C) Because it contradicts the consensus of the Companions
D) Because nazar is only required for scholars

Key Takeaway
Rational inquiry (nazar) is a commanded act that leads to knowledge and certainty, not doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided thinking deeply about your faith because it felt dangerous? This lesson says nazar is commanded and removes doubt. What doubt are you avoiding confronting today? Commit to examining one belief this week using rational inquiry.
?
The passage says nazar moves you from taqlid to knowledge. Are you blindly following what your family or community believes without personal verification? Identify one belief you hold on taqlid and resolve to study it with nazar this month.
?
If nazar is commanded, how would you answer to Allah if He asks why you relied on blind imitation rather than seeking knowledge? Write down one step you will take tonight to start.
