The First Obligation: Ma'rifa and Differing Opinions
Al-Sawi affirms ma'rifa as the first obligation and lists conflicting views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of God (ma'rifa) is considered the first obligation and how it differs from other proposed first duties.

Core Idea
Knowledge (ma'rifa) is the first duty; all other obligations depend on it.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary establishes the foundational duty in Islamic theology: knowledge of God. Before any other act of worship, a responsible person must first know their Creator. Al-Sawi affirms this as the correct view and then lists several conflicting opinions from other scholars, including the Mu'tazilite position that doubt is the first step. As you read, notice how the author prioritizes the end (knowledge) over the means (reasoning).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the translation. Notice how the author begins with an emphatic statement about ma'rifa.
The passage is somewhat truncated; focus on the views that are present.
2
Identify the author's view and the opposing views
Al-Sawi states the correct view: ma'rifa is first. Then list the six conflicting opinions mentioned. Which one seems odd to you?
The opinion of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i (doubt) is particularly notable.
3
Reflect on why ma'rifa is the first obligation
The author says 'since all obligations depend on it.' Why would that be? Consider that no act of worship can be valid without intentional knowledge of whom you are worshiping.
This connects to the broader kalam project of establishing knowledge of God through rational proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ المعرفة هي أوّل واجب على المكلف، إذ جميع الواجبات متوقفة عليها . وقوله : (فاعرف) أي: اعرف أنَّها. قوله : (هي أوّل واجب على المكلَّف) أي ذكراً أو أنثى حرّاً أو عبداً، إنسيّاً أو جنيّاً، وهذا هو الحقُّ، ولذا اقتصر عليه، ومقابله أقوال؛ قيل : النَّظر ، وقيل : أوّل جزء منه ، وقيل : القصد إليه، وقيل: الشَّكُ، وهو لأبي هاشم الجبائي رئيس المعتزلة وقيل : النُّطق بالشهادتين، وقيل:
And know that knowledge (ma'rifa) is the first obligation upon the responsible person, since all obligations depend on it. And His saying: 'So know' i.e., know that it. His saying: 'It is the first obligation upon the responsible person' i.e., male or female, free or slave, human or jinn, and this is the truth, hence he confined himself to it. Contrary to it are opinions: It is said: speculative reasoning (nazar), and it is said: the first part of it, and it is said: the intention towards it, and it is said: doubt (shakk) – and this is the opinion of Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, head of the Mu'tazila – and it is said: uttering the two testimonies (nutaq bi al-shahadatayn), and it is said:
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage asserts that ma'rifa (knowledge of God) is the first religious duty. The commentary expands that this applies to all humans and jinn regardless of gender or status. It then lists opposing views: some say the first duty is nazar (speculative reasoning), others say it is the first part of nazar, the intention to do nazar, doubt (by Abu Hashim), or uttering the shahada. The author sides with ma'rifa as the truth.

Misconception: Some think the first duty is speculative reasoning (nazar) itself.
Correction: The author argues that the first duty is knowledge (ma'rifa), while nazar is the means to attain it.

Checkpoint

A) Speculative reasoning (nazar)
B) Knowledge (ma'rifa) of God
C) Uttering the two testimonies
D) Doubt (shakk)

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God (ma'rifa) is the foundational obligation in Islam, upon which all other duties depend, as affirmed by Al-Sawi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Knowledge is the First Obligation
The author states that knowledge is the first obligation and its rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge is considered the very first religious duty in Islam and how it underpins all other obligations.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the foundation of all religious duties.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle of Islamic theology: that knowledge (ma'rifa) is the very first obligation upon every accountable person. The author asserts this directly and explains the rationale: without knowledge, no other duty can be properly fulfilled. We'll examine the text and its implications.

Guided Path

The First Obligation: Knowledge Over Other Views
The author lists various opinions on the first duty and settles on knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various opinions on the first duty in Islam and why the author affirms knowledge as the correct view.

Core Idea
The first duty is knowledge of Allah, not doubt, imitation, or speculation.

The author of the matn declares that the first duty upon every accountable person is knowledge of Allah. But this was not the only opinion. In this passage, the commentator lists numerous differing views from various scholars, including the Mu'tazilites, before reaffirming the truth of knowledge. This lesson surveys those opinions and highlights the author's definitive stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full list of opinions.
Notice the phrase 'this is the truth' which indicates the author's position.
2
Identify the main opinions
Go through each of the listed opinions one by one. What is nazar? What is shakk? Who held which view?
Pay special attention to the opinion attributed to Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i.
3
Understand the significance of the list
Why does the commentator list so many views? This shows the range of scholarly discussion and then the rejection of all but one.
The list also helps the student know what others have said.
4
Connect to the learning objective
Reflect: knowing the diversity of opinions, you can appreciate why the author firmly asserts knowledge as the first duty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هي أوّل واجب على المكلَّف) أي ذكراً أو أنثى حرّاً أو عبداً، إنسيّاً أو جنيّاً، وهذا هو الحقُّ، ولذا اقتصر عليه، ومقابله أقوال؛ قيل : النَّظر ، وقيل : أوّل جزء منه ، وقيل : القصد إليه، وقيل: الشَّكُ، وهو لأبي هاشم الجبائي رئيس المعتزلة وقيل : النُّطق بالشهادتين، وقيل: التقليد، وقيل: أحد أمرين ؛ التَّقليد أو المعرفة، وقيل : التَّفرغ للنظر، بمعنى : ترك الشَّواغل ، وقيل : اعتقاد وجوب النظر ، وقيل : الإيمان .
His saying: (It is the first duty upon the legally responsible person) meaning: male or female, free or slave, human or jinn, and this is the truth, hence he confined himself to it. And opposing it are sayings: it was said: consideration (nazar); and it was said: the first part of it; and it was said: the intention towards it; and it was said: doubt (shakk), and this is from Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, head of the Mu'tazila; and it was said: uttering the two testimonies; and it was said: imitation (taqlid); and it was said: one of two things: imitation or knowledge; and it was said: making time free for consideration, meaning leaving distractions; and it was said: belief in the obligation of consideration; and it was said: faith.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'It is the first duty' from the source text. The author explains that the statement applies universally to all mukallaf: male, female, free, slave, human, jinn. He affirms that this is the correct position. Then he lists a series of alternative opinions from various scholars: some say the first duty is nazar (speculative reasoning), others say it is the first part of that reasoning, some say it is qasd (intention) to reason, some say shakk (doubt) — attributed to the Mu'tazilite Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i — others say uttering the shahada, taqlid (imitation), one of two things (taqlid or knowledge), making time free for nazar, belief in the obligation of nazar, and faith. This enumeration shows the diversity of views before settling on knowledge.

Key Terms
النظر
الشك
التقليد
الإيمان
القصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i
C) Ibn Arabi
D) Al-Sawi

Key Takeaway
Despite many opinions, the first duty is undeniably knowledge of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Obligation is by Sharia, Not by Reason
The author asserts that obligation is known by revelation, not reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to the Ash'ari school, moral and religious obligations are known only through revelation, not through unaided reason.

Core Idea
Revealed law, not reason, is the source of obligation.

This passage addresses a fundamental methodological difference between the Ash'aris and the Mu'tazila. The author asserts that the obligation to know Allah is derived solely from the divine law, not from rational inference. This short but significant statement sets the stage for the Ash'ari emphasis on revelation as the primary source of theological knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the concise style.
The key claim is in the first sentence.
2
Identify the main assertion
What is the author asserting about the source of obligation? Look at the first part: 'واجبة بالشرع لا بالعقل'.
3
Understand the disagreement
The author mentions 'خلافاً للمعتزلة' — contrary to the Mu'tazila. Research who they are if needed, but note that they held reason could determine obligations independently.
This is a classic Ash'ari-Mu'tazili debate.
4
Analyze the explanation of the phrase
The author expands: 'إن وجوب المعرفة لم يدرك إلا من الشرع'. What does this imply about the role of reason?
Reason can only confirm what revelation says; it cannot originate obligations.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'فلا حكم قبل الشرع أصلا'. Why is this significant for understanding Islamic theology?
It means no moral or religious duty exists without divine communication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجبة بالشرع لا بالعقل، خلافاً للمعتزلة. قوله : (واجبة بالشَّرع) : أي : إنَّ وجوب المعرفة لم يدرك إلَّا من الشَّرع، ولم يعلم إلا منه، فلا حكم قبل الشرع أصلاً لا أصليّاً ولا فرعياً.
It is obligatory by the revealed law, not by reason, contrary to the Mu'tazila. His saying: (obligatory by the law) means: that the obligation of knowledge is not perceived except from the law, and is not known except from it, so there is no ruling before the law at all, neither fundamental nor subsidiary.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage explains that the obligation of acquiring knowledge (al-ma'rifa) is known through revelation (shar'), not through rational deduction alone. This is a point of disagreement with the Mu'tazila, who held that reason can independently determine obligations. The author further clarifies that no legal ruling, whether fundamental (asli) or subsidiary (far'i), exists for humans prior to revelation. This emphasizes the Ash'ari position that moral obligations are known only through divine communication.

Key Terms
الشرع
العقل
المعتزلة
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reason alone can determine moral and religious obligations.
Correction: The author insists that obligations are only known through revelation; reason is insufficient without divine law.

Checkpoint

A) By reason alone
B) By the revealed law
C) By intuition
D) By consensus

Key Takeaway
Obligation is known only through revelation; reason alone cannot determine it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The author says there is no ruling before the law at all. Does this make you more or less careful about claiming something is obligatory? How can you ensure your sense of duty is rooted in revelation, not culture or personal preference?

The Ruling and Meaning of Knowing Allah
The commentator interprets 'ma'rifa' and cites Quranic verses about Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the knowledge of Allah required in the ruling refers to knowledge of His necessary existence, and how Quranic verses illustrate His attributes.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah means knowing Him as the Necessary Being, as defined by reason and revelation.

The commentator begins by clarifying the meaning of 'ma'rifa' in the context of the obligation to know Allah. He explains that it is not mere linguistic knowledge but a specific theological understanding of Allah's necessary existence. He then provides Quranic verses that describe Allah's essence, speech, actions, attributes, and self, to ground this knowledge in revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get a sense of the commentator's main points.
Pay attention to the grammatical note about 'an'.
2
Identify the definition of ma'rifa
Find where the commentator defines ma'rifa as 'your knowledge of the necessary (al-wajib)'.
This is the core theological point.
3
Examine the Quranic verses
Notice the five categories: essence (Ikhlas), speech (Be), actions (every day), attributes (First/Last/etc.), and self (nothing like Him).
Each verse addresses a different aspect.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Understand that these verses together show that Allah is known through revelation and reason, and that He is incomparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم معرفة الله تعالى
ولعدم تكليفنا بذلك، فسَّرَ المعرفة بما هو المراد فقال:
١٤٧ أن (أي: يعرف) هو وإن كان مرفوعاً لتجرُّده من ناصب وجازم إلا أن المعنى على تقدير (أن) المصدرية نحو : (تسمع بالمُعيدي خير من تراه أي معرفة الله تعالى هي معرفتك (الواجب) :
اللهُ أَحَدٌ... إلى آخر السُّورة (۱) ، وإن سألت عن أقواله؛ فقد قال : إِنَّمَا قَوْلُنَا لِشَيْءٍ إِذَا أَرَدْنَهُ أَن تَقُولَ لَهُ كُن فَيَكُونُ (٢) ، وإن سألت أفعاله؛ فقد عن قال : كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ (۳) ، وإن سألت عن نعته؛ فقد قال تعالى : الأَولُ وَالْآخِرُ وَالظَّاهِرُ وَالْبَاطِنُ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ) (٤)، وإن سألت عن ذاته، فقد قال: (لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ) (٥) .
قوله : ( ولعدم تكليفنا بذلك) : عطف علة على معلول، كذا قرَّره الشَّرح ، ولعلَّ الأظهر أنَّه عطف معلول على علَّة.
قوله : (إِلَّا أَنَّ المعنى ... إلخ): وجه ذلك أنَّ ما بعد أي التَّفسيريَّة يكون عطف بيان لما قبله، وما قبله مصدر صريح، فيجب تأويل هذا بمصدر .
قوله : (تسمع) مبتدأ وخير) خبره والمبتدأ لا يكون إلَّا اسماً
(۱) سورة الإخلاص : (۱).
(۲) سورة النمل : (٤٠). (۳) سورة الرحمن: (۲۹).
(٤) سورة الحديد: (۳).
(٥) سورة الشورى ((۱۱)
The ruling of knowing Allah the Exalted. And because we are not obligated by that, he interpreted 'knowledge' (al-ma'rifa) as what is intended, and said: '147 That (i.e., he knows) is in the nominative because it is free from any agent or particle of jussive, but the meaning is based on the estimation of the subordinating particle 'an', like: you hear about al-Mu'aydi is better than seeing him, i.e., knowledge of Allah the Exalted is your knowledge of the necessary (al-wajib). Allah is One... to the end of the surah (1). And if you ask about His words, He said: Our saying to a thing when We intend it is only that We say to it 'Be' and it is (2). And if you ask about His actions, He said: Every day He is upon some matter (3). And if you ask about His attributes, He said: He is the First and the Last, the Manifest and the Hidden, and He is of all things Knowing (4). And if you ask about His essence, He said: There is nothing like Him (5). His saying: (And because we are not obligated by that) is a conjunction of a cause to an effect, as the commentary established, but apparently it is a conjunction of an effect to a cause. His saying: (Except that the meaning... etc.) The reason is that what follows the explicative 'ay' is an appositive to what precedes it, and what precedes it is an explicit verbal noun, so this must be interpreted as a verbal noun. His saying: (You hear) is a subject and (better) is its predicate, and the subject must be a noun.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the matn (the original text) regarding the ruling of knowing Allah. The commentator begins by stating that the matn interpreted 'ma'rifa' (knowledge) in a specific sense suited to the context. The matn then gives a grammatical analysis of the word 'an' (that) in the phrase, explaining that despite being nominative, it is treated as a subordinating particle. An example is provided: 'you hear about al-Mu'aydi is better than seeing him,' which is a proverb meaning that hearing about something can be better than seeing it, but here it is applied to the concept that knowing Allah is knowing the necessary (al-wajib). Then several Quranic verses are cited to illustrate different aspects of Allah: Surah al-Ikhlas for His essence, verses about His speech, actions, attributes (First, Last, Manifest, Hidden), and the negation of likeness. The commentator then explains three expressions from the matn: First, the phrase 'and because we are not obligated by that' is analyzed as a conjunction of cause to effect, though the commentator suggests it might be the opposite, an effect to a cause. Second, the phrase 'except that the meaning' is explained: what follows the explicative 'ay' (i.e.) is an appositive (badal) that must be interpreted as a verbal noun to match the preceding explicit verbal noun. Third, the example 'you hear' is parsed as a nominal sentence with a subject and predicate, confirming that the subject must be a noun.

Key Terms
معرفة
واجب
تفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'ma'rifa' here means simple linguistic knowledge or mere acknowledgment of Allah's existence.
Correction: The passage shows that 'ma'rifa' refers to specific theological knowledge of Allah's necessary existence, supported by Quranic descriptions of His attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the intended meaning of 'ma'rifa' in the context of the ruling of knowing Allah?
A) Sensory perception
B) Knowledge of the necessary (al-wajib)
C) Mere acknowledgment of existence
D) Memorization of Quranic verses

Key Takeaway
The obligatory knowledge of Allah is the knowledge of His necessary existence, as defined by reason and revealed in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allah is One' (Surah al-Ikhlas), do you truly grasp that He is the Necessary Being (al-wajib) – that He alone exists by necessity and everything else depends on Him? How would your attitude toward your own needs change if you deeply felt this truth?
?
Which of the five aspects of Allah (essence, speech, actions, attributes, incomparability) do you most neglect in your daily thoughts and worship? What is one concrete step you can take this week to intentionally reflect on that aspect?
?
The passage teaches that indirect knowledge of Allah through revelation can be superior to direct perception. How does this challenge your reliance on your own senses and experiences? Will you commit to studying the Quran more deeply as the primary source of your knowledge of Allah?

Grammatical Analysis of Commentary Expressions
The commentator explains three grammatical points from the matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical analysis of three specific expressions from the matn regarding cause-effect relationships and appositive structures.

Core Idea
The commentator's linguistic precision reveals deeper theological meanings through grammatical analysis.

In this lesson, we examine the commentator's detailed grammatical analysis of three phrases from the original text. He discusses whether a conjunction is cause-to-effect or effect-to-cause, the nature of the explicative 'ay' and appositive, and the parsing of a nominal sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the three expressions
Read the Arabic and translation for each of the three explained phrases.
They are: (1) 'ولعدم تكليفنا بذلك', (2) 'إِلَّا أَنَّ المعنى', (3) 'تسمع'.
2
Understand the cause-effect discussion
Analyze the first expression: is the conjunction from cause to effect or effect to cause? The commentator presents both views.
3
Grasp the appositive rule
Learn that after the explicative 'ay', what follows must be an appositive (عطف بيان) that is a verbal noun to match the preceding explicit verbal noun.
4
Note the nominal sentence parsing
See how 'تسمع' is parsed as a subject (مبتدأ) and 'خير' as its predicate (خبر), confirming the subject must be a noun.

The Path to Knowing God: The Temporality of the World
Al-Sawi introduces the proof of the world's temporality and explains the method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for al-Sawi, the path to knowledge of God is through establishing the temporality of the world, and you will grasp the methodological shift from rational judgments to applied proof.

Core Idea
The temporality of the world is the essential proof for knowing God.

Having established the categories of rational judgment and the obligation to know God, al-Sawi now introduces the specific path that leads to that knowledge: proving that the world is temporally originated (hadith). This passage marks a transition from general principles to the first concrete argument in kalām. It also clarifies key terms like 'path,' 'proof,' and the nature of relational (i‘tibārī) matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the text introduce the new topic.
2
Identify the main claim
Find where al-Sawi states that the path to knowing God is the temporality of the world. This is the central thesis.
3
Understand the methodological shift
Notice that al-Sawi moves from discussing rational judgments to presenting a specific proof. This shows the applied nature of kalām.
The divisions of rational judgment were preparatory.
4
Note the glosses on key terms
Examine how al-Sawi explains 'knowledge' as assent (taṣdīq) and the 'path' as a metaphor for proof. These clarifications are essential.
The metaphor of the path is a declared simile.
5
Reflect on the role of i‘tibārī matters
Consider why al-Sawi mentions that states are relational mental considerations. This ties to the nature of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان أن العالم حادث ولما فرغ من بيان أقسام الحكم العقلي ووجوب معرفة الله تعالى على كل مكلّف؛ أخذ في بيان الطَّريق الموصل إلى معرفته تعالى وهي حدوث العالم، فقال: (ثم) بعد أن عرفت أنَّه يجب على كلِّ مكلَّف شرعاً أن يعرف ما يجب في حقه تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز (اعلَمَنْ) - بنون التوكيد الخفيفة – وضمَّن العلم معنى التصديق فعدَّاه بالباء في قوله : (بأنَّ هذا العالما) - بجميع أجزائه - قوله : (فإنَّها لا تتصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم) : أي : بل هي حال توصف بالتبوت لا بالوجود ولا بالعدم والحقُّ أنَّها أمور اعتباريَّة لا ثبوت لها في الخارج، وإنَّما هي أمور يعتبرها الذهن. قوله : (أخذ في بيان الطَّريق الموصل) : المراد به البرهان والدليل فشبه بالطريق الحسي بجامع أنَّ كلّاً يوصل للمقصود على سبيل الاستعارة التّصريحيَّة. قوله : (ثم بعد أن عرفت أي من قولنا السابق : (وواجب شرعاً على المكلف . . . إلخ). قوله : (وضمَّن العلم ... إلخ): جواب عن سؤال حاصله : أنَّ مادَّة
A chapter on explaining that the world is temporally originated. When he finished explaining the divisions of rational judgment and the obligation of knowing God Almighty upon every legally responsible person, he began explaining the path that leads to knowledge of Him Almighty, which is the temporality of the world. He said: 'Then after you have known that it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person legally to know what is necessary in His right Almighty, what is impossible, and what is possible, know (with the light nun of emphasis) (i‘laman)' – and he embedded in 'knowledge' the meaning of assent (taṣdīq) and thus made it transitive with the bā’ in his saying: 'that this world (al-‘ālima) – with all its parts.' His saying: 'for it is not described by existence or non-existence' means rather it is a state described by affirmation (thubut) not by existence or non-existence, and the truth is that they are relational matters (umūr i‘tibāriyya) with no external reality, but rather they are matters considered by the mind. His saying: 'he began explaining the path that leads' means the proof and evidence, likened to a sensory path because each leads to the intended goal, as a declared metaphor. His saying: 'then after you have known' refers to our previous statement: 'and it is legally obligatory upon the legally responsible person... etc.' His saying: 'and he embedded in knowledge... etc.' is an answer to a question whose gist is that the matter...
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage begins a new section titled 'Chapter on explaining that the world is temporally originated' (Fasl fi bayan anna al-‘alam hadith). The author states that after finishing the explanation of the divisions of rational judgment and the obligation of knowing God upon every legally responsible person, he proceeds to explain the path that leads to knowledge of God, which is the temporality of the world. He then quotes the base text: 'Then after you have known that it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person...' and notes that the verb 'i‘laman' has the light nun of emphasis. The author explains that the verb 'knowledge' is given the meaning of 'assent' (taṣdīq), making it transitive with the bā’ in the phrase 'that this world – with all its parts.' He then glosses the phrase 'for it is not described by existence or non-existence' as referring to a state described by affirmation (thubut) rather than existence or non-existence; and the truth is that such things are relational matters (umūr i‘tibāriyya) with no external reality, only mental considerations. Next, he glosses 'he began explaining the path that leads' as referring to the proof and evidence, likening it to a sensory path metaphorically because each conveys to the intended goal. Finally, he notes that 'then after you have known' refers back to an earlier statement about the legal obligation upon the responsible person, and the phrase 'and he embedded in knowledge...' is an answer to a question about the material (incomplete).

Key Terms
حدوث العالم
طريق موصل
برهان
دليل
تصديق
أمور اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God is directly intuitive without reasoning.
Correction: Al-Sawi indicates that knowledge of God comes through the proof of the world's temporality, emphasizing reasoned assent.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the 'path that leads' to knowledge of God?
A) The study of rational judgments.
B) The temporality of the world.
C) Intuitive knowledge.
D) The existence of states.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of the world is the foundational proof (dalīl) that leads to knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that knowing God requires following a rational proof—the temporality of the world. Do you personally possess such a reasoned conviction, or have you simply inherited your faith? Take ten minutes today to write down the actual evidence you rely on for your belief in God.
?
The author stresses that it is obligatory (wājib) upon every legally responsible person to know God through this path. In your daily life, how much time do you dedicate to fulfilling this obligation compared to other duties? Commit to a specific time this week to study the proof of the world's temporality until you can explain it clearly.
?
The passage distinguishes between mental constructs (umūr iʿtibāriyya) and external reality. Are there ideas you hold about God that might be mere mental assumptions rather than truths grounded in the world's creation? Are you willing to test them against the evidence of temporality, and if they fail, to change your mind?

The World as a Sign and Refutation of Sophists
Al-Sawi defines the world as a sign and affirms fixed realities against sophists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world is called a sign, how it indicates the existence of its Maker, and that the realities of things are fixed, contrary to the Sophists.

Core Idea
The world is a sign pointing to God, and its realities are fixed and knowable.

Al-Sawi begins this section by reminding us that proving the world is temporally originated is the path to knowing God. He then explains why the world is called 'a sign' and uses the demonstrative pronoun 'this' to show that the realities of things are fixed. Finally, he rebukes the Sophists who deny this fixedness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the purpose is to prove the world's temporal origination as a path to knowing God.
Focus on the phrase 'الطريق الموصل' – the path that leads.
2
Understand why the world is called a sign
Al-Sawi states that the world is named 'عالم' because it is a sign (علامة) of the existence of its Maker. Reflect on how creation points to a creator.
The word 'عالم' is derived from 'علم' but here it is linked to 'علامة'.
3
See the argument from the demonstrative pronoun
The use of 'هذا' in 'هذا العالم' indicates that the realities of things are fixed and knowledge of them is achieved. This sets up the refutation of the Sophists.
Al-Sawi explains that the demonstrative points to something present, implying objective reality.
4
Recognize the refutation of the Sophists
Al-Sawi affirms that the fixedness of realities is accepted by all religious communities except the Sophists, who are divided into three groups. Understand that this passage defends epistemological objectivity.
The text breaks off before naming the three groups, but the stance is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
في بيان أن العالم حادث
ولما فرغ من بيان أقسام الحكم العقلي ووجوب معرفة الله تعالى على كل مكلّف؛ أخذ في بيان الطَّريق الموصل إلى معرفته تعالى وهي حدوث العالم، فقال:
(ثم) بعد أن عرفت أنَّه يجب على كلِّ مكلَّف شرعاً أن يعرف ما يجب في حقه تعالى وما يستحيل وما يجوز (اعلَمَنْ) - بنون التوكيد الخفيفة – وضمَّن العلم معنى التصديق فعدَّاه بالباء في قوله : (بأنَّ هذا العالم)
بجميع أجزائه -
قوله : (فإنَّها لا تتصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم) : أي : بل هي حال توصف بالتبوت لا بالوجود ولا بالعدم والحقُّ أنَّها أمور اعتباريَّة لا ثبوت لها في الخارج، وإنَّما هي أمور يعتبرها الذهن.
قوله : (أخذ في بيان الطَّريق الموصل) : المراد به البرهان والدليل فشبه بالطريق الحسي بجامع أنَّ كلّاً يوصل للمقصود على سبيل الاستعارة التّصريحيَّة.
قوله : (ثم بعد أن عرفت أي من قولنا السابق : (وواجب شرعاً على المكلف . . . إلخ).
قوله : (وضمَّن العلم ... إلخ): جواب عن سؤال حاصله : أنَّ مادَّة
سُمِّي بذلك لأنَّه علامة؛ أي دليل، على وجود صانعه .
وفي التعبير باسم الإشارة إشارة إلى أنَّ حقائق الأشياء ثابتة، وأنَّ العلم بها متحقق، وهو كذلك عند جميع الملل إلا السوفسطائية، فقد خالفوا في ذلك، وهم فرق ثلاثة :
قوله : (سمي) : أي : العالم باعتبار مدلوله.
وقوله : (بذلك) : أي : بالعالم باعتبار داله
قوله : (وفي التَّعبير باسم الإشارة . . . إلخ): بيان ذلك أنَّ الإشارة إنَّما يشار بها إلى موجود حاضر .
قوله : (إلى أنّ حقائق الأشياء : حقيقة، وهي والماهية والمائية جمع والهويّة بمعنى واحد .
وقوله : (ثابتة :أي متحققة؛ لأنَّ الثَّابت والمتحقق والموجود بمعنى واحد، وحقيقة الشَّيء ما به الشَّيء هو هو ؛ كالحيوانيَّة والناطقيَّة بالنسبة للإنسان، فهو الأول : عائد على المتعقل في الأذهان، والثَّاني : عائد على المتعقل في الخارج ونفس الأمر .
قوله : (إِلَّا السُّوفسطائيَّة) : اسم لجماعة مخصوصة من اليونان، توغلوا في علم الرّياضة حتَّى أدَّاهم ذلك إلى الضَّلال، وهو اسم مركَّب، (فسوف) بمعنى : العلم والحكمة، (وإسطائيَّة) بمعنى : المزخرف المزيَّن فمعنى الجميع أصحاب العلم والحكمة المزخرفة المزينة، قال بعضهم:
Chapter. On explaining that the world is temporally originated. And when he finished explaining the divisions of rational judgment and the obligation of knowing God Most High upon every morally responsible person, he began explaining the path that leads to knowledge of Him Most High, which is the temporal origination of the world, and said: (Then) after you have known that it is legally obligatory upon every morally responsible person to know what is necessary with respect to God Most High, what is impossible, and what is permissible, (know) – with the light nun of emphasis – and he embedded in 'knowledge' the meaning of 'assent' and thus made it transitive with the particle 'bā'' in his saying: (that this world) – with all its parts – His saying: (For they are not qualified by existence nor by non-existence): meaning: rather they are a state described by permanence, not by existence nor non-existence, and the truth is that they are mental considerations with no fixed reality in the external, but are things the mind considers. His saying: (he began explaining the path that leads): what is meant is the proof and the evidence, so he compared it to the sensory path due to the commonality that each leads to the intended goal, on the way of explicit metaphor. His saying: (then after you have known) i.e., from our previous saying: (and it is legally obligatory upon the morally responsible person... etc.). His saying: (and he embedded in knowledge... etc.): is an answer to a question the summary of which is that the matter [of the verb] is... He was named that because he is a sign; i.e., a proof, of the existence of his Maker. And in the expression with the demonstrative pronoun there is an indication that the realities of things are fixed, and that knowledge of them is achieved, and this is the case with all religious communities except the Sophists, for they have opposed this, and they are three sects: His saying: (He was named) i.e., the world with respect to its signification. And His saying: (by that) i.e., by the world with respect to its signifier. His saying: (And in the expression with the demonstrative pronoun... etc.): An explanation of that: the demonstrative pronoun is only used to point to something present. His saying: (that the realities of things): reality, and it is [synonymous with] quiddity and essence and identity, all with one meaning. And His saying: (fixed) i.e., achieved; because the fixed, the achieved, and the existent are all one meaning. And the reality of a thing is that by which the thing is what it is; like animality and rationality with respect to the human. The first [pronoun] refers to what is intellectually apprehended in minds, and the second refers to what is intellectually apprehended in the external and in the real order. His saying: (except the Sophists): is the name of a particular group of Greeks, who delved into the science of exercise until that led them to misguidance. It is a compound name: 'soph' means knowledge and wisdom, and 'istia' means embellished and adorned. So the meaning of the whole is: the people of embellished and adorned knowledge and wisdom. Some of them said:
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on a line from the matn (Sharh al-Kharidat) introducing the section on proving the temporal origination (حدوث) of the world. The commentator, Al-Sawi, first states the purpose of the section: after explaining the divisions of rational judgment and the obligation to know God, the author proceeds to explain the path that leads to knowledge of God, which is the temporal origination of the world. He then glosses the matn's phrase 'ثم اعلمن بأن هذا العالم' (Then know that this world), noting that the verb 'اعلمن' uses the light nun of emphasis and that the verb 'علم' is given the meaning of 'assent' (تصديق), making it transitive with the preposition 'باء'. He then explains a separate phrase from the matn or earlier commentary: 'فإنها لا تتصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم' (for they are not qualified by existence nor by non-existence), clarifying that this refers to a state (حال) described by permanence (ثبوت), not existence or non-existence, and that these are mental considerations (أمور اعتبارية) without external reality. Next, he explains the metaphor of 'الطريق الموصل' (the path that leads), saying it is a simile comparing proof/evidence to a sensory path, both leading to the goal. He then refers the phrase 'ثم بعد أن عرفت' back to a previous statement about legal obligation. Following this, he begins to answer a question about the embedding of 'علم' with 'تصديق', but the text cuts off mid-sentence. The commentary then shifts to explaining why the world is called 'عالم': it is a sign (علامة) or proof of the existence of its Maker. The use of the demonstrative pronoun 'هذا' in 'هذا العالم' indicates that the realities of things (حقائق الأشياء) are fixed (ثابتة) and that knowledge of them is achieved (متحقق). This is the position of all religious communities except the Sophists (السوفسطائية), who oppose this and are divided into three groups (though the enumeration of these groups is not present in the text). Al-Sawi then provides additional clarifications: 'سمي' refers to the world by its signification, and 'بذلك' refers to the world by its signifier. He explains that the demonstrative pronoun points to something present, supporting the claim that realities are fixed. He defines 'حقيقة' as synonymous with ماهية, مائية, and هوية. He states that 'ثابتة' means 'achieved' (متحققة), and that ثابت, متحقق, and موجود are synonymous. The reality of a thing is that by which it is what it is, like animality and rationality for a human. The first pronoun (in 'به') refers to the intellectually apprehended in minds, the second to the intellectually apprehended in the external and the real order. Finally, he explains the term 'السوفسطائية': it is a name for a specific group of Greeks who delved into the science of exercise (رياضة) until they fell into misguidance. The name is compound: 'سوف' meaning wisdom, and 'سطائية' meaning embellished, so the whole means 'people of embellished wisdom'. The passage ends mid-quote with 'قال بعضهم:' (some of them said).

Key Terms
عالم
حادث
علامة
سوفسطائية
حقائق الأشياء
ثبوت
ماهية
حقيقة
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the fixedness of realities implies physical permanence or that things have no essence.
Correction: Al-Sawi explains that fixedness means epistemological objectivity: things have real essences knowable by the mind, not that they are physically unchanging.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Sawi say the world is called ‘عالم’?
A) Because it contains knowledge ('ilm).

D) Because it is temporally originated.

Key Takeaway
The world is a sign of God's existence, and the realities of things are fixed and knowable, contra the Sophists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Introduction to the Seven Objections
Al-Sawi introduces the seven objections and Ahl al-Sunnah's responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ahl al-Sunnah systematically refute seven key objections against the creation of the world, and that these form the subject of the upcoming section.

Core Idea
Seven objections, one masterful response: Ahl al-Sunnah’s methodology of refutation.

After establishing the proof of the world's creation, the text now introduces a section dedicated to answering objections. Al-Sawi (or the author of the matn) calls these the 'seven objectives' – seven doubts raised by opponents. This brief passage sets the stage, promising that Ahl al-Sunnah have answered each one with the best possible reply. Let us see what these objections are and how they are refuted.

Guided Path

2
Understand the purpose
This passage introduces a new section. The 'seven objectives' refer to seven doubts that opponents raise against the doctrine of creation. Ahl al-Sunnah's responses are described as 'the best answer'. This frames the following content as a systematic defense.

Mnemonics of the Six Objections
Al-Sawi records a poetic mnemonic summarizing six objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recall the six philosophical objections against the world's contingency using a single mnemonic poem.

Core Idea
Six objections, one poetic line: memorize the structure of the philosophers' arguments.

This passage presents a unique pedagogical tool: a mnemonic poem by Shaykh al-Amir that encodes the first six objections to the world's temporality. By memorizing the key words, you can recall the entire argumentative structure. Let's break it down.

Guided Path
1
Read the poetic line and its translation
Read the Arabic line of rajaz poetry and its literal translation. Notice the six key terms: 'Precedence of God, likewise non-existence, its gradation, its possibility, with a particularizer, effect all together.'
The word 'ṭarrā' at the end means 'all together'.
2
Identify the six key terms
List the six terms: sabaq al-ilāh, kadhā al-ʿadam, tadrījuhu, imkānuhu, maʿa mūjib, athar. These are cues for each objection.
Each term is explained individually in the passage.
3
Match each term to its explanation
For each key word, read the corresponding explanation given by al-Sawi. Connect the term to the objection it represents.
For example, 'sabaq al-ilāh' refers to the objection that if the world were temporal, God would precede it by a duration.
4
Reflect on the purpose of mnemonic verses in kalam
Consider why scholars like Shaykh al-Amir used poetry. These mnemonic devices help students memorize complex arguments and recall them during debate.
This poem does not contain refutations; it only summarizes the opponents' arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نظم تلك الشبه على هذا الترتيب أستاذنا الشيخ الأمير في بيت من الرجز: سبق الإله كذا العدم تدريجه إمكانـه مـع مـوجـب أثر طرّا فقوله : ( سبق الإله إشارة للأولى وهي قولهم : لو كان حادثاً لسبقه الإله بمدة . . . إلخ . وقوله : (كذا العدم للثانية، وهي قولهم : عدمه متقدم عليه بالزمان فيلزم قدم الزمان . وقوله : (تدريجه) للثالثة، وهي قولهم : وجوده قبل زمنه بمدة جائز، فيتدرج للعدم . وقوله : (إمكانه) للرَّابعة ؛ أعني : لو كان حادثاً؛ لكان مسبوقاً بإمكانه . وقوله : (مع موجب للخامسة، وهي لو كان حادثاً ؛ لاحتاج لما يخصه بزمنه، وهو إما قديم وإما حادث . . . إلخ . وقوله : (أثَر) إشارة لشبهة التَّأثير حال الوجود أو العدم، وهي السادسة .
And our teacher Shaykh al-Amir versified these sophisms in this order in a line of rajaz: 'Precedence of God, likewise non-existence, its gradation, its possibility, with a particularizer, effect all together.' So his saying: (Precedence of God) is an indication of the first, which is their saying: If it were temporal, God would precede it by a duration… etc. His saying: (likewise non-existence) is for the second, which is their saying: Its non-existence precedes it in time, necessitating the eternity of time. His saying: (its gradation) is for the third, which is their saying: Its existence before its time is possible, so it gradually approaches non-existence. His saying: (its possibility) is for the fourth, i.e., if it were temporal, it would be preceded by its possibility. His saying: (with a particularizer) is for the fifth, which is: if it were temporal, it would need what specifies its time, and that is either eternal or temporal… etc. His saying: (effect) is an indication of the sophism of the effect during existence or non-existence, which is the sixth.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents a mnemonic poetic line by Shaykh al-Amir that summarizes the first six philosophical objections (shubah) against the world's contingency. The poem consists of key words that serve as memory cues for each argument. The author then explains each word: 'Precedence of God' (sabaq al-ilāh) refers to the first objection: if the world is temporal, God must have preceded it by a duration, implying pre-eternal time. 'Likewise non-existence' (kadhā al-ʿadam) is the second: the world's non-existence precedes it in time, implying the eternity of time. 'Its gradation' (tadrījuhu) is the third: temporal existence can be preceded by a possible time, leading to infinite gradation toward non-existence. 'Its possibility' (imkānuhu) is the fourth: a temporal thing is preceded by its possibility, implying that possibility is eternal. 'With a particularizer' (maʿa mūjib) is the fifth: the world requires a particularizer for its specific time, leading to regress. 'Effect' (athar) is the sixth: the problem of effect during non-existence or existence. This poetic summary helps students memorize the structure of the philosophers' arguments before engaging with the Ashʿarī refutations.

Key Terms
سبق الإله (sabaq al-ilāh): precedence of God
كذا العدم (kadhā al-ʿadam): likewise non-existence
تدريج (tadrīj): gradation
إمكان (imkān): possibility
موجب (mūjib): particularizer
أثر (athar): effect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem provides the Ashʿarī refutation of the objections.
Correction: The poem only summarizes the objections as presented by the philosophers; it does not contain the refutations.

Checkpoint
Which objection does the word 'imkānuhu' (its possibility) refer to in the poem?

B) The second objection about priority of non-existence.
C) The fourth objection about being preceded by possibility.
D) The fifth objection about needing a particularizer.

Key Takeaway
The poem is a compact tool for memorizing the structure of the philosophers' arguments before engaging with Ashʿarī refutations.

The Seven Topics: Refuting Philosophers with Poetic Precision
Sanusi introduces the seven topics and presents a poetic mnemonic for refuting philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the purpose and structure of the seven topics used by scholars to refute philosophical arguments about God and creation.

Core Idea
The seven topics are a mnemonic arsenal against philosophical errors about God.

This lesson introduces the seven topics (al-maṭālib as-sab‘ah), a systematic set of arguments crafted by Islamic scholars to counter philosophical objections, particularly those concerning divine attributes and temporal creation. The passage presents a poetic summary that serves as a mnemonic device, making these complex arguments easier to memorize and deploy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The seven topics are introduced as a means to refute philosophers. Recognize that the text expresses hope that these topics will lead to salvation.
The author is confident these arguments are spiritually beneficial.
2
Identify the seventh topic
The phrase 'all' (ṭaran) refers to the seventh topic: the impossibility of change in the Creator. This is a key point.
This will be explained in detail later.
3
Memorize the poetic verse
The scholars compiled the seven topics into a concise rajaz verse. Read the verse and note each word corresponds to an argument.
The verse: 'Zīd, mā qām, mā intaqal, mā kamanā, mā infakka, lā 'adamu qadīm, lāḥanā.'
4
Connect to the lessons ahead
Realize that each word in the verse will be explained in subsequent lessons. This sets the stage for deeper study.

المطالب السبعة
[مطلب في المطالب السبعة]
وقوله : (طراً) للسَّابعة، وهي لزوم التَّغيُّر في الصانع بطرو كونه صانعاً، فدونك مقاصد سبعة نرجو من فضل الله أن يسدَّ بها أبواب النيران ويدخلنا بها الجنان .
وذكر العلماء مطالب سبعة قصدوا بها الرَّدَّ على الفلاسفة أيضاً، جمعها بعضهم في قوله : من الرجز]

The seven topics. [Section on the seven topics] And his saying: (all) for the seventh, which is the necessity of change in the Maker due to the occurrence of His being a Maker. So here are seven objectives, which we hope by Allah's grace that He will close thereby the doors of Hellfire and admit us thereby into Paradise. And the scholars mentioned seven topics intending thereby to refute the philosophers as well; some of them collected them in his saying, in rajaz meter: 'Zīd, mā qām, mā intaqal, mā kamanā, mā infakka, lā 'adamu qadīm, lāḥanā.'

Commentary
This passage provides an introduction to the seven topics (al-maṭālib as-sab‘ah) that will be used to refute philosophical objections regarding the attributes of God and the creation of the world. The author first explains that the phrase 'all' (ṭaran) refers to the seventh topic, which concerns the impossibility of change in the Creator. He then expresses hope that these seven topics will be a means of salvation. Next, he notes that scholars have compiled these topics into a concise poetic line in rajaz meter, which serves as a mnemonic for the seven arguments. The verse lists: 'Zīd, mā qām, mā intaqal, mā kamanā, mā infakka, lā 'adamu qadīm, lāḥanā.' Each word or phrase corresponds to one of the seven arguments that will be explained in the following lessons.

The Obligation to Know God's Attributes and the Path to Knowledge
Sawi states the obligation and the path of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that every accountable person must know the three categories of necessary, impossible, and possible for God, and that the path to this knowledge is through recognizing the temporality of the world.

Core Idea
The first duty is to know three rational categories and the proof from the world's temporality.

This passage from Sawi's hashia establishes the very first duty of the mukallaf: to learn what is necessary, impossible, and possible for God. It then reveals the key that unlocks this knowledge — the argument from the temporality of the world. As you read, notice how Sawi grounds theological method in universal rational principles.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the three categories
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the three categories that every mukallaf must know: necessary, impossible, and possible for God.
These categories are the foundation of all theological discussion.
2
Understand the analogy of body and motion
Sawi uses an analogy: a body cannot be free from motion or rest, otherwise contradictories would coexist. Reflect on how this illustrates the necessity of attributes.
This analogy shows that some things are inherently necessary.
3
Recognize the path to knowledge
The hashia explains that 'the path leading to knowledge' is the temporality of the world. Understand why this argument is central to proving God's existence and attributes.
The world's temporality implies a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الصفات الواجبة لله تعالى أولاً : الوجود إذا علمت أنَّه يجب على كلِّ مكلَّف أن يعرف ما يجب وما يستحيل وما يجوز الله تعالى وعلمت الطَّريق الموصل إلى المعرفة (فاعلم بأنَّ الوصف) ضروري؛ لأنَّه لا يعقل كون الجرم منفكّاً عن كونه متحركاً أو ساكناً، مثلاً ، إذ لو انفكَ عن الحركة و السُّكون ؛ لزم ارتفاع النقيضين وهما حركة ولا حركة وسكون ولا سكون. قوله : (أن يعرف ما يجب . . . إلخ) أي: لتوقف الفنّ عليها . . . قوله : (وعلمت الطريق الموصل) : أي : وهو حدوث العالم. قوله : ( فاعلم : عبّر بالعلم ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّه لا يكتفي في هذا الفن بغيره والعلم هو الجزم المطابق للحقِّ عن موجب، عن موجب، والخطاب للمكلَّف،
The exposition of the attributes necessary for God Most High: First, Existence. If you know that it is incumbent upon every accountable person to know what is necessary, what is impossible, and what is possible for God Most High, and you have known the path leading to knowledge, (then know that the attribute) is necessary; because it is inconceivable that a body be free from being moving or resting, for example, since if it were free from motion and rest, the raising of the two contradictories would be necessary, i.e., motion and not-motion, rest and not-rest. His saying: 'that he know what is necessary . . . etc.' i.e., because the art depends on them. His saying: 'and you have known the path leading' i.e., it is the temporality of the world. His saying: 'so know' – he expressed it with knowledge, indicating that nothing other than it suffices in this art, and knowledge is the decisive judgment corresponding to truth from a cause, from a cause, and the address is to the accountable person.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a definition and commentary on the first necessary attribute of God: Existence. It begins by stating the obligation for every morally responsible person (mukallaf) to know what is necessary, impossible, and possible for God. Then it mentions the path that leads to this knowledge, which is the temporality of the world (huduth al-‘alam). The commentator then explains that the attribute of existence is necessary, using an analogy: a body cannot be free from motion or rest, otherwise contradictories would coexist; similarly, God must have existence. Then the hashia comments on specific phrases in the matn. First, 'that he know what is necessary etc.' means that the entire art of kalam depends on knowing these categories. Second, 'the path leading' refers to the argument from the temporality of the world (that the world is originated, thus needs a Creator). Third, the word 'fa'lam' (so know) is used to emphasize that mere opinion or conjecture is not sufficient; only certain knowledge (al-‘ilm) qualifies, which is defined as a decisive judgment that corresponds to reality and arises from a cause. The address is directed to the accountable person.

Key Terms
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
الطريق الموصل
حدوث العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think knowing God is optional or that only existence matters without detailed attributes.
Correction: The passage explicitly states the duty to know what is necessary, impossible, and possible for God.

Checkpoint
According to Sawi's hashia, what is 'the path leading to knowledge'?
A) The senses
B) Rational arguments
C) The temporality of the world
D) Divine revelation

Key Takeaway
The first step in theology is to know the three categories and to prove God's existence via the temporality of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you sincerely say that you possess the rational proof from the temporality of the world that establishes God's necessary existence? If not, will you commit to learning it this week?
?
When you look at the world—mountains, stars, your own body—do you see the evidence of their beginning, the mark of temporality that points to the Creator? Or have you become numb to the obvious?
?
Are you among those who merely inherit belief in God without ever personally grasping the intellectual obligation to know the necessary, impossible, and possible regarding Him? What will you do to change that starting today?

The Definition of Knowledge as Certain Judgment
Sawi defines knowledge and emphasizes its necessity in this art.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of knowledge (al-ilm) as a decisive judgment that corresponds to truth and arises from a cause, and why this definition is crucial for kalam.

Core Idea
True knowledge is certain, matches reality, and is grounded in evidence.

In this short but weighty passage, Sawi defines 'ilm' itself — the very currency of theological discourse. He insists that mere opinion is insufficient; only certain knowledge that corresponds to truth and is caused by evidence qualifies. This definition sets the bar for all theological claims.

Reflect on why knowledge must arise from a cause (evidence or proof). This distinguishes knowledge from blind faith or conjecture.
In kalam, valid knowledge is always justified.

Springs of Wisdom and Spiritual Proximity to God
Al-Sawi defines three terms: springs of wisdom, with your Lord, and something of that.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi interprets 'springs of wisdom' as divine secrets and 'with your Lord' as spiritual proximity rather than physical location.

Core Idea
Wisdom springs from divine secrets, and nearness to God is spiritual rank, not place.

In this passage, Al-Sawi explains three key phrases from the text. He shows that 'springs of wisdom' refer to the divine secrets and cognitions that appear in the heart. He then clarifies that 'with your Lord' means presence of station, not spatial location. Finally, he connects these fine points of wisdom to worship and thankfulness, leading to the gushing of these springs and nearness to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let Al-Sawi's concise style come through.
The passage has three distinct phrases being explained.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Notice how Al-Sawi redefines 'springs of wisdom', 'with your Lord', and 'something of that'. Each has a specific spiritual meaning.
Pay attention to the contrast between literal place and spiritual station.
3
Reflect on the connection to worship
The fine points of wisdom lead to worship and thankfulness, and the result is the gushing of wisdom and nearness to God. Ask yourself how this fits into the broader lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ينابيع الحكمة) : الإضافة بيانيَّة والمعنى : فيترتب على ذلك ظهور الحكمة في قلبك والمراد بها : الأسرار والمعارف.
قوله : (عند ربك) : المراد عندية مكانة لا مكان؛ وهي القرب المعنوي.
قوله : (شيئاً من ذلك : أي من دقائق الحكم الموصلة إلى العبادة، : والشكر المترتب على ذلك تفجر ينابيع الحكمة والقرب من الله تعالى.
His saying: (springs of wisdom): the idafa is explanatory, and the meaning: so results from that the appearance of wisdom in your heart, and by it is meant: the secrets and cognitions. His saying: (with your Lord) meaning presence of station, not place; it is spiritual proximity. His saying: (something of that) meaning from the subtleties of wisdom that lead to worship and thankfulness, and resulting from that is the gushing of springs of wisdom and nearness to God the Exalted.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage explains three terms. First, 'springs of wisdom' (yanabi' al-hikma) is interpreted as an explanatory idafa: the springing forth of wisdom, which is the divine secrets and cognitions (asrar wa ma'arif). Second, 'with your Lord' ('inda rabbik) is interpreted as a presence of rank (makana), not place, meaning spiritual proximity (qurb ma'nawi). Third, 'something of that' (shay'an min dhalik) refers to the fine points of wisdom that lead to worship and thankfulness, and the result is the gushing of these springs and nearness to God. This reinforces the previous lesson's progression.

Correction: Al-Sawi explicitly states it is presence of station (makana), not place, and calls it spiritual proximity (qurb ma'nawi).

Key Takeaway

Knowing God Through Proof and Experience
Hashiya defines terms for cosmic contemplation and experiential knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator distinguishes between rational proof and experiential knowledge (dhawq and 'iyan) as two valid paths to knowing God's generosity, and how he interprets cosmological terms.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God comes both through proof and through direct spiritual taste.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks key phrases from the original text that describe the believer's contemplation of the heavens and the earth. He explains that such contemplation leads to wondrous spiritual secrets in the heart. Ultimately, it yields knowledge that God is the Generous Bestower—a knowledge attainable either through rational proof or through direct experiential taste and vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the commentator's style of glossing specific phrases.
The passage is a series of short glosses on words from the original.
2
Identify the glossed terms
Pick out each phrase being glossed: 'sky', 'earth', 'would lead you', 'wondrous wonder', 'know that He is the Generous Bestower'. Note the commentator's definitions.
Pay attention to the terms 'upper world' and 'lower world'.
3
Focus on the two modes of knowledge
The final gloss mentions two ways of knowing God's generosity: by proof (al-dalil) or by taste and direct vision (al-dhawq wa al-'iyan). Reflect on why both are given.
This shows that theological knowledge includes both intellectual and experiential dimensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثم إذا نظرت إلى السَّماء . . . إلخ) : المراد العالم العلوي . وقوله : (وإلى الأرض ... إلخ) : المراد : العالم السفلي . قوله : لأفضى بك) : أي : لأداك ووصلك . قوله : (إلى العجب العجاب أي من المعارف والأسرار التي تحلُّ في القلوب وتنوّرها . قوله : (وعلمت أنَّه المحسن الوهّاب أي : إما بالدليل، أو الذوق والعيان .

Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a series of glosses on phrases from the original text that describe contemplating the creation and its effect. The commentator identifies 'sky' as the upper world (world of celestial spheres) and 'earth' as the lower world (the terrestrial realm). Then he explains that the phrase 'لأفضى بك' means to bring or lead one to something. The phrase 'إلى العجب العجاب' is interpreted as the spiritual knowledge and secrets that settle in and illuminate the hearts. Finally, 'وعلمت أنَّه المحسن الوهّاب' is glossed as two possible modes of knowledge: rational proof (الدليل) or direct experiential taste and vision (الذوق والعيان). This shows that for the commentator, knowledge of God's generosity can be acquired both through intellectual reasoning and through spiritual experience.

A Supplication for Divine Love and Its Benefits
Hashiya glosses each petition of a supplication, explaining its meaning and spiritual benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and spiritual significance of each petition in this supplication for divine favor and love.

Core Idea
Love for Allah is the head of eternal happiness.

This passage presents a commentary on a supplication attributed to the Shaykh. Each phrase of the supplication is glossed to reveal its deeper meaning and the spiritual benefit it seeks. The commentary emphasizes that love for Allah is the foundation of eternal bliss, and the other petitions are connected to that love.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how each phrase of the supplication is followed by a brief gloss in the commentary.
The commentary uses 'قوله : ...' to mark each petition.
2
Identify each petition and its gloss
List the six petitions mentioned: tawfiq, acceptance, detachment, love, pleasure of union, and forgiveness. For each, note the commentator's explanation.
3
Focus on the key term 'tawfiq'
The commentary mentions that 'tawfiq' (divine enabling) has been explained earlier. Reflect on its importance as the first petition.
Tawfiq is the initial request that enables all spiritual progress.
4
Understand the central role of love
The commentary states that love is 'the head of eternal happiness.' Consider why love is given such prominence and how the other petitions relate to it.
The pleasure of union and forgiveness are presented as consequences of love.
5
Reflect on the final petition and the quote
The last petition asks for help when slipping, and the commentary links it to forgiveness, citing Abu al-Hasan al-Shadhili. Think about the connection between being a beloved lover and being forgiven.
This shows that love entails mercy and forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (اللَّهم ; وفقنا ) : دعاء من الشَّيخ له وللمسلمين، وتقدم معنى التوفيق(١) قوله : لما فيه رضاك) : أي : قبولك لنا وإثابتك إيَّانا . قوله : (واقطعنا عن كل شيء سواك) : أي : فلا تجعل قلوبنا متعلقة به ولا ملتفتة إليه . قوله: (واملأ قلوبنا بحبك ... إلخ) : طلب المحبَّة; لأنَّها رأس السعادة الأبدية. قوله : (وأذقنا لذة الوصل ) : أي : المترتبة على المحبَّة. قوله: (وخذ بأيدينا إن زللنا :أي: لأنَّ المحبَّ المحبوب مغفور الذَّنب، قال أبو الحسن الشاذلي : واجعل سيئاتنا سيئات من أحببت).
His saying: (O Allah, grant us success) is a supplication from the Shaykh for himself and the Muslims, and the meaning of tawfiq (divine enabling) has preceded (1). His saying: (for what pleases You) means: Your acceptance of us and Your rewarding us. His saying: (and cut us off from everything other than You) means: do not make our hearts attached to it nor turned towards it. Commentary
In this passage, the commentator explains each phrase of a supplication attributed to the Shaykh (likely the author of the original text). First, 'اللهم وفقنا' is identified as a prayer for tawfiq (divine facilitation), with reference to a prior explanation. Then 'لما فيه رضاك' is glossed as God's acceptance and reward. 'واقطعنا عن كل شيء سواك' means detachment of the heart from anything other than God. 'واملأ قلوبنا بحبك' is a request for love, which is described as the head (i.e., the chief component) of eternal happiness. 'وأذقنا لذة الوصل' refers to the pleasure that comes from that love. Finally, 'وخذ بأيدينا إن زللنا' is interpreted as a plea for forgiveness, citing a saying of Abu al-Hasan al-Shadhili that the beloved lover's sins are forgiven. The commentary thus connects each petition to a spiritual benefit, emphasizing love as the core of salvation.

Why Explicit Negative Attributes?
Sanusi answers an objection about sufficiency of necessary existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the scholars of kalam explicitly list negative attributes despite their being implied by the concept of necessary existence.

Core Idea
The gravity of tawhid demands explicit teaching, not just implication.

You might wonder why kalam texts enumerate negative attributes like pre-eternity and dissimilarity when the single concept 'necessary existence' might cover them. Sanusi anticipates this objection and answers with the life-or-death importance of tawhid. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the objection and the answer.
The objection is introduced with 'In qulta'.
2
Identify the objection and answer
What is the claim? That wajib al-wujud suffices. How does Sanusi answer? By noting the importance of tawhid and the method of kalam scholars.
Notice the phrase 'lam yaktifū bi-dalālat al-iltizām'.
3
Reflect on the pedagogical principle
Why is explicit teaching preferred over implication? Consider the high stakes of tawhid for salvation.
This shows that in matters of creed, clarity overrides brevity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت : إنَّ وجوب الوجود يغني عن القدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث .
أجيب بأنَّه لمَّا كان التَّوحيد أهم الأمور المطلوبة من الشخص، إذ به ينجو من دار البوار ؛ وضَّح علماء الكلام المقام، ولم يكتفوا بدلالة الالتزام.
If you say: Necessity of existence dispenses with pre-eternity, everlastingness, and dissimilarity to originated things. The answer is that since tawhid (divine unity) is the most important of matters required from a person, for by it he escapes the abode of perdition, the scholars of kalam clarified the topic and were not content with connotative indication.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector claims that the concept of wajib al-wujud (necessary existence) alone should suffice, making the three negative attributes (pre-eternity, everlastingness, dissimilarity to created things) redundant. The answer is that because tawhid (divine unity) is the most crucial matter for salvation—by it one escapes eternal perdition—the scholars of kalam have explained the matter in detail and did not rely merely on connotative implication (dalalat al-iltizam). In other words, the attributes are explicitly delineated for clarity and emphasis, even if implicit in the concept of necessary existence.

Key Terms
وجوب الوجود (wujūb al-wujūd)
التوحيد (al-tawḥīd)
دلالة الالتزام (dalālat al-iltizām)
دار البوار (dār al-bawār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negative attributes are redundant because they are implied by necessary existence.
Correction: They are explicitly taught because tawhid's importance demands clarity, not mere implication.

Checkpoint
Why did the scholars of kalam not rely solely on the connotative indication of wajib al-wujud?
A) Because it is too difficult to understand
B) Because tawhid is so important that explicit clarification is needed
C) Because the attributes are not actually implied
D) Because they wanted to increase the number of attributes

Key Takeaway
The explicit mention of negative attributes is necessary because tawhid is the key to salvation, and the scholars leave nothing to mere implication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you satisfied with a vague, implicit belief in God's attributes, or have you sought explicit knowledge as the scholars did? What is at stake if you remain content with mere implication?
?
If tawhid is the most important matter for escaping the abode of perdition, how much time and effort have you truly dedicated to understanding it correctly? Does your effort match the gravity?
?
The scholars did not leave tawhid to implication. Examine your own heart: do you take your beliefs for granted, or do you actively seek to clarify and deepen them, knowing your salvation depends on it?

Definition and Linguistic Root of Taqwa
Al-Sawi defines taqwa and cites al-Razi on its linguistic origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dual definition of taqwa—both as compliance with divine commands and as a protective shield against sin—as explained by al-Sawi and al-Razi.

Core Idea
Taqwa means actively shielding yourself from sin through resolve and knowledge.

Al-Sawi opens his commentary by defining taqwa, the central virtue of Islamic piety. He first gives the religious definition, then traces the word back to its linguistic root, quoting Imam al-Razi. This prepares the reader to appreciate the full depth of the term before moving to further nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the religious definition
Notice that taqwa is defined as 'compliance with commands by action and prohibitions by abandonment.' This is the shar'i meaning that combines belief and action.
This distinguishes taqwa from mere intellectual assent.
2
Explore the linguistic metaphor
Al-Razi explains that the root 'w-q-y' means taking protection, like a shield. Taqwa is a spiritual shield between a person and sin, built on strong resolve and awareness of sin's ugliness.
The shield image reinforces the idea of active defense.
3
Connect the two definitions
The linguistic meaning supports the shar'i definition: fulfilling commands and avoiding prohibitions is the practical way to erect that protective barrier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تِلتَ) أي : أدركت (التَّقى) أي: التقوى، وهي امتثال المأمورات فعلاً والمَنهيَّاتِ تركاً . قال الإمام الرازي : التَّقى والتَّقوى واحد، وهما لغة: بمعنى اتخاذ وهو الوقاية؛ أي: ما يقي الشَّخص ؛ يعني : يحفظه ويحول بينه وبين ما يخافه؛ مثل الترس ونحوه في الأجسام، فكأنَّ المعنى: جعل بينه وبين المعاصي وقاية تحول بينه وبينها، من قوَّة عَزْمِه على تَرْكِها واستحضار علمه بقُبحها، نقله الشيخ عبد السلام اللقاني في شرح الجزائرية» (۱). وهذه الجملة إنشائية في المعنى، قصد بها الدُّعاءَ قوله : (فكأن المعنى ... إلخ) : التفت الشيخ إلى أنَّ المراد بالتَّقوى: الخوف من الله تعالى، الناشئ عنه عدم صدور ما يغضب الله تعالى. قوله : ( إنشائية في المعنى) : أي : خبريَّة في اللفظ. (۱) فتح المجيد بكفاية المريد (ق/ ۱۳۱)،
('You attained') i.e., you perceived, (al-tuqā) i.e., al-taqwā, which is compliance with commands by action and prohibitions by abandonment. Imam al-Rāzī said: al-tuqā and al-taqwā are one; linguistically they mean taking protection, that is, what protects a person, i.e., it preserves him and interposes between him and what he fears, like a shield and the like in physical objects. So it is as if the meaning is: placing between himself and sins a protection that interposes between him and them, from the strength of his resolve to abandon them and his bringing to mind his knowledge of their ugliness. This was transmitted by Shaykh 'Abd al-Salām al-Laqānī in his commentary on al-Jazā'iriyya. (1) This sentence is performative (inshā'iyya) in meaning, intended as supplication. His statement: 'as if the meaning... etc.' The Shaykh turned to the fact that what is meant by taqwā is fear of God Almighty, from which arises not committing what angers God. His statement: 'performative in meaning' means it is declarative in wording.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage explains the term taqwā (often translated as piety or God-consciousness). It first gives the shar'ī definition: fulfilling the commands and abstaining from the prohibitions. Then it elaborates on the linguistic origin, quoting Imam al-Rāzī. Linguistically, taqwā comes from the root w-q-y, meaning to guard or protect. It is like a shield that a person uses to protect themselves from harm. So metaphorically, taqwā means setting up a barrier between oneself and sin, through strong resolve and knowledge of sin's evil consequences. The passage then notes that the phrase 'you attained taqwā' is actually a supplication (du'ā') in meaning, though it is phrased as a declarative sentence. Finally, it refines the meaning: taqwā is not just literal shielding but the fear of God that prevents sin. This nuanced explanation is typical in kalam works that draw on both linguistic and religious definitions.

Key Terms
التقوى
التقى
الوقاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think taqwa is merely fear or piety without active effort.
Correction: The passage shows taqwa is active: 'compliance with commands by action' and 'placing a barrier' through resolve and knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi, what is the linguistic meaning of taqwa?
A) Fear
B) Obedience
C) Taking protection
D) Piety

Key Takeaway
Taqwa is both compliance with divine law and an active spiritual shield against sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What sin have you been tolerating because you haven't brought to mind its true ugliness? What is one concrete step you will take today to strengthen your resolve against it?
?
The shield of taqwa is built from 'knowledge of the ugliness' of sin and 'strength of resolve to abandon it'. Which of these two is weaker in your life right now? What will you do to fortify it starting tomorrow morning?
?
The passage says taqwa interposes between you and sins like a shield. If you were to honestly assess your current shield, where are the holes? Write down one specific temptation you face regularly and describe what it would look like to use both knowledge and resolve to block it.

The Performative Meaning and True Fear of God
Al-Sawi explains the performative nature of the supplication and refines taqwa as fear of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the phrase 'you attained taqwa' is a supplication in meaning, and how taqwa ultimately refers to a fear of God that prevents sin.

Core Idea
Taqwa is not just a shield; it is the reverential fear that makes the shield effective.

Having defined taqwa, al-Sawi now clarifies a subtle point about the sentence expressing it. He explains that the declarative phrase 'you attained taqwa' is actually a prayer, and then refines the meaning of taqwa by linking it directly to reverential fear of God that prevents wrongdoing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the performative aspect
Notice that al-Sawi says the sentence is 'performative in meaning' (inshā'iyya) but 'declarative in wording' (khabariyya). This means the statement 'you attained taqwa' is actually a supplication for the person to achieve it.
This is a subtle linguistic point common in Arabic rhetoric.
2
Understand the final refinement
Al-Sawi clarifies that the essence of taqwa is fear of Allah, which naturally leads to avoiding what displeases Him. This fear is the source of the protective shield mentioned earlier.
It connects back to the linguistic metaphor of protection.
